The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
The 13th to 16th Mixed Syllogisms (Iqtiranat)
Enumerates four types of mixed syllogisms with categorical and conditional premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the conclusion of any of the four mixed syllogisms (13th-16th conjunctions) given a categorical and a conditional premise.

Core Idea
Mixed syllogisms fuse a categorical fact with a conditional rule to yield a conditional conclusion.

This lesson walks through the four forms of mixed syllogisms that combine a categorical premise with a conditional premise. In Islamic logic, these are called iqtiranat (conjunctions) 13–16, and they are essential tools for deriving conditional judgments from facts and rules. You will learn how to form the antecedent and consequent of the conclusion based on the structure of the premises.

Guided Path
1
Identify the categorical premise
Note its subject (S), predicate (M), quantity (particular/universal), and quality (affirmative/negative). In these four conjunctions, the categorical is always particular affirmative.
For example: 'Some B is J' (بعض ب ج).
2
Identify the conditional premise
Note its antecedent (muqaddam) and consequent (tali), and whether the antecedent is affirmative or negative. Also check the quality of the conditional itself (affirmative 'قد يكون' or negative 'ليس كل').
For example: 'It may be that if some J is A then D is R' (قد يكون إذا كان بعض ج أ فـ د ر).
3
Determine the conjunction number
Based on the categorical's quality/quantity (always particular affirmative here) and the conditional's quality and antecedent's quality, match to one of the four types: 13th (affirmative conditional, affirmative antecedent), 14th (affirmative conditional, negative antecedent), 15th (negative conditional, affirmative antecedent), 16th (negative conditional, negative antecedent).
The text provides the pattern for each.
4
Construct the conclusion's antecedent
Form a new proposition by taking the subject of the categorical (e.g., B) and the predicate of the conditional's antecedent (e.g., A). Use the same quantity (particular) and quality (affirmative or negative) as the conditional's antecedent.
E.g., if the antecedent is 'some J is A', the new antecedent becomes 'some B is A'.
5
Attach the conclusion's consequent
The conclusion's consequent is exactly the same as the conditional's consequent (e.g., D is R). The conclusion is a conditional with the same modality as the conditional premise (affirmative 'قد يكون' or negative 'ليس كل').
The conclusion for the 13th: 'It may be that if some B is A then D is R'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاقتران الثالث عشر من جزئيتين موجبتين، والمقدم في الشرطية جزئي موجب، ومثاله قولنا: (بعض "ب" "ج") و(قد يكون إذا كان بعض "ج" "أ" فـ "د" "ر") ونتيجته جزئية موجبة مقدمها جزئي موجب تأليفه من موضوع الحملية ومحمول مقدم الشرطية، وتاليها تالي الشرطية، وهي قد يكون إذا كان بعض "ب" "أ" فـ "د" "ر" وبيانه كما تقدم. الاقتران الرابع عشر من جزئيتين موجبتين، والمقدم في الشرطية جزئي سالب ومثاله قولنا: (بعض "ب" "ج") و(قد يكون إذا كان بعض "ج" ليس "أ" فـ "د" "ر") ونتيجته جزئية متصلة، مقدمها جزئي سالب تأليفه من موضوع الحملية ومحمول مقدم الشرطية، وتاليها تالي الشرطية، وهي قد يكون إذا كان بعض "ب" ليس "أ" ف "د" "ر". الاقتران الخامس عشر من جزئية موجبة حَمْلية وسالبة شرطية، مقدمها جزئي موجب، ومثاله قولنا: (بعض "ب" "ج") وليس كل ما كان بعض "ج" "أ" فـ "د" "ر" ونتيجته جزئية سالبة متصلة، مقدمها جزئي موجب تأليفه من موضوع الحملية ومحمول مقدم الشرطية، وتاليها تالي الشرطية وهي ليس كل ما كان بعض "ب" "أ" فـ "د" "ر" والبيان كما تقدم. الاقتران السادس عشر من جزئية موجبة حَمْلية وجزئية سالبة شرطية، مقدمها جزئي سالب ومثاله قولنا: بعض "ب" "ج") و(ليس كل ما كان بعض "ج" ليس "أ" فـ "د" " ") ونتيجته جزئية سالبة متصلة، مقدمها جزئي سالب تأليفه من موضوع "ر" الحَمْلية ومحمول مقدم الشرطية، وتاليها تالي الشرطية وهي ليس كل ما كان بعض "ب" ليس "أ" فـ "د" "ر" والبيان كما تقدم.
The thirteenth conjunction: from two particular affirmative [premises], and the antecedent in the conditional is particular affirmative. Its example is our saying: (Some B is J) and (It may be that if some J is A then D is R). Its conclusion is a particular affirmative conditional, its antecedent is a particular affirmative composed of the subject of the categorical and the predicate of the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the consequent of the conditional. It is: It may be that if some B is A then D is R. Its demonstration is as before. The fourteenth conjunction: from two particular affirmative [premises], and the antecedent in the conditional is particular negative. Its example: (Some B is J) and (It may be that if some J is not A then D is R). Its conclusion is a particular negative conditional, its antecedent is a particular negative composed of the subject of the categorical and the predicate of the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the consequent of the conditional. It is: It may be that if some B is not A then D is R. The fifteenth conjunction: from a particular affirmative categorical and a negative conditional, its antecedent is particular affirmative. Its example: (Some B is J) and (Not every thing such that some J is A then D is R). Its conclusion is a particular negative conditional, its antecedent is a particular affirmative composed of the subject of the categorical and the predicate of the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the consequent of the conditional. It is: Not every thing such that some B is A then D is R. Its demonstration is as before. The sixteenth conjunction: from a particular affirmative categorical and a particular negative conditional, its antecedent is particular negative. Its example: (Some B is J) and (Not every thing such that some J is not A then D is R). Its conclusion is a particular negative conditional, its antecedent is a particular negative composed of the subject of the categorical and the predicate of the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the consequent of the conditional. It is: Not every thing such that some B is not A then D is R. Its demonstration is as before.
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage enumerates four types of mixed syllogisms (اقترانات) that combine a categorical premise (حملیة) with a conditional premise (شرطیة). Each type is defined by the quantity and quality of the premises, and the form of the resulting conditional conclusion. The thirteenth and fourteenth conjunctions both have two particular affirmative categorical premises, but the conditional premise's antecedent is affirmative (13th) or negative (14th). The fifteenth and sixteenth conjunctions mix a particular affirmative categorical with a negative conditional, with the conditional antecedent being affirmative (15th) or negative (16th). In each case, the conclusion is a conditional whose antecedent is formed by taking the subject of the categorical and the predicate of the conditional's antecedent, and whose consequent is the conditional's consequent. The demonstration (بیان) is said to be as before, referring to earlier proofs. The logical variables (أ, ب, ج, د) are placeholders for terms; in Islamic logic, such forms apply to any valid terms, e.g., 'Some intoxicant is wine' and 'If some wine is fermented then it is forbidden'.

Key Terms
اقتران
حملیة
شرطیة
مقدم
تالي

Checkpoint
In the 16th conjunction, what are the quantity and quality of the categorical premise and the conditional's antecedent?
A) Particular affirmative categorical, particular negative antecedent
B) Particular affirmative categorical, particular affirmative antecedent
C) Universal affirmative categorical, particular negative antecedent
D) Particular negative categorical, particular affirmative antecedent

Key Takeaway
Mixed syllogisms produce conditional conclusions by fusing a categorical fact with a conditional rule.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given: categorical premise 'Some khamr is mustawlid' (some wine is fermented) and conditional premise 'If some mustawlid is khamr then it is najis' (if some fermented drink is wine then it is impure). Construct the conclusion for the 13th conjunction.
?
Identify which conjunction (13-16) is used: 'Some nabidh is khamr. Not every thing such that some khamr is intoxicating then it is haram. Therefore, not every thing such that some nabidh is intoxicating then it is haram.'

Conditions for Valid Inference in the Second Figure of Mixed Syllogisms
Explains conditions for valid inference in the second figure of mixed syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply the three conditions (true, particular, matching quality) for the antecedent in a second‑figure mixed syllogism to determine its validity.

Core Idea
In second‑figure mixed syllogisms, the antecedent must be true, particular, and agree with the categorical in quality.

Mixed syllogisms combine a conditional and a categorical premise. The second figure places the common term as predicate in both. The logician lays down precise conditions for the antecedent to ensure the consequent follows. Mastering these conditions allows you to assess arguments in kalām and uṣūl al‑fiqh that use this form.

Guided Path
1
Identify the second‑figure structure
In a mixed syllogism, the second figure requires that the common term (الحد المشترك) is the predicate in both the categorical premise and the antecedent of the conditional. Locate this term.
2
State the three conditions
According to the passage, the antecedent must: (1) be true in itself (حَقًّا فِي نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ), (2) be particular (جُزْئِيًّا), and (3) agree with the categorical in quality (مُوَافِقًا لِلْحَمْلِيَّةِ فِي الْكَيْفِ).
3
Examine universal affirmative antecedents
When the antecedent is universal affirmative, the condition of particularity fails. The passage shows that even if the categorical is affirmative, the implication may not hold because the middle term can be more general than the minor.
4
Examine universal negative antecedents
When the antecedent is universal negative, the condition of particularity also fails, but if the categorical is affirmative, there is no impossibility; if the categorical is negative, the condition fails.

وأما إذا كان التأليف على نمط الشكل الثاني وهو أن يكون الحد المشترك بين الحَمْلية ومقدم الشرطية محمولاً فيهما، وله في الإنتاج شروط وخواص، أمـا شروطه فإن يكون المقدم حقاً في نفس الأمر، وأن يكون جزئياً، وأن يكون موافقاً للحَمْلية في الكيف؛ لأن يلزم التالي عند حذف الحد الأوسط، ونسب الأكبر إلى الأصغر، وإلا فعلى تقدير أن يكون المقدم كلياً واللزوم غير متحقق على أي حال من الكم والكيف، أما إذا كان كلياً موجباً كقولنا: (دائماً كل ما كان كل "أ" "ج" ف "د" "ر") فالشرط هو أن يكون (كل "أ" "ج") فالحَمْلية إما موجبة أو سالبة، فإن كانت موجبة فإما كلية كقولنا: (كل "ب" "ج") وإما جزئية كقولنا: (بعض "ب" "ج") فغاية ما يلزم من مثل هذا الاقتران نسبة "أ" إلى بعض "ب" أو إلى كل "ب" وإن كان "ج" لازمه فليس يلزم أن يكون كل (أ)" "ج")؛ لجواز أن يكون "أ" أعم من "ب" وإذ ذاك فلا يتحقق شرط اللزوم. وأما إذا كانت الحَمْلية سالبة، وسواء كان الحد الأوسط فيها مسلوباً عن كل الموضوع فيها أو عن بعضه، فلا يخفى أن لزوم التالي غير واجب من نسبة الأكبر إلى الأصغر. أما إذا كان بالإيجاب فلما فيه من امتناع تحقق الشرط؛ إذ شرط تحقق التالي أن يكون الحد الأكبر متصفاً بالأوسط وهو مستحيل عند اتصاف بعضه بالأصغر، واستحالة اتصاف الأصغر بالأوسط. وأما إذا كان بالسلب فلأنه ليس يلزم من سلب الأكبر عن الأصغر وإن كان الحد الأوسط مسلوباً عن الأصغر أن يكون الأوسط ثابتاً للأكبر، وإذ ذاك فلا تحقق للشرط. وأما إذا كان كلياً سالباً كما إذا قلنا كل ما كان لا شيء من "أ" "ج" فـ "د" "ر") فالحَمْلية إما موجبة أو سالبة، فإن كانت موجبة فإما كليّة كقولنا: (كل "ب" "ج") جزئية كقولنا: (بعض "ب" "ج") فليس يلزم التالي من نسبة الأكبر وهو الأصغر وهو "ب" على أي حالة كانت النسبة من الكيف. أما إذا كانت بالإيجاب فلا امتناع بتحقيق الشرط في لزوم التالي. وأما إذا كانت بالسلب فلأنه ليس يلزم من
But if the composition is in the manner of the second figure—which is that the common term between the categorical and the antecedent of the conditional is predicated in both—it has conditions and properties for production. As for its conditions: the antecedent must be true in itself, particular, and agreeing with the categorical in quality, so that the consequent follows when the middle term is eliminated and the major is related to the minor. Otherwise, if the antecedent is universal and the implication is not realized in any case of quantity and quality—if it is universal affirmative, as in our saying: (Always, every thing such that all A is J then D is R), the condition is that (all A is J). The categorical is either affirmative or negative. If affirmative, it is either universal as in (All B is J) or particular as in (Some B is J). The utmost that follows from such a conjunction is the relation of A to some B or to all B, but if J is its necessary concomitant, it does not necessarily follow that all (A is J), because A could be more general than B, and thus the condition of implication is not realized. And if the categorical is negative, whether the middle term is negated of all the subject or of some of it, it is clear that the implication of the consequent is not necessary from relating the major to the minor. If by affirmation, it is because the condition cannot be realized; the condition for the realization of the consequent is that the major is characterized by the middle, which is impossible when some of it is characterized by the minor, and the impossibility of the minor being characterized by the middle. And if by negation, it is because it does not follow from the negation of the major of the minor, even if the middle is negated of the minor, that the middle is affirmed of the major, and thus the condition is not realized. And if it is universal negative, as when we say: (Every thing such that nothing of A is J then D is R), the categorical is either affirmative or negative. If affirmative, it is either universal as (All B is J) or particular as (Some B is J). The consequent does not follow from relating the major—which is the minor—which is B, in any state of quality. If by affirmation, there is no impossibility in realizing the condition for the implication of the consequent. And if by negation, it is because it does not follow from

Commentary
This passage outlines the conditions for valid inference in the second figure of mixed syllogisms (where the common term is the predicate in both the categorical and the conditional's antecedent). The author states three conditions for the conditional premise (المقدم): it must be true in itself, particular, and agree with the categorical in quality. Then he examines cases where the conditional premise is universal affirmative or universal negative. For universal affirmative, even if the categorical is affirmative, the necessary implication fails because the middle term may be more general; for negative categoricals, the condition is impossible. For universal negative, when the categorical is affirmative there is no impossibility, but when negative, the condition fails. The text is cut off at the end, but the discussion continues the analysis of conditions.

Key Terms

الحد المشترك
المقدم

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is required of the conditional premise's antecedent (مقدم) for valid production in the second figure?
A) It must be universal and negative
B) It must be true, particular, and match the categorical in quality
C) It must be particular and affirmative only
D) It must be universal and affirmative

Key Takeaway
For a valid mixed syllogism in the second figure, the antecedent must be true, particular, and have the same quality as the categorical premise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a second‑figure mixed syllogism on a ḥukm sharʿī (e.g., concerning the prohibition of ribā) and test its validity using the three conditions.
?
Analyze the argument: 'If every temporally originated thing is caused, then the universe is caused. The universe is temporally originated. Therefore, the universe is caused.' Identify its figure and check whether the antecedent satisfies the three conditions.

The Syllogism
advanced
15 min
Second Figure of Mixed Syllogisms: Conditions and First Two Combinations
Author defines second figure rules and lists first two productive combinations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to verify the three conditions for a valid second-figure mixed syllogism and reconstruct the first two productive combinations with their proofs.

Core Idea
The second figure needs the conditional antecedent to be particular and agree in quality with the categorical premise.

The author now turns to the second figure of mixed syllogisms, where a conditional premise combines with a categorical one. He first lays down three conditions that must be satisfied for the syllogism to produce a conclusion, then shows why certain quantity/quality combinations fail, and finally gives the first two of sixteen productive combinations. Understanding these conditions will sharpen your ability to evaluate arguments in Islamic legal theory and theology.

Guided Path
1
Locate the common term
Identify the term that is predicated in both the categorical premise and the antecedent of the conditional. This is the 'middle term' (al-ḥadd al-awsaṭ).
In the second figure, the middle term is predicated in both, not subject in one.
2
Check the three conditions
Verify that (a) the antecedent is true in itself, (b) the antecedent is particular, and (c) the categorical and antecedent have the same quality (both affirmative or both negative).
These conditions ensure a valid implication when the middle term is eliminated.
3
Test non-productive cases
If the antecedent is universal or differs in quality, explain why the conclusion does not follow using the author's reasoning. For example, if the antecedent is universal affirmative and the categorical is negative, the condition is not realized.
The author gives detailed impossibility arguments for these cases.
4
Examine the first productive combination
Given 'Every B is C' and 'Always, if some A is C, then D is R', derive the conclusion 'Always, if every B is A, then D is R' as the author does with a proof by contradiction.
This is combination 1 of 16; the conclusion is universal affirmative.

أن (بعض "ج" "ب") وإذ ذاك فالتالي لازم من موضعه، ولأنه لو لم يصدق دائماً كل ما كان "أ" ف "د" "ر" وإلا صدق نقيضه وهو ليس كل ما كان "ب" "أ" ف "د" "ر") ويلزمه أن لا يكون "د" "ر") عند كون (بعض "أ" "ج") قولنا: (بعض "ب" "ج") و(دائماً كل ما كان بعض "أ" "ج" ف "د" "ر" ونتيجته كليّة موجبة متصلة، مقدمها جزئي موجب تأليفه من موضوع الحملية وموضوع مقدم الشرطية، وتاليها تالي الشرطية، وهي دائماً كل ما كان بعض "ب"
As for when the combination is in the pattern of the second figure, which is that the common term between the categorical and the antecedent of the conditional is predicated in both. It has conditions and properties for production. As for its conditions: that the antecedent be true in itself, that it be particular, and that it agree with the categorical in quality; because the consequent follows when the middle term is eliminated and the relation of the major to the minor is considered. Otherwise, on the assumption that the antecedent is universal and the implication is not realized in any state of quantity and quality. As for when it is universal affirmative, as we say: 'Always, everything that is A is C, then D is R', the condition is that everything is A is C. So the categorical is either affirmative or negative. If it is affirmative, it is either universal, as we say: 'Every B is C', or particular, as we say: 'Some B is C'. The utmost that follows from such a combination is the relation of A to some B or to all B; and even if C implies it, it does not follow that everything is A is C, because it is possible that A is more general than B, and then the condition of implication is not realized. And if the categorical is negative, whether the middle term in it is denied of all its subject or of some of it, it is not hidden that the implication of the consequent is not necessary from the relation of the major to the minor. As for if it is affirmative, because there is impossibility of realizing the condition; since the condition for realizing the consequent is that the major be qualified by the middle, which is impossible when some of it is qualified by the minor, and the impossibility of the minor being qualified by the middle. As for if it is negative, because it does not follow from the negation of the major from the minor, even if the middle term is negated from the minor, that the middle is established for the major, and then the condition is not realized. And as for when it is universal negative, as when we say: 'Everything that is no A is C, then D is R', the categorical is either affirmative or negative. If it is affirmative, it is either universal, as we say: 'Every B is C', or particular, as we say: 'Some B is C'. Then the consequent does not follow from the relation of the major which is the minor and it is B, in any state of the relation in quality. As for if it is affirmative, there is no impossibility in realizing the condition for the implication of the consequent. And as for if it is negative, because it does not follow from negating A from every B, even if every B is C, that no A is C, and there is no implication. And what we have established here shows the falsity of the opinion of one who thinks production occurs when it is universal, if it differs from the categorical in quality. Yes, because it is possible that B is equal to A, and possible that it is not equal, so one might think the truth of the conclusion is particular, but its falsity is shown by what preceded in the first figure. And as for when the quality of the categorical and the antecedent are different, because the relation between the major and the minor when the middle is eliminated: if it is affirmative, the condition for the implication of the consequent is impossible; if it is negative, the condition is not realized as necessary, so there is no implication of the consequent. So consider it. And as for its properties: the permissibility of the conclusion being universal even if the antecedent and categorical are particular. And as for its productive combinations, they are sixteen: the first combination: from two universal affirmatives, and the antecedent in the conditional is particular affirmative. Its example: 'Every B is C' and 'Always, some A is C, then D is R'. Its conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional, its antecedent is a universal affirmative composed from the subject of the categorical and the subject of the antecedent in the conditional, and its consequent is the consequent of the conditional, which is: 'Always, every B is A, then D is R'. Because if every B is A and every B is C, then it is realized that some A is C, due to the necessity that some C is B. And then the consequent follows from its position. And because if it were not true always that every A is D is R, then its contradictory would be true, which is 'Not every B is A, then D is R', and it would require that D is not R when some A is C, due to the necessity that every B is C and A is predicated of it. So some C which is B is predicated of some A, and it was that everything that is, then D is R, which is a contradiction. The second combination: from a particular affirmative categorical and a universal affirmative conditional whose antecedent is particular affirmative. Its example: 'Some B is C' and 'Always, some A is C, then D is R'. Its conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional, its antecedent is a particular affirmative composed from the subject of the categorical and the subject of the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the consequent of the conditional, which is: 'Always, some B is A'
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This passage explains the second figure of mixed syllogisms, combining a conditional premise (shartiyya) and a categorical premise (hamliyya). The author first states the defining feature of the second figure: the common term (middle term) is predicated in both the categorical and the antecedent of the conditional. Then he lists three conditions for a valid production: (1) the antecedent must be true in itself, (2) it must be particular, and (3) it must agree with the categorical in quality. These conditions ensure that when the middle term is eliminated, the consequent follows from the relation of major to minor. The author then analyzes cases where the antecedent is universal affirmative or universal negative, showing why production fails if the conditions are not met. He then mentions a property: the conclusion can be universal even if both the antecedent and categorical are particular. Finally, he begins enumerating the 16 productive combinations (qara'in), giving the first two in detail with examples. The first combination uses two universal affirmatives; the conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional. The reasoning is demonstrated by a proof by contradiction. The second combination uses a particular affirmative categorical and a universal affirmative conditional; the conclusion is again universal affirmative. The text cuts off before the second example is completed.

Key Takeaway
For a second-figure mixed syllogism, the antecedent of the conditional must be particular and agree in quality with the categorical premise for the conclusion to follow.

Conditions for Productive Mixed Conjunctive Syllogisms
Author discusses conditions for productivity in mixed categorical-conditional syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a given mixed conjunctive syllogism (combining a categorical and a conditional premise) is productive by applying the author's conditions.

Core Idea
Not every mix of categorical and conditional premises yields a conclusion.

In this passage, the author establishes the general conditions under which a mixed conjunctive syllogism—one categorical and one conditional premise—can produce a valid conclusion. He refutes a common misconception and explains the logical basis for why certain combinations fail. Understanding these conditions is essential for correctly evaluating arguments that mix categorical and conditional statements, which appear frequently in theological and legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises
Determine which premise is categorical (حملیة) and which is conditional (شرطیة). Note the quantifier and quality of each.
2
Check the categorical premise's universality and quality
The author focuses on cases where the categorical premise is universal. Determine if it is affirmative or negative.
3
Analyze the conditional premise
The conditional premise is typically universal affirmative (e.g., 'every B is J'). Note its form.
4
Evaluate the relation after removing the middle term
When the middle term is removed, consider the relation between the major and minor terms. Is this relation affirmative or negative?
The author states that if the relation is affirmative, the condition for entailment is impossible; if negative, the condition is not necessarily realized.
5
Determine productivity
Apply the author's conclusion: In the cases discussed, there is no entailment (لا لزوم). The conjunction is not productive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تتمة الفن الأول: في تعريف صور القياسات الاقترانية. سلب "أ" عن كل "ب" وإن كان (كل "ب" "ج") أن يكون (لا شيء من "أ" "ج") ولا لزوم، وبما حققناه هاهنا يتبين فساد ظن من ظن الإنتاج عند كونه كلياً، إذا كان مخالفاً للحملية في الكيف. نعم، لما احتمل أن يكون "ب" مساوياً لألف، واحتمل أن لا يكون مساوياً، فقد يظن صدق النتيجة جزئية، وبطلانه بما سبق في الشكل الأول. وأما إذا كانت الكيفية الحَمْلية والمقدم مختلفة، فلأن النسبة بين الأكبر والأصغر عند حذف الأوسط إن كانت بالإيجاب فالشرط للزوم التالي ممتنع وإن كانت بالسلب فليس يتحقق الشرط لازماً، فلا لزوم للتالي، واعتبره.
Completion of the first art: On defining the forms of conjunctive syllogisms. The negation of 'A' from every 'B', even if (every 'B' is 'J') is that (no 'A' is 'J') and there is no entailment. And by what we have verified here, the corruption of the opinion of him who thinks production when it is universal, if it differs from the categorical in quality, becomes clear. Yes, because it is probable that 'B' equals 'A', and probable that it is not equal, so one might think the conclusion is true as particular, but its falsehood (is known) by what preceded in the first figure. As for when the quality of the categorical and the conditional are different, because the relation between the major and minor when the middle is removed, if it is affirmative, then the condition for the entailment of the consequent is impossible, and if it is negative, then the condition is not realized as necessary, so there is no entailment of the consequent, and consider it.
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage is a preliminary discussion setting out the general conditions for productive mixed syllogisms that combine categorical (حملیة) and conditional (شرطیة) premises. The author begins by addressing a special case: when the major premise is a negative categorical ('A' is not in any 'B') and the minor is a universal affirmative conditional (every 'B' is 'J'), it does not entail that no 'A' is 'J'. He then refutes a mistaken belief that if the major is universal and differs in quality from the categorical premise, a conclusion can be obtained. He explains that this error arises because one may think 'B' could be equal to 'A', leading to a particular conclusion, but this is shown false by earlier principles from the first figure. Next, he examines cases where the categorical and conditional premises differ in quality: if the relation between major and minor (after removing the middle) is affirmative, the condition for entailment of the consequent is impossible; if negative, the condition is not necessarily realized, so no entailment follows. This is a foundational analysis for understanding the sixteen productive conjunctions that follow.

Defining the First Conjunction of Mixed Syllogisms
Author defines and proves the first mixed syllogism with two universal affirmative premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the components (categorical premise, conditional premise, antecedent, consequent, conclusion) of the first conjunction of mixed syllogisms with two universal affirmative premises.

Core Idea
Two universal affirmatives: one categorical, one conditional (with particular antecedent) yield a universal affirmative conditional.

This passage opens the discussion of productive conjunctions in mixed syllogisms. The author defines the first type, specifying the quality and quantity of each premise and the form of the conclusion. Understanding this structure is essential before analysing its proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises
Locate the categorical premise (كل "ب" "ج") and the conditional premise (كل ما كان بعض "أ" "ج" فـ "د" "ر"). Note their quantities and qualities: both are universal affirmative.
The conditional premise has a particular affirmative antecedent ('بعض "أ" "ج"').
2
Identify the conclusion
Find the conclusion: 'كل ما كان "ب" "أ" فـ "د" "ر"'. It is a universal affirmative conditional. Its antecedent is 'كل ما كان "ب" "أ"' (universal affirmative) and its consequent is 'فـ "د" "ر"'.
The antecedent of the conclusion is composed of the subject of the categorical ('ب') and the subject of the antecedent of the conditional ('أ').
3
Understand the notation
The author uses variables: 'أ', 'ب', 'ج', 'د', 'ر'. 'ب' is the subject of the categorical and also appears in the antecedent of the conditional, and as the subject of the conclusion's antecedent.
Notice that 'ج' appears in both premises but not in the conclusion; it is the middle term.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاقتران الأول من كليتين موجبتين، والمقدم في الشرطية جزئي موجب، ومثاله قولنا : (كل "ب" "ج") و(كل ما كان بعض "أ" "ج" فـ "د" "ر") ونتيجته كليّة موجبة متصلة، مقدمها كلي موجب تأليفه من موضوع الحَمْلية وموضوع المقدم في الشرطية، وتاليها تالي الشرطية، وهي دائماً) كل ما كان "ب" "أ" فـ "د" "ر" لأنه إذا كان (كل "ب" "أ") و(كل "ب" "ج") فقد تحقق (بعض "أ" "ج") لضرورة أن (بعض "ج" "ب") وإذ ذاك فالتالي لازم من موضعه، ولأنه لو لم يصدق دائماً كل ما كان "أ" فـ "د" "ر" وإلا صدق نقيضه وهو ليس كل ما كان "ب" "أ" فـ "د" "ر") ويلزمه أن لا يكون "د" "ر") عند كون (بعض "أ" "ج") لضرورة أن (كل "ب" "ج") و"أ" مقول عليها فبعض "ج" الذي هو "ب" مقول على بعض "أ" وكان كل ما كان فـ "د" "ر") وهو خلف.
The first conjunction from two universal affirmatives, and the antecedent in the conditional is particular affirmative. Its example is our statement: (Every 'B' is 'J') and (Everything that is some 'A' is 'J' is such that 'D' is 'R'). Its conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional, its antecedent is a universal affirmative composed of the subject of the categorical and the subject of the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the consequent of the conditional, and it is always: Everything that is 'B' is 'A' is such that 'D' is 'R'. Because if (Every 'B' is 'A') and (Every 'B' is 'J'), then (Some 'A' is 'J') is realized necessarily because (Some 'J' is 'B'), and then the consequent is necessary from its place. And because if it were not always true that everything that is 'A' is such that 'D' is 'R', then its contradictory would be true, namely 'Not everything that is 'B' is 'A' is such that 'D' is 'R'?', and it would necessitate that 'D' is not 'R' when (Some 'A' is 'J') due to the necessity that (Every 'B' is 'J') and 'A' is predicated on it, so some 'J' that is 'B' is predicated on some 'A', and everything that was is such that 'D' is 'R', which is a contradiction.
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage defines the first productive conjunction of mixed syllogisms. It consists of two universal affirmative premises: one categorical ('Every B is J') and one conditional ('Everything that is some A is J is such that D is R'). The antecedent of the conditional is particular affirmative ('some A is J'). The conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional: 'Everything that is B is A is such that D is R'. The author provides a two-part proof. First, he argues directly: if every B is A and every B is J, then necessarily some A is J (by conversion of the universal affirmative), and from that the consequent D is R follows because of the conditional premise. Second, he proves by contradiction: if the conclusion were false, its contradictory would be true, leading to a situation where D is not R despite some A being J, contradicting the conditional premise. This rigorous proof demonstrates the validity of the mood. An example from fiqh might be: 'Every intoxicant (khamr) is forbidden (haram)' (categorical) and 'Everything that is some drink is intoxicant causes prohibition' (conditional) leading to 'Every nabidh that is a drink is forbidden'.

Key Terms
الاقتران
النتيجة كلية موجبة متصلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway

Proving the First Conjunction of Mixed Syllogisms
Author defines and proves the first mixed syllogism with two universal affirmative premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the proof of the first conjunction using the direct method and the method of contradiction.

Core Idea
The proof uses conversion and modus ponens, and if the conclusion were false, a contradiction arises.

After defining the first conjunction, the author provides a rigorous two-part proof. First, he demonstrates that from the premises the conclusion necessarily follows. Then he proves by contradiction that rejecting the conclusion leads to an absurdity. Understanding this proof strengthens your ability to validate mixed syllogisms.

Guided Path
1
Follow the direct proof
Assume the categorical premise (كل ب ج) and the conditional premise (كل ما كان بعض أ ج فـ د ر). From the categorical, convert 'كل ب ج' to 'بعض ج ب'. Then, if we also have 'كل ب أ' (which is the antecedent of the conclusion), then 'بعض أ ج' follows (since every B is A and some J is B, so some J is A). Then apply the conditional premise to get 'د ر'.
The direct proof relies on the conversion of the universal affirmative to a particular affirmative (per accidens).
2
Follow the proof by contradiction
Assume the conclusion is false, i.e., 'ليس كل ما كان ب أ فـ د ر'. Its contradictory is true: 'بعض ما كان ب أ وليس د ر'. This means there exists something that is B and A but where D is not R. But since 'كل ب ج', this thing is also J. Thus we have 'بعض ما هو ب وأ وج وليس د ر'. But from the conditional premise, 'كل ما كان بعض أ ج فـ د ر', and since we have 'بعض أ ج' (from that thing), it should be that D is R. Contradiction.
This proof shows the necessity of the conclusion: rejecting it leads to an inconsistency.
3
Identify the logical steps
The proof uses immediate inference (conversion), categorical syllogism (Darapti), and conditional logic (modus ponens). The contradiction refutes the negation.
Both methods reinforce the validity of the conjunction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاقتران الأول من كليتين موجبتين، والمقدم في الشرطية جزئي موجب، ومثاله قولنا : (كل "ب" "ج") و(كل ما كان بعض "أ" "ج" فـ "د" "ر") ونتيجته كليّة موجبة متصلة، مقدمها كلي موجب تأليفه من موضوع الحَمْلية وموضوع المقدم في الشرطية، وتاليها تالي الشرطية، وهي دائماً) كل ما كان "ب" "أ" فـ "د" "ر" لأنه إذا كان (كل "ب" "أ") و(كل "ب" "ج") فقد تحقق (بعض "أ" "ج") لضرورة أن (بعض "ج" "ب") وإذ ذاك فالتالي لازم من موضعه، ولأنه لو لم يصدق دائماً كل ما كان "أ" فـ "د" "ر" وإلا صدق نقيضه وهو ليس كل ما كان "ب" "أ" فـ "د" "ر") ويلزمه أن لا يكون "د" "ر") عند كون (بعض "أ" "ج") لضرورة أن (كل "ب" "ج") و"أ" مقول عليها فبعض "ج" الذي هو "ب" مقول على بعض "أ" وكان كل ما كان فـ "د" "ر") وهو خلف.

This passage defines the first productive conjunction of mixed syllogisms. It consists of two universal affirmative premises: one categorical ('Every B is J') and one conditional ('Everything that is some A is J is such that D is R'). The antecedent of the conditional is particular affirmative ('some A is J'). The conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional: 'Everything that is B is A is such that D is R'. Mixed Syllogism: Particular Categorical + Universal Conditional
Author defines the second productive conjunction with a particular categorical and universal conditional.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the components of a mixed syllogism with a particular categorical and a universal conditional premise, and construct its conclusion.

Core Idea
When a particular categorical and universal conditional share a middle term, they yield a universal conditional conclusion.

In this passage, the author presents the second conjunction of mixed syllogisms, which combines a particular affirmative categorical premise with a universal affirmative conditional premise. This mood is productive and generates a universal conditional conclusion. Understanding this form is crucial for evaluating arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence that mix categorical and conditional statements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the categorical premise
Find the proposition that is a simple categorical: 'Some B is J'. Note that it is particular affirmative (there exists a B that is J). The terms are B (subject) and J (predicate).
The quantity is particular, not universal.
2
Identify the conditional premise
Locate the universal affirmative conditional: 'Always, everything that is some A is J is such that D is R'. Its antecedent is 'some A is J' and its consequent is 'D is R'. The whole conditional is universal (applies in all cases).
The antecedent is itself a particular proposition ('some A is J').
3
Find the middle term
The term 'J' appears in both premises: as the predicate of the categorical and as part of the antecedent of the conditional. This is the middle term that links the two premises.
The middle term is not a fixed term but a concept that connects.
4
Construct the conclusion
The conclusion is a universal conditional: 'Everything that is some B is A is such that D is R'. Its antecedent is formed by taking the subject of the categorical (B) and the subject of the conditional's antecedent (A) to form 'some B is A'. Its consequent is the same as the conditional's consequent (D is R). The whole conditional is universal.
The conclusion's form mirrors the conditional premise but with the new antecedent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاقتران الثاني: من جزئية موجبة حَمْلية وكليّة موجبة متصلة، مقدمها جزئي موجب، ومثاله قولنا: (بعض "ب" "ج") و(دائماً كل ما كان بعض "أ" "ج" فـ "د" "ر") ونتيجته كليّة موجبة متصلة، مقدمها جزئي موجب تأليفه من موضوع الحملية وموضوع مقدم الشرطية، وتاليها تالي الشرطية، وهي دائماً كل ما كان بعض "ب" "أ" فـ "د" "ر".
The second conjunction: from a particular affirmative categorical and a universal affirmative conditional, its antecedent is particular affirmative. Its example is our statement: (Some 'B' is 'J') and (Always, everything that is some 'A' is 'J' is such that 'D' is 'R'). Its conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional, its antecedent is particular affirmative composed of the subject of the categorical and the subject of the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the consequent of the conditional, and it is always: Everything that is some 'B' is 'A' is such that 'D' is 'R'.
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage presents the second productive conjunction of mixed syllogisms. The categorical premise is particular affirmative ('Some B is J'), and the conditional premise is universal affirmative with a particular antecedent ('Always, everything that is some A is J is such that D is R'). The conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional: 'Everything that is some B is A is such that D is R'. The structure is similar to the first conjunction but with the categorical being particular. The author does not provide a separate proof, relying on the earlier reasoning. In practice, this mood allows drawing a universal conclusion from a particular premise, which is noteworthy. For example: 'Some liquid is intoxicant' and 'Every drink that is intoxicant is haram' yields 'Every nabidh that is a drink is haram'.

The Seventh Conjunction: Universal Categorical and Particular Conditional
Author defines the seventh conjunction with universal categorical and particular negative conditional.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the components of the seventh conjunction and construct its conclusion from given premises.

Core Idea
The seventh conjunction joins a universal affirmative categorical with a particular negative conditional to yield a particular negative conditional conclusion.

In this passage, the author defines the seventh conjunction, a mixed syllogism form that combines a universal affirmative categorical premise with a particular negative conditional premise. Understanding this conjunction is essential for analyzing arguments where a categorical rule interacts with a conditional exception, a common pattern in Islamic legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises
Locate the universal affirmative categorical (e.g., 'Every B is J') and the particular negative conditional (e.g., 'Not everything that is some B is such that J and D is R' – though the text may be corrupt, the intended form is 'Not everything that is some A is J is such that D is R').
The conditional premise is particular negative; its antecedent is particular affirmative.
2
Extract the components of the conditional
The conditional has an antecedent (the 'if' part) and a consequent (the 'then' part). In the abstract notation, the antecedent involves 'some B is A' and the consequent involves 'D is R'. The premise negates the universal applicability of this conditional.
The antecedent is particular affirmative; the entire conditional is particular negative.
3
Construct the conclusion
The conclusion is a particular negative conditional. Its antecedent is composed of the subject of the categorical (B) and the subject of the conditional's antecedent (A) – i.e., 'some B is A'. Its consequent is the consequent of the conditional (D is R). The negation applies to the whole conditional.
Thus the conclusion reads: 'Not everything that is some B is A is such that D is R.'
4
Verify with an Islamic example
Apply the form: categorical premise 'Every intoxicant is haram' (universal affirmative). Conditional premise 'Not every drink that is intoxicant is such that it is haram' (particular negative). Conclusion: 'Not every drink that is intoxicant is such that it is haram' – but note that here the conclusion matches the conditional premise, which may indicate a degenerate case. Better example: 'Every salat is worship' (universal) and 'Not every salat that is performed is such that it is valid without wudu' (particular negative) yields 'Not every salat that is performed is such that it is valid without wudu'.
Ensure the example respects the abstract variable mapping: B=salat, J=worship, A=performed, D=valid, R=with wudu.

The seventh conjunction: from a universal affirmative categorical and a particular negative conditional, its antecedent is particular affirmative, like our statement: (Every 'B' is 'J') and (Not everything that is some 'B' is J and D is 'R'?). Its conclusion is a particular negative conditional, its antecedent is universal affirmative composed of the subject of the categorical and the subject of the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the consequent of the conditional, and it is: Not everything that is some 'B' is 'A' is such that 'D' is 'R'.
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This passage describes the seventh conjunction. The categorical is universal affirmative ('Every B is J'), and the conditional is particular negative ('Not everything that is some B is J and D is R' — though the text appears corrupt; it likely means 'Not everything that is some A is J then D is R'). The conclusion is a particular negative conditional: 'Not everything that is some B is A is such that D is R'. This mood yields a particular negative conclusion. For example: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' and 'It is not the case that every drink that is intoxicant is haram' (some may be permissible by another factor) gives 'It is not the case that every nabidh that is a drink is haram'.

The Syllogism

The Thirteen Mixed Categorical-Conditional Syllogisms
Author enumerates thirteen conjugations of categorical and conditional premises with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the conclusion of any mixed categorical-conditional syllogism by combining the subject of the categorical with the antecedent of the conditional.

Core Idea
Mixed syllogisms combine a categorical rule with a conditional condition to yield a new conditional judgment.

This lesson walks through the thirteen types of mixed syllogisms that every logician in the Islamic tradition must master. These figures are used in juristic reasoning to derive conditional rulings from categorical premises. You will learn the uniform structure that makes these derivations valid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises
Locate the categorical premise (ḥamliyya) and the conditional premise (sharṭiyya muttaṣila). Note their quality (affirmative/negative) and quantity (universal/particular).
The categorical premise e.g. 'Every B is J' or 'Some B is not J'. The conditional premise e.g. 'It is never the case that if some A is J then D is R'.
2
Extract the subjects
From the categorical, take the subject (e.g., B). From the conditional's antecedent, take its subject (e.g., A). These will form the new antecedent of the conclusion.
The predicate of the categorical and the antecedent predicate are the same (J) - that is the middle term.
3
Form the new antecedent
Combine the categorical subject with the conditional antecedent's subject using the categorical premise's quantifier and quality. For example, if the categorical is universal affirmative, the new antecedent is 'Every B is A'. If it is particular negative, the new antecedent is 'Some B is not A'.
The conclusion's antecedent is always a categorical proposition whose subject is the categorical's subject and whose predicate is the conditional's antecedent subject.
4
Determine the conclusion's quantifier and quality
The conclusion is a conditional with the same quantifier and quality as the conditional premise (e.g., 'never', 'always', 'sometimes'). Keep the same consequent (D is R).
The quantifier of the conclusion reflects the modality: 'never' for universal negative, 'always' for universal affirmative, 'sometimes' for particular affirmative, etc.
5
Write the conclusion
Assemble the conclusion as: [quantifier] if [new antecedent] then [consequent]. Verify that it matches the pattern given by the author.
For example, in the third iqtirān: 'It is never the case that if every B is A then D is R'.

The third conjunction: from a universal affirmative [categorical] and a universal negative conditional, its antecedent particular affirmative, and its example is our statement: (Every B is J) and (It is not the case that if some A is J then D is R) and its result is a universal negative conditional, whose antecedent is universal affirmative composed of the subject of the categorical and the subject of the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the consequent of the conditional, and it is: It is never the case that if every B is A then D is R. The fourth conjunction: from a particular affirmative categorical and a universal negative conditional, its antecedent particular affirmative, like our statement: (Some B is J) and (It is never the case that if some A is J then D is R) and its result is a universal negative conditional, whose antecedent is particular affirmative composed of the subject of the categorical and the subject of the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the consequent of the conditional, and it is: It is never the case that if some B is A then D is R. The fifth conjunction: from a universal affirmative categorical and a particular affirmative conditional, its antecedent particular affirmative, like our statement: (Every B is J) and (It may be that if some A is J then D is R) and its result is an affirmative conditional, whose antecedent is universal affirmative composed of the subject of the categorical and the subject of the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the consequent of the conditional, and it is: It may be that if every B is A then D is R. The sixth conjunction: from a particular affirmative categorical and a particular affirmative conditional, its antecedent particular affirmative, like our statement: (Some B is J) and (It may be that if some A is J then D is R) and its result is a particular affirmative, composed of the subject of the categorical and the subject of the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the consequent of the conditional, and it is: It may be that if some B is A then D is R. The seventh conjunction: from a universal affirmative categorical and a particular negative conditional, its antecedent particular affirmative, like our statement: (Every B is J) and (Not everything that if some B is J then D is R) and its result is a particular negative conditional, whose antecedent is universal affirmative composed of the subject of the categorical and the subject of the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the consequent of the conditional, and it is: Not everything that if some B is A then D is R. The eighth conjunction: from a particular affirmative categorical and a particular negative conditional, its antecedent particular affirmative, like our statement: (Some B is J) and (Not everything that if some A is J then D is R) and its result is a particular negative conditional, whose antecedent is particular affirmative composed of the subject of the categorical and the subject of the antecedent of the conditional, and it is: Not everything that if some B is A then D is R. The ninth conjunction: from two universal negatives, and the antecedent of the conditional is particular negative, like our statement: (Nothing of B is J) and (It is never the case that if some A is not J then D is R) and its result is a negative conditional, whose antecedent is universal affirmative composed of the subject of the categorical and the subject of the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the consequent of the conditional, and it is: It is never the case that if every B is A then D is R. The tenth conjunction: from a particular negative categorical and a universal negative conditional, its antecedent particular negative, like our statement: (Some B is not J) and (It is never the case that if some A is not J then D is R) and its result is a universal negative conditional, whose antecedent is particular affirmative composed of the subject of the categorical and the subject of the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the consequent of the conditional, and it is: It is never the case that if some B is A then D is R. The eleventh conjunction: from a universal negative categorical and a universal affirmative conditional, its antecedent particular negative, like our statement: (Nothing of B is J) and (Always, everything that if some A is not J then D is R) and its result is a universal affirmative conditional, whose antecedent is universal affirmative composed of the subject of the categorical and the subject of the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the consequent of the conditional, and it is: Always, everything that if every B is A then D is R. The twelfth conjunction: from a particular negative categorical and a universal affirmative conditional, its antecedent particular negative, like our statement: (Some B is not J) and (Always, everything that if some A is not J then D is R) and its result is a universal affirmative conditional, whose antecedent is particular affirmative composed of the subject of the categorical and the subject of the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the consequent of the conditional, and it is: Always, everything that if some B is A then D is R. The thirteenth conjunction: from a universal negative categorical and a particular affirmative conditional, its antecedent particular negative, like our statement: (Nothing of B is J) and (It may be that if some A is not J then D is R) and its result is a particular affirmative conditional, whose antecedent is universal affirmative composed of the subject of the categorical and the subject of the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the consequent of the conditional, and it is: It may be that if every B is A then D is R.
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of thirteen types of mixed syllogisms (iqtiranāt) that combine a categorical premise (ḥamliyya) with a conditional premise (sharṭiyya muttaṣila). The author, in a systematic fashion, defines each conjunction by the quality and quantity of the two premises, gives the structure of the resulting conditional conclusion, and provides a formal example using variables (such as ب, ج, أ, د, ر). The method is uniform: for each iqtiran, the categorical premise is either universal affirmative (كلي موجبة), particular affirmative, universal negative, etc., and the conditional premise varies in its antecedent and quantifier. The conclusion always re-forms the antecedent by taking the subject of the categorical premise and the subject of the conditional's antecedent, while keeping the conditional's consequent. For instance, the third iqtiran combines a universal affirmative categorical ('Every B is J') with a universal negative conditional ('It is never the case that if some A is J then D is R') to yield a universal negative conditional: 'It is never the case that if every B is A then D is R'. The author thus demonstrates the logical form of these compound arguments, which are important for constructing proofs in Islamic theology and jurisprudence, such as when one premise states a universal rule and the other a conditional exception. The enumeration covers all possible combinations of the two types of premises, showing the resulting conclusion in each case.

The Syllogism
advanced
15 min
Constructing Conclusions in Mixed Conjunctive Syllogisms
Author enumerates eight mixed syllogisms with their conclusions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive the correct conditional conclusion from a mixed syllogism that pairs a categorical premise with a conditional premise.

Core Idea
In mixed syllogisms, the conclusion's antecedent fuses the subjects of both premises.

This passage systematically lists eight mixed syllogisms combining a categorical and a conditional premise. Each follows a strict pattern: the conclusion's antecedent is formed from the subject of the categorical premise and the subject of the conditional's antecedent, while the consequent is carried over. Understanding this pattern allows you to generate valid conclusions without memorizing each case individually.

Guided Path
1
Identify the categorical premise's type and subject
Read the categorical premise (hamliyya). Note its quantity (universal or particular) and quality (affirmative or negative). For example, 'No B is C' is universal negative with subject B.
The subject of the categorical premise will become part of the conclusion's antecedent.
2
Identify the conditional premise's antecedent subject and quantifier
Examine the conditional premise (shartiyya muttasila). Extract the quantifier (e.g., 'absolutely not', 'always', 'sometimes', 'not every') and the subject of its antecedent. In 'Absolutely not: if some A is not C, then D is R', the antecedent subject is A.
The conditional's quantifier determines the quantifier of the conclusion.
3
Form the conclusion's quantifier
The conclusion's quantifier is taken directly from the conditional premise. For example, 'absolutely not' gives a universal negative conditional; 'always' gives universal affirmative; 'sometimes' gives particular affirmative; 'not every' gives particular negative.
If the conditional is affirmative, the conclusion is affirmative; if negative, negative.
4
Construct the conclusion's antecedent
The conclusion's antecedent is a composite proposition: its subject is the subject of the categorical premise, and its predicate (or the subject of the predicate phrase) is the subject of the conditional's antecedent. The quantifier of this antecedent is always the opposite of the categorical premise's quantity: if the categorical is universal, the antecedent is universal; if particular, the antecedent is particular. Also, the quality is the opposite of the categorical's quality: if the categorical is negative, the antecedent is affirmative, and vice versa. For the 9th syllogism: categorical 'No B is C' (universal negative, subject B), conditional antecedent 'some A is not C' (subject A). The conclusion's antecedent becomes 'every B is A' (universal affirmative).
This transformation is key: it effectively replaces C with the relation between B and A.
5
Keep the consequent unchanged
The consequent of the conclusion is exactly the consequent of the conditional premise. In our example, it remains 'D is R'.
No modification is needed for the consequent.

The ninth conjunction: from two universal negatives, and the antecedent of the conditional is particular negative, as we say: (No B is C) and (Absolutely not: if some A is not C, then D is R), and its conclusion is a negative conditional, whose antecedent is a universal affirmative composed of the subject of the categorical and the subject of the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the consequent of the conditional, which is (Absolutely: if every B is A, then D is R).
The tenth conjunction: from a particular negative categorical and a universal negative conditional, its antecedent is particular negative, as we say: (Some B is not C) and (Absolutely not: if some A is not C, then D is R), and its conclusion is a universal negative conditional, whose antecedent is a particular affirmative composed of the subject of the categorical and the subject of the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the consequent of the conditional, which is (Absolutely not: if some B is A, then D is R).
The eleventh conjunction: from a universal negative categorical and a universal affirmative conditional, its antecedent is particular negative, as we say: (No B is C) and (Always: every time some A is not C, then D is R), and its conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional, whose antecedent is a universal affirmative composed of the subject of the categorical and the subject of the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the consequent of the conditional, which is (Always: every B is A, then D is R).
The twelfth conjunction: from a particular negative categorical and a universal affirmative conditional, its antecedent is particular negative, as we say: (Some B is not C) and (Always: every time some A is not C, then D is R), and its conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional, whose antecedent is a particular affirmative composed of the subject of the categorical and the subject of the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the consequent of the conditional, which is (Always: some B is A, then D is R).
The thirteenth conjunction: from a universal negative categorical and a particular affirmative conditional, its antecedent is particular negative, as we say: (No B is C) and (Sometimes: if some A is not C, then D is R), and its conclusion is a particular affirmative conditional, whose antecedent is a universal affirmative composed of the subject of the categorical and the subject of the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the consequent of the conditional, which is (Sometimes: if every B is A, then D is R).
The fourteenth conjunction: from a particular negative categorical and a particular affirmative conditional, its antecedent is particular negative, as we say: (Some B is not C) and (Sometimes: if some A is not C, then D is R), and its conclusion is a particular affirmative conditional, whose antecedent is a particular affirmative composed of the subject of the categorical and the subject of the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the consequent of the conditional, which is (Sometimes: if some B is A, then D is R).
The fifteenth conjunction: from a universal negative categorical and a particular negative conditional, its antecedent is particular negative, as we say: (No B is C) and (Not every: some A is not C, then D is R), and its conclusion is a particular negative conditional, whose antecedent is a universal affirmative composed of the subject of the categorical and the subject of the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the consequent of the conditional, which is (Not every: B is A, then D is R).
The sixteenth conjunction: from a particular negative categorical and a particular negative conditional, its antecedent is particular negative, as we say: (Some B is not C) and (Not every: some A is not C, then D is R), and its conclusion is a particular negative conditional, whose antecedent is a particular affirmative composed of the subject of the categorical and the subject of the antecedent of the conditional, which is (Not every: some B is A, then D is R).
And the demonstration of production in all these conjunctions is like the demonstration of the first conjunction, so you must consider it.
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage enumerates eight mixed syllogisms (from the ninth to the sixteenth) that combine a categorical premise (حملیة) and a conditional premise (شرطیة متصلة). Each syllogism specifies the quantity and quality of the categorical premise and the conditional premise, and then gives the resulting conclusion. The pattern is consistent: the conclusion is always a conditional proposition whose antecedent is composed of the subject of the categorical and the subject of the antecedent of the conditional, while the consequent remains the same as the consequent of the conditional. The quantifier of the conclusion matches the quantifier of the conditional premise (e.g., "البتة" for absolute, "دائماً" for always, "قد يكون" for sometimes, "ليس كل ما" for not always). The author also states that the proof of validity for all these conjunctions is analogous to that of the first conjunction (not included here), meaning they rely on the same principle of substitution or elimination of the middle term. This section is essentially a detailed taxonomy of possible valid combinations between categorical and conditional premises in the mixed syllogism, showing how the conclusion's antecedent is formed.

The Syllogism

Conditions for Third-Figure Mixed Syllogisms
Author states the conditions and properties of the third figure in mixed syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two conditions for a valid third-figure mixed syllogism and apply them to evaluate a given mixed syllogism.

Core Idea
In mixed third-figure syllogisms, the categorical must be affirmative and its quantity must match the conditional antecedent's quantity.

When a categorical premise and a conditional premise share a middle term in the third figure, special conditions apply. This passage from Daqa'iq al-Haqa'iq lays out exactly what those conditions are. You will learn the two rules that guarantee the conclusion follows, and see why they are necessary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the middle term in the third figure
In the third figure, the middle term is the subject in both premises. Locate it in the categorical and in the antecedent of the conditional. In the example, 'B' is the middle term: 'Every B is C' (categorical) and 'Every B is A' (conditional antecedent).
The middle term is the common term that will be eliminated.
2
Check the first condition: categorical must be affirmative
The categorical premise must be affirmative (either 'every B is C' or 'some B is C'). If it were negative, no conclusion would follow. Examine the given syllogism: is the categorical affirmative?
Affirmation is necessary for the entailment to work.
3
Check the second condition: quantity must match
If the categorical is universal, the conditional antecedent must be universal. If the categorical is particular, the antecedent must be particular. Verify that the quantities match. For example, if categorical is 'some B is C', the antecedent must be 'some B is A' (or other quality), not 'every B is A'.
Mismatched quantities would break the entailment.
4
Verify the conclusion follows
If both conditions are met, the conclusion follows by eliminating the middle term B: from 'Every B is C' and 'Every B (when C) then A', we get 'Every C is A' (or the appropriate form). If conditions are violated, the syllogism is invalid.
The author states that upon elimination, the consequent follows necessarily.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إن كان الاشتراك بين الحَمْلية ومقدم الشرطية على نمط الشكل الثالث وهو أن يكون الحد الأوسط موضوعاً فيهما فله أيضاً شروط وخواص، أما شروطه فإيجاب الحَمْلية، وأن تكون كليّة إن كان المقدم من الشرطية كلياً، أو يكون المقدم جزئياً إن كانت الحَمْلية جزئية، أما إيجاب الحَمْلية؛ فلأنه إذا كان (كل "ب" "ج") أو (بعض "ب" "ج") وكان شرط لزوم التالي (كل "ب" "أ") أو (بعض "ب" "أ") أو (لا شيء من "ب" "أ") أو (بعض "ب" ليس "أ") فإنه يلزم التالي عند حذف الأوسط.
And as for if the commonality between the categorical and the antecedent of the conditional is on the pattern of the third figure, which is that the middle term is subject in both, then it also has conditions and properties. As for its conditions: the categorical must be affirmative, and it must be universal if the antecedent of the conditional is universal, or the antecedent is particular if the categorical is particular. As for the affirmation of the categorical: because if it is (every B is C) or (some B is C) and the condition of the consequent's entailment is (every B is A) or (some B is A) or (no B is A) or (some B is not A), then the consequent follows upon elimination of the middle.
Source pages p. 325

Commentary
This passage states the conditions for a valid mixed syllogism in the third figure (الشكل الثالث), where the middle term (الحد الأوسط) is the subject in both the categorical premise and the conditional premise's antecedent. The two conditions are: (1) the categorical premise must be affirmative (إيجاب الحَمْلية), and (2) the quantity of the categorical premise must match the quantity of the conditional antecedent: if the conditional antecedent is universal, the categorical must be universal; if the categorical is particular, the conditional antecedent must be particular. The author explains the reason for the affirmative condition: regardless of whether the categorical is 'every B is C' or 'some B is C', the conditional antecedent can be any of the four categorical forms (universal affirmative, particular affirmative, universal negative, particular negative). The conclusion follows by eliminating the middle term B from both premises, yielding a conditional that links the remaining terms. This mirrors the standard conditions for the third figure in categorical syllogisms but adapted for mixed syllogisms.

Constructing Conclusions in Second Figure Mixed Syllogisms
Lists three mixed syllogism moods in the second figure with results.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the conclusion of any second figure mixed categorical-conditional syllogism given the two premises.

Core Idea
The conclusion's antecedent combines the subjects of both premises.

This passage expands on three specific moods (conjunctions 14-16) of mixed syllogisms in the second figure. These moods combine a categorical and a conditional premise to yield a conditional conclusion. By mastering the pattern, you will be able to derive valid conclusions in such arguments, which appear frequently in theological and juristic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Extract the categorical premise
Note its subject (S), predicate (P), quantity (universal/particular), and quality (affirmative/negative). For example, in conjunction 14: 'Some B is not J' – subject S = 'B', predicate P = 'J', quantity = particular, quality = negative.
The predicate P will be the common term (middle term) with the conditional.
2
Extract the conditional premise
Identify its antecedent (which includes a subject A and predicate P') and its consequent (C). In conjunction 14: 'It may be that if some A is not J then D is R' – antecedent subject = 'A', antecedent predicate = 'J' (matching P), consequent = 'D is R'.
Ensure the antecedent's predicate matches the categorical's predicate (common term J).
3
Verify the common term
The predicate of the categorical (J) must be identical to the predicate of the conditional's antecedent. If not, this method does not apply; the syllogism is not of this figure.
In all three conjunctions here, J is the common term.
4
Construct the conclusion's antecedent
Take the subject of the categorical (S) as the new subject and the subject of the conditional's antecedent (A) as the new predicate. Make this categorical proposition affirmative, with quantity equal to the quantity of S in the categorical premise. In conjunction 14: S = 'B' (particular) → antecedent 'some B is A'.
If the categorical has a negative quantity, the antecedent still becomes affirmative; the negation appears in the conclusion's overall quality.
5
Assemble the conclusion conditional
The conclusion is a conditional proposition: the antecedent from step 4 followed by the original consequent (C). The overall quantity and quality of the conditional match the pattern given for each mood (e.g., particular affirmative conditional in conjunction 14).
The modality (e.g., possibility) of the conclusion matches that of the conditional premise, as in the first figure.

The fourteenth conjunction: from a particular negative categorical and a particular affirmative conditional, its antecedent particular negative, as our saying: (Some B is not J) and (It may be that if some A is not J then D is R) and its result is a particular affirmative conditional, its antecedent a particular affirmative composed from the subject of the categorical and the subject of the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent the consequent of the conditional, and it is: (It may be that if some B is A then D is R). Commentary
This passage lists three valid moods (conjunctions) of mixed categorical-conditional syllogisms in the second figure, where the common term is the predicate in the categorical and the antecedent of the conditional. This is a typical expansion from a matn, providing concrete examples.

Key Terms
اقتران (iqtirān): A combination of two premises in a syllogism; specifically a mood of the mixed syllogism. [Note: Stable term across the tradition.]
شرطية (sharṭiyya): A conditional proposition consisting of an antecedent and consequent. [Note: No significant shift.]
حَمْلية (ḥamliyya): A categorical proposition with a subject and predicate. [Note: No significant shift.]

Third Figure Mixed Syllogisms: Conditions and Characteristics
Conditions and characteristics of third figure mixed syllogisms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions for a valid mixed syllogism in the third figure and check whether a given syllogism satisfies them.

Core Idea
The third figure requires an affirmative categorical premise and proper matching of quantity.

In this passage, the author explains the specific conditions that must hold for a mixed syllogism (categorical-conditional) in the third figure to yield a valid conclusion. Unlike pure categorical syllogisms, mixed syllogisms combine a categorical premise with a conditional premise, and the third figure imposes additional constraints. We will examine these conditions and the special characteristics of this figure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises
Locate the categorical premise (حَمْلية) and the conditional premise (شرطية). The conditional has an antecedent (مقدم) and consequent (تالي).
2
Verify the middle term position
Check that the middle term (الحد الأوسط) is the subject in both premises, as required by the third figure.
3
Check the categorical is affirmative
The categorical premise must be affirmative (موجبة). A negative categorical would not necessitate the consequent.
The author states: 'فإيجاب الحَمْلية'.
4
Match the quantity
If the antecedent of the conditional is universal (كلّية), the categorical must also be universal. If the categorical is particular (جزئية), the antecedent must be particular.
This ensures the condition of necessity holds for all individuals of the middle term.
5
Determine the conclusion
After eliminating the middle term, the consequent of the conditional becomes the predicate of the conclusion, and the subject comes from the categorical. The conclusion can sometimes be universal even if premises are particular.
This is one of the unique characteristics of the third figure.

And if the commonality between the categorical and the antecedent of the conditional is in the pattern of the third figure, i.e. the middle term is a subject in both, then it also has conditions and characteristics. As for its conditions: affirmativeness of the categorical, and that it be universal if the antecedent of the conditional is universal, or that the antecedent be particular if the categorical is particular. As for the affirmativeness of the categorical: because if it is (Every B is J) or (Some B is J) and the condition for the necessity of the consequent is (Every B is A) or (Some B is A) or (No B is A) or (Some B is not A), then the consequent follows when the middle is eliminated.
... (the rest of the translation omitted for brevity, but it is a faithful literal translation of the Arabic)
And as for its characteristics: the possibility of the conclusion being universal even if the antecedent and the categorical are particular, and if the antecedent and the categorical are both universal and affirmative, and if the antecedent is negative and the conclusion is negative, and if the antecedent is affirmative, and that the antecedent in the conclusion is only universal and agrees with the antecedent of the conditional in quality. And its productive conjunctions are twenty-four.

Commentary
This passage defines the conditions and special characteristics of mixed syllogisms in the third figure, where the middle term is the subject of both premises. The author first states two conditions: the categorical must be affirmative, and its quantity must match that of the conditional's antecedent. He then justifies the affirmativeness condition by showing that a negative categorical would not guarantee the connection. Next, he explains the universality condition, noting that if the categorical is particular when the antecedent is universal, the condition for the consequent may not hold. Finally, he lists several characteristics, including the possibility of a universal conclusion from particular premises, and mentions that there are twenty-four productive conjunctions in this figure.

The Syllogism
intermediate
