Third Figure Mixed Syllogisms: First Two Conjunctions
Gives the first two productive conjunctions of the third figure with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive the conclusion of a third-figure mixed syllogism from a universal categorical and a universal conditional premise.

Core Idea
Mixed syllogisms combine categorical and conditional to yield a conditional with composed antecedent.

The third figure of mixed syllogisms pairs a categorical premise with a conditional premise. The first two productive conjunctions (moods) are presented here: both have a universal affirmative categorical, but the conditional's antecedent is either universal affirmative or universal negative. The conclusion is always a universal affirmative conditional whose antecedent is a composition of the categorical's predicate and the conditional antecedent's predicate. This lesson will guide you through the construction and reasoning behind these conjunctions.

Guided Path
1
Extract the categorical premise
Identify the universal affirmative categorical: 'Every B is J'. Note that B is the middle term and J is the predicate (major term).
2
Extract the conditional premise
Identify the conditional: 'Every B that is A is such that D is R' (first conjunction) or 'Every B that is not A is such that D is R' (second conjunction). Note the predicate of the antecedent (A) and the consequent (D is R).
3
Form the conclusion's antecedent
The conclusion's antecedent is composed from the categorical's predicate (J) as subject and the conditional antecedent's predicate (A) as predicate, resulting in 'Every J is A' (if the conditional antecedent was affirmative) or 'Every J is not A' (if negative). The consequent remains 'D is R'. Thus the full conclusion is 'Every J that is A implies D is R' or 'Every J that is not A implies D is R'.
This composition is the key operation: the middle term B disappears, and the predicates combine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
#
الاقتران الأول من كليتين موجبتين والمقدم من الشرطية كلي موجب كقولنا: (كل "ب" "ج") و(كل ما كان "ب" "أ" فـ "د" "ر") ونتيجته كلية موجبة متصلة، مقدمها كلي موجب تأليفه من محمول الحَملية ومحمول مقدم الشرطية، وتاليها تالي الشرطية، وهي دائماً كل ما كان كل "ج" "أ" فـ "د" "ر") لأنه حينئذٍ يكون (كل "ب" "أ") .
الاقتران الثاني : من كليتين موجبتين والمقدم في الشرطية كلي سالب كقولنا: (كل "ب" "ج") و(دائماً كل ما كان لا شيء من "ب" "أ" فـ "د" "ر") ونتيجته كليّة موجبة متصلة، مقدمها كلي سالب تأليفه من محمول الحملية ومحمول مقدم

Second conjunction: from two universal affirmatives and the antecedent in the conditional is universal negative, as our saying: (Every B is J) and (Always every [case where] nothing of B is A then D is R). And its result is a universal affirmative conditional, its antecedent a universal negative composed from the predicate of the categorical and the predicate of the antecedent

The conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional whose antecedent is composed from the predicates of the premises. The author notes the justification: the middle term B is included under J, so the condition holds. The second mood has a universal affirmative categorical and a universal negative conditional antecedent, and the conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional with a universal negative antecedent. The text is cut off at the end of the second conjunction.

Conditions for Mixed Categorical-Conditional Syllogisms
Author states conditions and properties of mixed syllogisms with categorical minor premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the necessary conditions for a valid mixed categorical-conditional syllogism and predict the properties of its conclusion.

Core Idea
A mixed syllogism is valid only if the categorical premise is universal when the conditional is universal.

In this passage, the author lays out the precise conditions under which a syllogism that combines a categorical premise with a conditional premise yields a valid conclusion. Understanding these conditions is crucial for anyone who wants to construct or evaluate compound arguments in Islamic legal and theological reasoning. We will walk through each condition step by step, using the author's own notation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises
Locate the conditional and categorical premises. The author refers to the 'ratio of the major from the conditional' and 'minor of the categorical'.
2
Determine the required relationship when the conditional is affirmative
If the conditional premise has an affirmative antecedent (sharṭ luzūm al-tālī affirmative), then the middle term must relate to the major affirmatively.
3
Determine the required relationship when the conditional is negative
If the conditional is negative, the middle term must relate to the major negatively.
4
Check the universality condition
When the conditional premise is universal, the categorical premise must also be universal. Otherwise the implication may fail because the middle term could be more general than the minor.
5
Recognize the properties of the conclusion
The conclusion's antecedent is always universal and agrees in quality with the original conditional.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تتمة الفن الأول: في تعريف صور القياسات الاقترانية. ونسبة الأكبر من مقدم الشرطية إلى الأصغر من الحملية بالإيجاب إن كان شرط لزوم التالي نسبة الأوسط إلى الأكبر بالإيجاب؛ لضرورة دخول "ب" تحت الأصغر في الحَمْلية، وتحقق الشرط، أو بالسلب إن كان شرط لزوم التالي نسبة الأوسط إلى الأكبر في مقدم الشرطية بالسلب؛ لضرورة دخول الأوسط تحت الأصغر، وعلى تقدير كونها سالبة فلا يخفى أنه لا يلزم من سلب الأصغر عن الأوسط في الحملية نسبة الأكبر إلى الأصغر عند حذف الحد الأوسط بإيجاب ولا بسلب، وإذ ذاك فلا تحقق الشرط لزوم التالي، فلا لزوم . وأما اشتراط كون الحَمْلية كلية عند كليّة المقدم من الشرطية، فلتحقق شرط لزوم التالي من نسبة الأكبر إلى كل الأوسط، وإلا فعلى تقدير أن تكون جزئية، فالحكم بالأكبر على كل الأصغر قد لا يتحقق به شرط لزوم التالي من نسبة الأكبر إلى كل الأوسط؛ لجواز أن يكون الأوسط في الحَمْلية أعم من الأصغر؛ ولهذا المعنى وجب كون المقدم جزئياً، عند كون الحملية جزئية. وأما خواصه، فجواز كون النتيجة كلية وإن كان المقدم والحَمْلية جزئيين، وإن كان المقدم والحَمْلية كليين وموجبة، وإن كان المقدم سالباً وسالبة، وإن كان المقدم موجباً، وأن المقدم في النتيجة لا يكون إلا كلياً وموافقاً لمقدم الشرطية في الكيف. وأما قرائنه المنتجة فأربعة وعشرون
Continuation of the first art: On defining the forms of categorical syllogisms. The ratio of the major term from the premise of the conditional to the minor term of the categorical is by affirmation if the condition of the implication of the consequent is the ratio of the middle term to the major by affirmation; because it is necessary for 'b' to be under the minor in the categorical, and the condition is realized, or by negation if the condition of the implication of the consequent is the ratio of the middle term to the major in the premise of the conditional by negation; because it is necessary for the middle to be under the minor, and on the assumption that it is negative, it is not hidden that from the negation of the minor from the middle in the categorical there does not follow the ratio of the major to the minor when the middle term is eliminated, neither by affirmation nor by negation, and then the condition of the implication of the consequent is not realized, so there is no implication. And as for the condition that the categorical be universal when the premise of the conditional is universal, it is for the realization of the condition of the implication of the consequent from the ratio of the major to all of the middle, otherwise, on the assumption that it is particular, the judgment of the major on all of the minor may not realize the condition of the implication of the consequent from the ratio of the major to all of the middle; because it is possible that the middle in the categorical is more general than the minor; and for this meaning it is necessary that the premise be particular when the categorical is particular. And as for its properties: the possibility that the conclusion be universal even if the premise and the categorical are particular, and even if the premise and the categorical are universal and affirmative, and even if the premise is negative and the categorical is negative, and even if the premise is affirmative, and that the premise in the conclusion is only universal and agreeing with the premise of the conditional in quality. And as for its productive conjunctions, they are twenty-four.
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This passage defines the general conditions and properties of mixed syllogisms that combine a categorical premise (ḥamliyya) with a conditional premise (sharṭiyya). The author explains that for the implication (luzūm al-tālī) to hold, the ratio of the major term (al-akbar) from the conditional premise to the minor term (al-aṣghar) of the categorical must be either affirmative or negative depending on the nature of the conditional. Specifically, if the conditional has an affirmative antecedent, the middle term (al-awsaṭ) must relate to the major affirmatively; if the conditional is negative, the middle must relate to the major negatively. The author then addresses a condition: when the conditional premise is universal, the categorical premise must also be universal. This ensures that the major term applies to all instances of the middle term. Without this universality, the implication may fail if the middle term in the categorical is more general than the minor. Finally, the author lists some properties (khawāṣṣ) of such syllogisms: the conclusion can be universal even if the premises are particular, and the premise of the conclusion (the antecedent in the conditional conclusion) must always be universal and match the quality of the original conditional premise. The passage concludes by stating that there are twenty-four productive conjunctions (qarā'in muntija), which the author will enumerate.

Key Terms

شرط لزوم التالي
المقدم في النتيجة

The Second Productive Mixed Syllogism
Author provides the form and result of the second productive mixed syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the premises and conclusion of the second productive mixed syllogism and verify its form using Arabic logical variables.

Core Idea
Two universal affirmatives plus a universal negative antecedent yield a new conditional conclusion.

In the previous lesson, we saw the first productive mixed syllogism. Now, we explore the second conjunction, where the conditional premise has a universal negative antecedent. This configuration produces a conclusion that substitutes the categorical predicate into the conditional antecedent. Understanding this form sharpens your ability to combine categorical and conditional reasoning in Islamic theological arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the categorical premise
Locate the universal affirmative categorical premise: 'كل ب ج' (Every B is C). This premise states that all individuals of type B belong to type C.
B and C are terms from the logical variables in the passage.
2
Identify the conditional premise
Find the conditional premise: 'دائماً كل ما كان لا شيء من ب أ فـ د ر' (Always: whatever is not any B is A, then D is R). Note that the antecedent is 'لا شيء من ب أ' (universal negative: no B is A).
The conditional is universal affirmative in mode (دائماً) with a universal negative antecedent.
3
Extract the conclusion's antecedent
The conclusion's antecedent is formed by replacing B (the subject of the categorical) with C (the predicate of the categorical) in the conditional antecedent. Thus, 'لا شيء من ج أ' (no C is A).
This substitution relies on the inclusion 'every B is C'.
4
Write the complete conclusion
Combine the new antecedent with the original consequent: 'دائماً كل ما كان لا شيء من ج أ فـ د ر' (Always: whatever is not any C is A, then D is R). This is a universal affirmative conditional with a universal negative antecedent.
The author confirms this form is always productive (دائماً).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاقتران الثاني : من كليتين موجبتين والمقدم في الشرطية كلي سالب كقولنا: (كل "ب" "ج") و(دائماً كل ما كان لا شيء من "ب" "أ" فـ "د" ر") ونتيجته كليّة موجبة متصلة، مقدمها كلي سالب تأليفه من محمول الحملية ومحمول مقدم الشرطية، وتاليها تالي الشرطية، وهي دائماً) كل ما كان لا شيء من "ج" "أ" فـ "د" "ر" لأنه يكون (لا) شيء من "ب" "أ") لضرورة دخول كل "ب" في "ج".
The second conjunction: from two universal affirmatives, and the antecedent in the conditional is universal negative, like our saying: 'Every B is C' and 'Always every whatever is not any B is A is D is R', and its result is a universal affirmative conditional, its antecedent is universal negative composed from the predicate of the categorical and the predicate of the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the consequent of the conditional, and it is always 'Every whatever is not any C is A is D is R' because it is (not any B is A) due to the necessity of every B entering into C.
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This second conjunction also uses two universal affirmative premises, but the conditional antecedent is universal negative: 'Every B is C' and 'Always: whatever is not any B is A implies D is R'. The conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional with a universal negative antecedent: 'Every C is not A implies D is R'. The author explains that this follows because every B is contained in C, meaning that if something is not any B, it could still be C? Actually the reasoning: from 'Every B is C' and the conditional 'not any B is A => D is R', we infer that for any C, if it is not any B? Wait, the antecedent of the conclusion relates C and A negatively. The justification given: 'because it is not any B is A due to the necessity of every B entering into C.' This seems to use a form of contraposition or substitution. In traditional logic, from 'Every B is C' and 'No B is A => D is R', we can derive 'No C is A => D is R' only if we assume that 'No B is A' is equivalent to 'No C is A' given that B is a subset of C? Actually that would be invalid. Likely the author's reasoning is that since every B is C, the condition 'no B is A' is a sufficient condition for D is R, and since any C that is not A? Hmm. Perhaps the passage is obscure. For commentary, we simply explain the form as given without injecting modern validity concerns.

Fifth Productive Mixed Syllogism: Universal Affirmative Categorical + Universal Negative Conditional
Author provides the form and result of the fifth productive mixed syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fifth productive mixed syllogism and derive its conclusion from a universal affirmative categorical premise and a universal negative conditional premise.

Core Idea
Mixed syllogisms combine categorical and conditional premises; the fifth form yields a universal negative conditional conclusion.

In the previous lessons, you learned about mixed syllogisms that combine categorical and conditional premises. The fifth conjunction is particularly important because the conditional premise is universal negative. Understanding this form will enable you to recognize a valid pattern used in Islamic scholarly arguments, especially in Kalam and Usul al-Fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the categorical premise
The first premise is a universal affirmative categorical proposition, e.g., 'Every B is C'. Note that it asserts a universal relationship between two terms.
2
Identify the conditional premise
The second premise is a universal negative conditional proposition: 'It is not at all that whenever every B is A, then D is R.' The negation applies to the entire implication, denying it for all cases.
3
Construct the antecedent of the conclusion
The antecedent of the conclusion is formed by combining the predicate of the categorical (C) with the predicate of the antecedent of the conditional (A): 'Every C is A'. This is a new universal affirmative proposition.
4
Construct the consequent of the conclusion
The consequent of the conclusion is identical to the consequent of the conditional premise: 'D is R'. It remains unchanged.
5
Form the conclusion and verify the reasoning
The conclusion is a universal negative conditional: 'It is not at all that whenever every C is A, then D is R.' The author justifies this by noting that if the conclusion were false (i.e., if the implication held), then from 'Every B is C' and the assumed 'Every C is A' we would get 'Every B is A', which would make the original conditional premise false, a contradiction.
This is a form of contrapositive reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاقتران الخامس من كليّة موجبة حَمْلية وكلية سالبة شرطية، مقدمها كلي موجب، كقولنا: (كل "ب" "ج") وليس البتة كل ما كان كل "ب" "أ" فـ "د" "ر") ونتيجته كليّة سالبة متصلة، مقدمها كلي موجب تأليفه من محمول الحَمْلية ومحمول مقدم الشرطية، وتاليها تالي الشرطية، وهي ليس) البتة كل ما كان كل "ج" "أ" فـ "د" "ر" لأنه يكون إذ ذاك (كل "ب" "أ").
The fifth conjunction: from a universal affirmative categorical and a universal negative conditional, its antecedent is universal affirmative, like our saying: 'Every B is C' and 'It is not at all that every whatever is every B is A is D is R', and its result is a universal negative conditional, its antecedent is universal affirmative composed from the predicate of the categorical and the predicate of the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the consequent of the conditional, and it is 'It is not at all that every whatever is every C is A is D is R' because then it is (every B is A).
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This fifth conjunction changes the overall conditional premise to universal negative. The categorical is universal affirmative 'Every B is C', and the conditional is a universal negative: 'It is not the case that whenever every B is A then D is R'. The conclusion is a universal negative conditional: 'It is not the case that whenever every C is A then D is R'. The author's reasoning: because if the conclusion were false (i.e., if every C is A implies D is R were true), then since every B is C, it would imply that every B is A (since all B are C, and all C are A would make all B A), leading to a contradiction with the negative premise. This is a form of contrapositive reasoning.

Key Terms
كلية سالبة شرطية

Key Takeaway
The fifth conjunction shows how a universal affirmative categorical and a universal negative conditional combine to yield a universal negative conditional conclusion, with the antecedent composed from the predicates of the categorical and the conditional's antecedent.

The Syllogism
advanced
15 min
Parsing Mixed Syllogisms of the First Figure (Iqtiranat)
Author enumerates twelve mixed syllogisms combining a universal affirmative categorical with a conditional premise, producing conditional conclusions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to parse any mixed syllogism of the first figure by identifying the categorical premise, the conditional premise's antecedent quantifier and quality, and deriving the correct conclusion.

Core Idea
In mixed syllogisms, the conclusion's antecedent transforms the categorical predicate through the conditional's antecedent.

This passage systematically enumerates the valid moods of mixed syllogisms in the first figure. Each syllogism combines a universal affirmative categorical premise with a conditional premise that varies in quantity, quality, and the quantifier of its antecedent. By following the pattern, you will be able to derive the conclusion's antecedent and verify validity. We will focus on the reasoning behind the quantifier shift from the conditional's antecedent to the conclusion's antecedent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the categorical premise
The categorical premise is always 'Every B is J' (كل ب ج). This universal affirmative premise links the two terms B and J.
2
Parse the conditional premise's antecedent
Note the quantifier (كل, بعض, لا شيء) and quality (affirmative or negative) of the antecedent (مقدم). Also note whether the conditional itself is universal or particular affirmative or negative.
The antecedent's quantifier may be universal or particular.
3
Construct the conclusion's antecedent
The conclusion's antecedent is composed from the categorical predicate (J) and the predicate of the conditional's antecedent (A). The quantifier becomes universal: if the conditional's antecedent is affirmative, use كل; if negative, use لا شيء. The quality follows the antecedent's quality.
The shift from particular to universal occurs because of the universal categorical link.
4
Determine the conclusion's conditional quality
The conclusion's conditional quality (affirmative or negative) matches the conditional premise's quality. The consequent remains the same (D is R).
5
Apply the pattern to any iqtiran
Using the rules, write out the symbolic form of the conclusion for a given combination of premises. Practice with each iqtiran in the passage.
Memorisation of the twelve moods is essential for quick identification in argument analysis.

Continuation of the fourth article: On defining the forms of conditional arguments. The third conjunction: from two universal affirmatives, and the antecedent in the conditional is particular affirmative, as we say: (Every B is J) and (Whenever some B is A, then D is R). Its conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional, its antecedent is universal affirmative composed from the predicate of the categorical and the predicate of the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the consequent of the conditional, which is (Whenever every J is A, then D is R) because some B is within J, and then there is some B. The fourth conjunction: from two universal affirmatives, and the antecedent in the conditional is particular negative, as we say: (Every B is J) and (Whenever some B is not A, then D is R). Its conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional, its antecedent is universal negative composed from the predicate of the categorical and the predicate of the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the consequent of the conditional, which is (Whenever nothing of J is A, then D is R) because some B is within J and then there is some B that is not A. The fifth conjunction: from a universal affirmative categorical and a universal negative conditional, its antecedent is universal affirmative, as we say: (Every B is J) and (It is not the case that whenever every B is A, then D is R). Its conclusion is a universal negative conditional, its antecedent is universal affirmative composed from the predicate of the categorical and the predicate of the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the consequent of the conditional, which is (It is not the case that whenever every J is A, then D is R) because then every B would be A. The sixth conjunction: from a universal affirmative categorical and a universal negative conditional, its antecedent is universal negative, as we say: (Every B is J) and (It is not the case that whenever nothing of B is A, then D is R). Its conclusion is a universal negative conditional, its antecedent is universal negative composed from the predicate of the categorical and the predicate of the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the consequent of the conditional, which is (It is not the case that whenever nothing of J is A, then D is R) because then nothing of B would be A. The seventh conjunction: from a universal affirmative categorical and a universal negative conditional, its antecedent is particular affirmative, as we say: (Every B is J) and (It is not the case that whenever some B is A, then D is R). Its conclusion is a universal negative conditional, its antecedent is universal affirmative composed from the predicate of the categorical and the predicate of the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the consequent of the conditional, which is (It is not the case that whenever every J is A, then D is R) because some B is A. The eighth conjunction: from a universal affirmative categorical and a universal negative conditional, and the antecedent of the conditional is particular negative, as we say: (Every B is J) and (It is not the case that whenever some B is not A, then D is R). Its conclusion is a universal negative conditional, its antecedent is universal negative composed from the predicate of the categorical and the predicate of the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the consequent of the conditional, which is (It is not the case that whenever nothing of J is A, then D is R) because not every B is A. The ninth conjunction: from a universal affirmative categorical and a particular affirmative conditional, its antecedent is universal affirmative, as we say: (Every B is J) and (It may be that when every B is A, then D is R). Its conclusion is a particular affirmative conditional, its antecedent is universal affirmative composed from the predicate of the categorical and the predicate of the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the consequent of the conditional, which is (It may be that when every J is A, then D is R) because every B is A. The tenth conjunction: from a universal affirmative categorical and a particular affirmative conditional, its antecedent is universal negative, as we say: (Every B is J) and (It may be that when nothing of B is A, then D is R). Its conclusion is a particular affirmative conditional, its antecedent is universal negative composed from the predicate of the categorical and the predicate of the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the consequent of the conditional, which is (It may be that when nothing of J is A, then D is R) because nothing of B is A. The eleventh conjunction: from a universal affirmative categorical and a particular affirmative conditional, its antecedent is particular affirmative, as we say: (Every B is J) and (It may be that when some B is A, then D is R). Its conclusion is a particular affirmative conditional, its antecedent is universal affirmative and its consequent is the consequent of the conditional, which is (It may be that when every J is A, then D is R) because some B is A. The twelfth conjunction: from a universal affirmative categorical and a particular affirmative conditional, its antecedent is particular negative, as we say: (Every B is J) and (It may be that when some B is not A, then D is R). Its conclusion is a particular affirmative conditional, its antecedent is universal negative and its consequent is the consequent of the conditional, which is (It may be that when nothing of J is A, then D is R) because not every B is A.
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage enumerates twelve mixed syllogisms of the first figure, combining a universal affirmative categorical premise (Every B is J) with a conditional premise that varies in quantity and quality. Each syllogism's conclusion is a conditional proposition whose antecedent is derived from the categorical predicate (J) and the antecedent of the conditional premise, and whose consequent remains the same. The author demonstrates how the logical relation between B and J (the minor premise) and the conditional statement about B, A, D, and R yields a new conditional about J, A, D, and R. The first four conjunctions (third to sixth? Actually the text starts at third) involve universal conditional premises: third and fourth are positive with particular antecedents (affirmative and negative), fifth and sixth are negative with universal antecedents (affirmative and negative). The seventh and eighth are negative with particular antecedents. The ninth through twelfth involve particular conditional premises ('it may be that...'), with each antecedent quantifier. The reasoning relies on the fact that since every B is J, any statement about B implies a corresponding statement about J, but careful attention to quantifier shifts is needed. For example, in the third conjunction, 'Whenever some B is A, then D is R' together with 'Every B is J' yields 'Whenever every J is A, then D is R' because some B is inside J, so if some B is A, then J's being all A would include that B, but more precisely the conclusion must be universal. The justification given is that because some B is within J, and that some B is A, then if all J were A, that B would be A, but the reasoning appears to assume that the condition 'some B is A' is equivalent to the universality? Actually the text states: 'because some B is within J, and then there is some B'—this seems elliptical. In fact, the conclusion 'Whenever every J is A, then D is R' follows from the premises because if every J is A, then since every B is J, every B is A, so the antecedent 'some B is A' is true, hence D is R. The author is teaching the valid moods of mixed syllogisms in the first figure. Students must parse the symbolic notation and understand how the quantifiers interact. This passage is a quintessential example of Islamic logical formalism, using letters as term variables in the manner of the Peripatetic tradition. It should be memorized and practiced thoroughly.

Introduction to Conjunctive Syllogisms
Heading introducing the forms of conjunctive syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the subject matter of the first art of logic: conjunctive syllogisms, and explain their role in Islamic reasoning.

Core Idea
Conjunctive syllogisms are the backbone of categorical logic in the Islamic tradition.

This section heading marks the transition into a detailed study of conjunctive syllogisms (al-qiyasat al-iqtiraniyya). These are the standard categorical syllogisms that form the core of Aristotelian logic as adopted by Muslim logicians. Understanding their forms is essential for constructing valid arguments in kalam and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the section as a heading
The text reads 'تتمة الفن الأول : في تعريف صور القياسات الاقترانية' – a title that tells you the following content will define the forms of conjunctive syllogisms.
This is a structural marker in the text, not a logical argument itself.
2
Define the key term
Conjunctive syllogisms (القياسات الاقترانية) are syllogisms composed of two categorical (حملي) premises. They are sometimes called 'connected' because the middle term links the two premises.
The term 'iqtirani' contrasts with 'istithna'i' (conditional/disjunctive).
3
Understand the purpose of defining forms
The upcoming sections will enumerate the valid moods (ضروب) for each of the four figures (أشكال). This classification ensures that syllogistic arguments can be tested for validity.
This is a memorization anchor: you need to learn the moods by heart.

Continuation of the first art: on defining the forms of conjunctive syllogisms.
Source pages p. 328

Commentary
This is a section heading marking the continuation of the first art (al-fann al-awwal) of logic, which deals with defining the forms of conjunctive syllogisms (qiyasat iqtiraniyya). The text now proceeds to enumerate the various moods (iqtiranat) of mixed categorical and conditional premises.

The Seventeenth Iqtiran: Compound Syllogism with a Particular Affirmative Categorical Premise
Begins definition of seventeenth mood but cuts off; introduces premises only.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the components of a compound syllogism combining a particular affirmative categorical premise with a universal affirmative conditional premise, and predict the missing conclusion based on the pattern of previous moods.

Core Idea
A compound syllogism mixes categorical and conditional premises; its conclusion depends on the antecedent-consequent relationship.

This lesson introduces the seventeenth iqtiran (compound syllogism) from the Daqaiq al-Haqaiq. The text begins to define this mood by stating its two premises: a particular affirmative categorical proposition and a universal affirmative conditional proposition. Unfortunately, the manuscript cuts off before listing the antecedent of the conditional and the conclusion. However, by understanding the structure of such mixed syllogisms, we can infer how the conclusion is derived. This skill is essential for evaluating complex arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the categorical premise
Look for the premise described as 'جزئية موجبة حملية' (particular affirmative categorical). This is a proposition like 'Some B is J' (some individuals of a type have a property).
Remember that 'جزئية' means particular (not universal), and 'موجبة' means affirmative (not negative).
2
Identify the conditional premise
The second premise is 'كلية موجبة شرطية' (universal affirmative conditional). This is a conditional statement with a universal quantifier, e.g., 'If A is B then C is D' (for all cases).
The conditional has an antecedent (مقدم) and consequent (تال). The text cuts off after 'مقدمها' (its antecedent).
3
Hypothesize the missing conclusion
Based on the pattern of earlier moods in this text, the conclusion likely combines the subject of the categorical premise with the consequent of the conditional or modifies the antecedent. For instance, if the categorical is 'Some B is J' and the conditional is 'If A is B then C is D', a plausible conclusion is 'Some C is D' or 'Some A is C'.
Since the text is incomplete, this is a pedagogical hypothesis. The exact conclusion requires the complete source.
4
Recognize the compound nature
This syllogism is called 'iqtiran' (conjunction) because it mixes two different types of propositions: categorical and conditional. The validity depends on how the terms in the categorical relate to the antecedent/consequent of the conditional.
In the complete works, each iqtiran has specific validity conditions.

The seventeenth conjunction: from a particular affirmative categorical and a universal affirmative conditional, its antecedent...
Source pages p. 329

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of the definition of the seventeenth mood. It states that it is composed of a particular affirmative categorical premise and a universal affirmative conditional premise. The text then cuts off before specifying the antecedent of the conditional and the resulting conclusion. The pattern from previous moods suggests that the conclusion would involve a transformation of the antecedent's subject.

Mixed Syllogisms 13–16: Universal Categorical with Particular Negative Conditional
Author details four mixed syllogisms combining universal categorical with particular negative conditional.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive the conclusion for any of the mixed syllogisms (13th–16th) given the universal affirmative categorical premise and the particular negative conditional premise.

Core Idea
The conclusion's antecedent joins the categorical's predicate with the conditional's antecedent predicate.

Mixed syllogisms combine a categorical and a conditional premise. In these four conjunctions, the categorical is always 'Every B is C' and the conditional is a particular negative 'Not every instance when (pattern involving B and A) leads to (D is R)'. The conclusion is always a particular negative conditional with antecedent formed from C and A. Understanding this pattern is key to such arguments in Islamic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the fixed categorical premise
In all four conjunctions, the categorical is 'Every B is C' (universal affirmative). Recognize that B is the subject, C is the predicate.
This premise remains unchanged across the 13th to 16th conjunctions.
2
Identify the conditional premise's antecedent mood
The conditional premise is 'Not every instance when (Q) leads to (D is R)' where Q varies: universal affirmative (every B is A), universal negative (no B is A), particular affirmative (some B is A), or particular negative (some B is not A). Determine the quantity and quality of Q.
The mood of Q determines the mood of the conclusion's antecedent.
3
Form the conclusion's antecedent
The conclusion's antecedent is formed by replacing B with C in Q. So if Q is 'every B is A', conclusion's antecedent is 'every C is A' (universal affirmative). If Q is 'no B is A', antecedent is 'no C is A' (universal negative), etc.
This is the key inference: the categorical allows substitution of the middle term (B) with the predicate (C) in the conditional's antecedent.
4
Append the consequent and negate
The conclusion's consequent remains the same as the conditional's consequent: 'that D is R'. The conclusion as a whole is a particular negative conditional: 'Not every instance when (antecedent) is such that (consequent)'.
The negation 'Not every' carries over from the original conditional premise.
5
Verify by contradiction (optional check)
Assume the negation of the conclusion (i.e., 'Every instance when antecedent then consequent'). Combine with categorical to derive a contradiction with the original conditional. This confirms validity.
The proof hinges on the fact that if the conclusion were false, then the conditional's antecedent Q would hold for all B, contradicting the particular negative conditional.

The thirteenth conjunction: from a universal affirmative categorical and a particular negative conditional, whose antecedent is universal affirmative, like our saying: 'Every B is C' and 'Not every instance when every B is A is such that D is R'. Its conclusion is a particular negative conditional, whose antecedent is universal affirmative composed of the predicate of the categorical and the predicate of the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the consequent of the conditional, namely 'Not every instance when every C is A is such that D is R' because then it would be the case that 'Every B is A'. The fourteenth conjunction: from a universal affirmative categorical and a particular negative conditional, whose antecedent is universal negative, like our saying: 'Every B is C' and 'Not every instance when no B is A is such that D is R'. Its conclusion is a particular negative conditional, whose antecedent is universal negative composed of the predicate of the categorical and the predicate of the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the consequent of the conditional, namely 'Not every instance when no C is A is such that D is R' because then it would be the case that 'No B is A'. The fifteenth conjunction: from a universal affirmative categorical and a particular negative conditional, whose antecedent is particular affirmative, like our saying: 'Every B is C' and 'Not if some B is A then D is R'. Its conclusion is a particular negative conditional, whose antecedent is universal affirmative composed of the predicate of the categorical and the predicate of the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the consequent of the conditional, namely 'Not every instance when every C is A is such that D is R' because then it would be the case that 'Some B is A'. Commentary
This passage defines four specific mixed syllogisms (اقترانات) that combine a universal affirmative categorical premise with a particular negative conditional premise. The author enumerates them as the 13th through 16th conjunctions. For each, the categorical premise is fixed as 'Every B is C', while the conditional varies in the mood of its antecedent (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative). The conclusion is always a particular negative conditional, whose antecedent is formed by taking the predicate of the categorical (C) and the predicate of the antecedent of the conditional (A), and whose consequent is the same as the conditional's consequent (D is R). The author provides a proof for each: if the conclusion were false, it would entail a contradiction with the premises. For example, in the 13th, asserting 'Every C is A' (the opposite) combined with 'Every B is C' gives 'Every B is A', which contradicts the conditional. This shows the logical necessity of the conclusion. The pattern is consistent: the quantity (universal/particular) of the antecedent of the conclusion matches that of the conditional's antecedent, and the quality (affirmative/negative) matches the conditional's main connective.

Mixed Syllogisms with Particular Categorical Premise
Author generalizes mixed syllogisms where the categorical premise is particular affirmative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a valid mixed syllogism (categorical + conditional) where the categorical premise is particular affirmative, and determine the conclusion's modality from the conditional premise.

Core Idea
A particular categorical premise yields the same conclusion pattern as a universal one in mixed syllogisms.

In the previous lessons, we saw mixed syllogisms where the categorical premise was universal. Now the author extends that to cases where the categorical is particular affirmative. The key insight is that the conclusion's logical structure mirrors the universal case—the proof is identical. However, the modality (necessity, possibility, absoluteness) of the conclusion is determined by the conditional premise alone. This lesson will help you understand this generalization and apply it to arguments in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the categorical premise
In each conjunction, the categorical premise is 'Some B is C' (particular affirmative). Note that the quantity is particular, not universal.
The term 'B' is the middle term shared with the antecedent of the conditional.
2
Identify the conditional premise
The conditional premise varies in quantity and quality (universal affirmative/negative, particular affirmative/negative) and its antecedent can be particular affirmative or negative. For example, in conjunction 17: 'Every instance when some B is A is such that D is R' (universal affirmative conditional with particular affirmative antecedent).
The antecedent always involves the same term 'B' from the categorical.
3
Extract the conclusion pattern
The author states: 'Its conclusion is as if the categorical were universal.' This means that the conclusion is constructed similarly to when the categorical was universal—i.e., the same logical relation holds, but the subject of the conclusion will be particular (since the categorical is particular). For example, if the categorical is 'Some B is C' and the conditional is universal affirmative, the conclusion would be 'Some C is D' (or similar, depending on the specific form).
The proof is the same as previously given for universal categorical cases.
4
Determine modality and absoluteness
The modality (e.g., necessity, possibility) and absoluteness (unqualified) of the conclusion follow the conditional premise, not the categorical. If the conditional is necessary, the conclusion is necessary; if it is possible, the conclusion is possible.
This is a general rule applicable to all such mixed syllogisms.
5
Check for fourth figure cases
The passage ends by mentioning if the common term is in the pattern of the fourth figure (middle term predicate in first premise, subject in second). This would require separate treatment, but the text cuts off. In standard logic, the fourth figure has different valid moods.
For this lesson, focus on the standard first-figure-like arrangement where the middle term is subject of the categorical and subject of the conditional's antecedent.

The key insight is that when the categorical is particular, the conclusion remains particular but its construction mirrors the universal case; the proof relies on the same logical reduction.

Seven Forms of Mixed Categorical-Hypothetical Syllogisms
Lists seven patterns of mixed syllogisms with categorical and hypothetical premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structure of each of the seven mixed syllogistic patterns and state how the conclusion's modality follows the conditional premise.

Core Idea
In mixed syllogisms, the conclusion's certainty always follows the conditional, not the categorical premise.

In Islamic logic, mixed syllogisms combine a categorical premise (حمْلية) with a conditional premise (شرطية). The author lists seven valid patterns where the categorical is always particular affirmative, but the conditional varies in quantity and quality, and its antecedent may be affirmative or negative. Understanding these patterns is crucial for constructing sound arguments in fields like usul al-fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the categorical premise
In every pattern, the categorical premise is a particular affirmative (جزئية موجبة حمْلية), e.g., 'Some B is C'.
The categorical is always particular affirmative in these patterns.
2
Identify the conditional premise
Note its quantity (universal or particular) and quality (affirmative or negative). Also note the quality of its antecedent (مقدم): e.g., 'if some B is A' (affirmative) or 'if some B is not A' (negative).
The antecedent's quality affects the pattern.
3
Check the pattern index
Each pattern (18-24) is defined by a specific combination. For example, pattern 18 has a universal affirmative conditional with a particular negative antecedent; pattern 19 has a universal negative conditional with a particular affirmative antecedent.
Memorize the combinations using the variables.
4
State the conclusion
The conclusion is as if the categorical premise were universal, not particular. For pattern 18, the conclusion 'D is R' applies to all cases, not just some.
The categorical's particularity is effectively ignored in the conclusion.
5
Determine the conclusion's modality
The conclusion's modality (absolute or modal) follows the conditional premise, not the categorical. If the conditional is universal and absolute, the conclusion is universal and absolute; if the conditional is particular and modal, the conclusion follows that mode.
This is a key rule: the conditional governs the conclusion's certainty.

The eighteenth conjunction: from a particular affirmative categorical and a universal affirmative conditional whose antecedent is a particular negative, as in our saying: (Some B is C) and (Always, whenever some B is not A, then D is R); its conclusion is as if the categorical were also universal. The nineteenth conjunction: from a particular affirmative categorical and a universal negative conditional whose antecedent is a particular affirmative, as in our saying: (Some B is C) and (It is never the case that if some B is A then D is R). The twentieth conjunction: from a particular affirmative categorical and a universal negative conditional whose antecedent is a particular negative, as in our saying: (Some B is C) and (It is never the case that if some B is not A then D is R). The twenty-first conjunction: from a particular affirmative categorical and a particular conditional whose antecedent is a particular affirmative, as in our saying: (Some B is C) and (It may be that if some B is A then D is R). The twenty-second conjunction: from a particular affirmative categorical and a particular affirmative conditional whose antecedent is a particular negative, as in our saying: (Some B is C) and (It may be that if some B is not A then D is R). The twenty-fourth conjunction: from a particular affirmative categorical and a particular negative conditional whose antecedent is a particular negative, as in our saying: (Some B is C) and (Not every time some B is not A then D is R). Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of mixed categorical-hypothetical syllogisms (iqti-ranat). The author lists seven patterns (18–24) combining a particular affirmative categorical premise (جزئية موجبة حمْلية) with various conditional premises (شرطية). Each pattern is defined by the quality (positive/negative) and quantity (universal/particular) of the conditional, and the quality of its antecedent. For each, an example is given using logical variables (ب، ج، أ, د، ر). The conclusion (نتيجة) of all these patterns is stated to be as if the categorical were universal (كليّة), meaning the categorical premise behaves like a universal in the conclusion. The author also notes that in terms of absolute (إطلاق) and modal (جهة) judgments, the conclusion follows the conditional premise, not the categorical. This is a memorization-anchor for students to recognize these valid mixed syllogism forms.

Conditions for Fourth-Figure Mixed Syllogisms
Explains the three conditions for valid fourth-figure mixed syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three conditions for validity of a fourth-figure mixed syllogism and verify whether a given syllogism satisfies them using categorical reasoning.

Core Idea
Fourth-figure mixed syllogisms require specific restrictions on the antecedent's quantity and the categorical's quality.

This passage introduces three conditions that must be met for a mixed syllogism in the fourth figure to be valid. The author then justifies the first condition through a detailed categorical argument. Understanding these conditions is essential for constructing sound mixed syllogisms in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three conditions
Read the beginning of the passage: the first condition is that the antecedent must not be universal affirmative; the second: if the antecedent is negative, the categorical must be universal affirmative; the third: if the antecedent is particular affirmative, the categorical must be affirmative regardless of quantity.
2
Understand the first condition's rationale
The author argues that if the antecedent is universal affirmative ('every A is B'), then even if the categorical is universal affirmative ('every B is C'), the relation 'A is C' does not follow necessarily because A could be more general than C. Similarly if the categorical is negative, the condition fails.
Follow the author's use of variables: A (major), B (middle), C (minor).
3
Analyze the second condition's justification
If the antecedent is negative (e.g., 'no A is B' or 'some A is not B'), and the categorical is universal affirmative ('every B is C'), then combining with 'no C is A' yields the required condition: 'no A is B' and not 'every A is B'. This ensures necessity.
The passage partially justifies this condition.
4
Note the third condition
The third condition is stated but not fully justified in the excerpt. It requires the categorical to be affirmative if the antecedent is particular affirmative.
The text cuts off before completing the justification.

وأما إن كان الاشتراك بين الحَمْلية والمقدم من الشرطية على نمط الشكل الرابع فله شروط وخواص، أما شروطه فإن لا يكون المقدم من الشرطية كلياً موجباً، وأن تكون الحَمْلية كليّة موجبة إن كان المقدم من الشرطية سالباً، وعلى أي حال كان من الكم، وأن تكون الحَمْلية موجبة على أي حال كانت من الكم إن كان المقدم جزئياً موجباً. أما الشرط الأول: فلأنه إذا كان شرط لزوم التالي أن يكون (كل "أ" "ب") لم يتحقق شرط لزوم التالي من نسبة الأكبر إلى الأصغر عند حذف الأوسط على أي حالة كانت الحَمْلية من الكم والكيف، ولنبيّن ذلك إذا كانت الحَمْلية كلية موجبة وسالبة وأنت تعرفه في الجزئية – أما إذا كانت الحملية كلية موجبة، كقولنا: (كل "ب" "ج") فـ "ب" وإن كان داخلاً تحت "ج" فنسبة "أ" إلى كل "ج" مما لا يحقق شرط لزوم التالي؛ لجواز أن يكون أعم من "ج" ومن "ب". وأما إذا كانت سالبة أي "ج" مسلوباً فيها عن كل "ب" فنسبة "أ" إلى كل "ج" أو بعض "ج" كان بالإيجاب أو السلب، مما لا يتحقق معه شرط لزوم التالي، أما إذا كان بالإيجاب فلما فيه من امتناع تحقق الشرط حيث كان بعض "أ" متصفاً بـ "ج" الذي ليس "ب". وأما إذا كان بالسلب فلعدم امتناع سلب "أ" عن "ب" وعن "ج". وأما الشرط الثاني: فلأنه إذا كان المقدم سالباً و"ب" فيه مسلوب عن كل "أ" أو عن بعض "أ" فإذا كانت الحَمْلية كليّاً موجبة فقد تحقق ما هو شرط لزوم التالي من جهة أن كل "ب" داخل تحت "ج" فإذا كان لا شيء من "ج" "أ") فـ (لا شيء من "أ" "ج") وكل "ب" داخل تحت "ج" ولا شيء من "أ" "ب") وليس كل ("أ" "ب") وهو شرط اللزوم، ولو كانت الحَمْلية جزئية موجبة أو سالبة لم يتحقق الشرط. أما إذا كانت موجبة أي "ج" فيها ثابت لبعض "ب" ونسبة "أ" إلى "ج" عند حذف الحد الأوسط إما بالإيجاب أو بالسلب فإن كان بالإيجاب فلما فيه من امتناع تحقق شرط لزوم التالي، إن كان الشرط كلياً سالباً، ولما فيه من عدم لزوم الشرط إن كان الشرط جزئياً سالباً. وأما إن كانت النسبة أي نسبة "أ" إلى "ج" بالسلب فلما فيه من عدم لزوم
And if the sharing between the categorical and the antecedent of the conditional is on the pattern of the fourth figure, then it has conditions and properties. As for its conditions: first, that the antecedent of the conditional not be universal affirmative; second, that the categorical be universal affirmative if the antecedent of the conditional is negative, regardless of its quantity; third, that the categorical be affirmative regardless of its quantity if the antecedent is particular affirmative. As for the first condition: it is because if the condition for the necessity of the consequent is that (every A is B), then the condition for the necessity of the consequent is not fulfilled from the relation of the major to the minor when the middle is removed, regardless of the quantity and quality of the categorical. And we will show that if the categorical is universal affirmative and negative, and you know it in the particular case. As for when the categorical is universal affirmative, as in our saying: (Every B is C), then B, although it is included under C, the relation of A to every C does not fulfill the condition for the necessity of the consequent; because it is possible that it is more general than C and B. And if it is negative, i.e., C is negated in relation to every B, then the relation of A to every C or some C, whether by affirmation or negation, does not fulfill with it the condition for the necessity of the consequent. If by affirmation, then because of the impossibility of fulfilling the condition given that some A is characterized by C which is not B. And if by negation, then because there is no impossibility of negating A from B and from C. As for the second condition: it is because if the antecedent is negative and B in it is negated from every A or from some A, then if the categorical is universal affirmative, what is the condition for the necessity of the consequent is fulfilled in that every B is included under C. So if (no C is A), then (no A is C) and every B is included under C, and (no A is B), and it is not (every A is B), which is the condition of necessity. And if the categorical were particular affirmative or negative, the condition would not be fulfilled. As for if it is affirmative, i.e., C is affirmed of some B, and the relation of A to C when the middle term is removed, either by affirmation or negation, then if by affirmation, because of the impossibility of fulfilling the condition for the necessity of the consequent if the condition is universal negative, and because of the non-necessity of the condition if the condition is particular negative. And if the relation, i.e., the relation of A to C, is by negation, then because of the non-necessity of (the text cuts off).

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the conditions required for a valid mixed syllogism when the sharing between the categorical premise and the antecedent of the conditional follows the pattern of the fourth figure (الشكل الرابع). The author states three conditions: (1) the antecedent of the conditional must not be a universal affirmative; (2) if the antecedent is negative, the categorical must be universal affirmative; (3) if the antecedent is particular affirmative, the categorical must be affirmative regardless of quantity. The author then justifies the first condition through a detailed argument using categorical syllogistic reasoning. He shows that if the condition for the necessity of the consequent is that every A is B, then having a universal affirmative categorical (every B is C) does not guarantee the relation of A to C because A could be more general than C. Similarly, a universal negative categorical (no B is C) fails because the relation of A to C may be affirmative or negative but does not ensure the necessary condition. For the second condition, if the antecedent is negative and B is negated from A, then a universal affirmative categorical (every B is C) combined with 'no C is A' yields the required condition that no A is B and not every A is B, which is the condition of necessity. The text ends abruptly, likely continuing with further justification. This is a conceptual probe into the validity conditions for fourth-figure mixed syllogisms.

The Syllogism
intermediate

The Three Conditions of the Mixed Conditional-Categorical Syllogism
Author lists three conditions for the mixed syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a given mixed conditional-categorical syllogism satisfies the three conditions for validity.

Core Idea
Three precise conditions govern the validity of a syllogism mixing a conditional and a categorical premise.

In this passage, the author lays down the fundamental conditions that a mixed syllogism must meet. These conditions involve the quantity and quality of the antecedent (muqaddam) of the conditional and the categorical premise (hamliyya). Understanding them is essential for assessing the soundness of arguments that couple a conditional statement with a categorical one, a common structure in Islamic legal and theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Extract the three conditions
From the text, list the three conditions: (1) The antecedent of the conditional must not be universal affirmative. (2) If the antecedent is negative, the categorical must be universal affirmative. (3) If the antecedent is particular affirmative, the categorical must be affirmative (regardless of quantity).
These conditions are stated at the beginning of the Arabic passage.
2
Justify condition 1
The author argues that if the antecedent were universal affirmative (e.g., every A is B), then even with a universal affirmative categorical (every B is C), the condition for necessity fails because A might be more general than C and B, so the link between A and C may not hold.
The text uses the case where the categorical is universal affirmative and shows the impossibility of realizing the condition.
3
Justify condition 2
If the antecedent is negative (e.g., no A is B or some A is not B), the categorical must be universal affirmative to ensure that every B is inside C. Then from 'no C is A' we get 'no A is C', and since every B is C, we deduce 'no A is B', which is the condition for necessity.
The author shows that if the categorical were not universal affirmative, the condition would not be realized.
4
Justify condition 3
If the antecedent is particular affirmative (some A is B), the categorical must be affirmative (either universal or particular). This ensures that some part of B falls under C, and from 'every C is A' we get 'some A is B', satisfying the necessity condition.
The text explains that if the categorical were negative, the condition would not be realized.
5
Test with an Islamic example
Take the argument: 'Every intoxicant (B) is haram (C). If a drink (A) is not intoxicant, then it is permissible (D R).' Check condition 2: antecedent is negative, categorical is universal affirmative. Condition satisfied, so the syllogism is potentially valid.
Replace A, B, C, D, R with Islamic terms to see the conditions in action.

من كليتين موجبتين، والمقدم من الشرطية جزئي موجب. كقولنا: (كل "ب" "ج") و(دائماً كل ما كان بعض "أ" "ب" فـ "د" "ر") ونتيجته كلية موجبة متصلة، مقدمها كليّ موجب تأليفه من محمول الحَمْلية وموضوع مقدم الشرطية، وتاليها تالي الشرطية، وهي دائماً كل ما كان كل "ج" "أ" فـ "د" "ر") فـ (كل) "ب" "أ") لضرورة دخول كل "ب" تحت "ج" وإذا كان كل "ب" "أ") فـ (بعض "أ" ب) وهو شرط اللزوم.
It has conditions and properties. As for its conditions: that the antecedent of the conditional not be universal affirmative, and that the categorical be universal affirmative if the antecedent of the conditional is negative, regardless of its quantity, and that the categorical be affirmative regardless of its quantity if the antecedent is particular affirmative. As for the first condition: because if the condition for the necessity of the consequent is that (every A is B), the condition for the necessity of the consequent from the relation of the major to the minor upon eliminating the middle is not realized regardless of the quantity and quality of the categorical. We will clarify that if the categorical is universal affirmative and negative, and you know it in the particular. If the categorical is universal affirmative, like our saying: (every B is C), then B, even though it falls under C, the relation of A to every C does not realize the condition for the necessity of the consequent because it is possible that A is more general than C and than B. As for if it is negative, i.e., C is negated of every B, then the relation of A to every C or some C, whether by affirmation or negation, does not realize with it the condition for the necessity of the consequent. As for if it is by affirmation, because of the impossibility of realizing the condition since some A is qualified by C which is not B. And as for if it is by negation, because of the non-impossibility of negating A from B and from C. As for the second condition: because if the antecedent is negative and B in it is negated of every A or of some A, then if the categorical is universal affirmative, what is the condition for the necessity of the consequent is realized from the aspect that every B falls under C. So if no C is A, then no A is C, and every B falls under C, and no A is B, and not every A is B, which is the condition of necessity. And if the categorical were particular affirmative or negative, the condition would not be realized. As for if it is affirmative, i.e., C is affirmed of some B, and the relation of A to C upon eliminating the middle term is either by affirmation or negation. If it is by affirmation, because of the impossibility of realizing the condition for the necessity of the consequent if the condition is universal negative, and because of the lack of necessity of the condition if the condition is particular negative. And as for if the relation, i.e., the relation of A to C, is by negation, then because of the lack of necessity of the condition, for even if A is negated of some B which is C, it does not necessarily follow that the other part of B is negated of it. And as for if the categorical is particular negative, then because of the lack of necessity of the condition from the relation of A to C whether negation or affirmation. And upon this the impossibility of necessity becomes clear when the categorical is also universal negative. As for the third condition: because if the antecedent is particular, i.e., B is affirmed of some A, then if (every B is C) or (some B is C), then if (every C is A), then (some A is B), which is the condition of necessity. And if the categorical were negative, this condition would not be realized. As for its properties: the possibility of the conclusion being universal even if the antecedent of the conditional and the categorical are particular, and vice versa, and that the antecedent of the conclusion is only universal, whether the categorical and the antecedent of the conditional are both universal or particular, or one is universal and the other particular. As for its productive pairings, they are sixteen pairings: The first pairing is from two universal affirmatives, and the antecedent of the conditional is universal negative, like our saying: (every B is C) and (everything of which no A is B, then D is R). Its conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional, its antecedent is universal negative composed of the predicate of the categorical and the subject of the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the consequent of the conditional, and it is always: everything of which no C is A, then D is R. Because if no C is A, then no A is C, and every B falls under C, so no A is B, which is the condition of necessity. The second pairing is from two universal affirmatives, and the antecedent of the conditional is particular affirmative, like our saying: (every B is C) and (always: everything of which some A is B, then D is R). Its conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional, its antecedent is universal affirmative composed of the predicate of the categorical and the subject of the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the consequent of the conditional, and it is always: everything of which every C is A, then D is R, so every B is A, due to the necessity of every B falling under C, and if every B is A, then some A is B, which is the condition of necessity.
Source pages p. 331

Commentary
This passage defines the conditions (shurut) and properties (khawass) of a mixed syllogism combining a conditional premise (shartiyya) and a categorical premise (hamliyya), then gives the first two of sixteen productive pairings (qara'in). The author lists three conditions: (1) The antecedent of the conditional must not be universal affirmative; (2) If the antecedent is negative, the categorical must be universal affirmative; (3) If the antecedent is particular affirmative, the categorical must be affirmative regardless of quantity. Each condition is justified by considering the logical relationships between the terms. For example, the first condition is explained by showing that if the categorical were universal affirmative, the relation of the major to the middle might not guarantee the necessity. The properties include that the conclusion can be universal even if both premises are particular, and that the antecedent of the conclusion is always universal. Then the first two pairings are given: In the first, the categorical is 'every B is C' and the conditional's antecedent is 'no A is B'; the conclusion is a conditional with antecedent 'no C is A' and the same consequent. In the second, the categorical is 'every B is C' and the conditional's antecedent is 'some A is B'; the conclusion has antecedent 'every C is A'. These examples use abstract variables (A, B, C, D, R) but can be understood with Islamic examples: e.g., for the first, if 'every intoxicant is unlawful' and 'if no wine is intoxicant then it is permissible', we conclude 'if no unlawful is intoxicant then it is permissible'. The conditions ensure the middle term (B) connects correctly.

Key Terms
المقدم (al-muqaddam): The antecedent of the conditional proposition in the mixed syllogism. [Note: No significant shift.]
الشرطية (al-shartiyya): A conditional proposition (if-then) forming one premise of the mixed syllogism. [Note: In some texts, shartiyya may also refer to disjunctive conditionals, but here it is clearly conjunctive (muttasila).]
الحملية (al-hamliyya): A categorical (subject-predicate) proposition forming the other premise. [Note: No significant shift.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Particular affirmative
C) Universal affirmative
D) Particular negative

Key Takeaway
A mixed conditional-categorical syllogism is valid only when its premises meet three specific conditions on quantity and quality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the following mixed syllogism: 'Every intoxicant is haram (categorical). If a drink is khamr, then it is intoxicant (conditional). Conclusion: If a drink is khamr, then it is haram.' Check whether the antecedent of the conditional is 'a drink is khamr'. Is it universal affirmative? What condition is violated? Explain.
?
Construct a valid mixed syllogism on a fiqh topic that satisfies all three conditions. Use the pattern: categorical universal affirmative, conditional with negative antecedent. Write out the premises and conclusion using Arabic terms.

The Syllogism
intermediate

Properties and Productive Pairings of the Mixed Syllogism
Author describes properties and first two productive pairings of the mixed syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the conclusion of a mixed syllogism given a productive pairing and list the first two of the sixteen pairings.

Core Idea
The mixed syllogism has two key properties and sixteen productive pairings that yield valid conclusions.

After laying down the conditions, the author turns to the properties (khawass) of the mixed syllogism: the conclusion can be universal even if both premises are particular, and the antecedent of the conclusion is always universal. He then introduces the sixteen productive pairings (qara'in) and illustrates the first two. This lesson focuses on these properties and the first two pairings, which serve as templates for generating valid mixed syllogisms.

Guided Path
1
State the two properties
From the text: (1) The conclusion can be universal even if the antecedent of the conditional and the categorical are particular, and vice versa. (2) The antecedent of the conclusion is always universal, whether the premises are universal or particular.
These properties are stated after the conditions in the Arabic passage.
2
Understand the first pairing
First pairing: categorical premise 'كل ب ج' (every B is C), conditional antecedent 'لا شيء من أ ب' (no A is B), consequent 'د ر' (D is R). Conclusion: conditional with antecedent 'لا شيء من ج أ' (no C is A) and the same consequent. The reasoning: if no C is A, then no A is C; since every B is C, it follows that no A is B (the condition for necessity).
The author uses the Arabic variables: أ (A), ب (B), ج (C), د (D), ر (R).
3
Understand the second pairing
Second pairing: same categorical 'كل ب ج', conditional antecedent 'بعض أ ب' (some A is B), conclusion: 'كل ج أ' (every C is A) as antecedent. Reasoning: if every C is A, then every B is A (since B is inside C), and then some A is B (the condition for necessity).
Both pairings assume the categorical and conditional are universal affirmative in quantity and quality, except the conditional antecedent differs.
4
Apply to Islamic examples
Replace variables: Let ب = intoxicant, ج = haram, أ = wine, د = drink, ر = permissible. For first pairing: categorical: كل مسكر حرام (every intoxicant is haram); conditional: كل ما لا شيء من الخمر مسكر فهو حلال (everything of which no wine is intoxicant is permissible); conclusion: كل ما لا شيء من الحرام خمر فهو حلال (everything of which no haram is wine is permissible).
This example shows how the abstract pairing maps to a real Islamic ruling.

بالسلب فلما فيه من عدم لزوم الشرط، فإنه وإن كان "أ" مسلوباً عن بعض "ب" وهو ما هو "ج" فليس يلزم أن يكون البعض الآخر من "ب" مسلوباً عنه. وأما إذا كانت الحَمْلية جزئية سالبة فلما فيه من عدم لزوم الشرط من (بعض "ب" "ج") فإذا كان ( كل "ج" "أ") فـ (بعض "أ" "ب") وهو شرط اللزوم، ولو كانت الحَمْلية سالبة لم يكن هذا الشرط متحققاً. وأما خواصه، فجواز كون النتيجة كلية وإن كان المقدم من الشرطية والحَمْلية جزئيين وبالعكس، وأن المقدم من النتيجة لا يكون إلا كلياً، وسواء كانت الحملية والمقدم من الشرطية كليين أو جزئيين، أو أن أحدهما كلي والآخر جزئي. وأما قرائنه المنتجة فستة عشر اقتراناً: الاقتران الأول من كليتين موجبتين، والمقدم من الشرطية كلي سالب كقولنا: (كل "ب" "ج") و(كل ما كان لا شيء من "أ" "ب" فـ "د" "ر") ونتيجته كلية موجبة متصلة، مقدمها كلي سالب تأليفه من محمول الحَمْلية وموضوع مقدم الشرطية، وتاليها تالي الشرطية وهي دائماً كل ما كان لا شيء من "ج" "أ" ف "د" "ر"، لأنه إذا كان (لا) شيء من "ج" "أ") فـ (لا شيء من "أ" "ج") وكان كل "ب" داخلاً تحت "ج" ف (لا شيء من "أ" "ب") وهو شرط اللزوم. الاقتران الثاني من كليتين موجبتين، والمقدم من الشرطية جزئي موجب. كقولنا: (كل "ب" "ج") و(دائماً كل ما كان بعض "أ" "ب" فـ "د" "ر") ونتيجته كلية موجبة متصلة، مقدمها كليّ موجب تأليفه من محمول الحَمْلية وموضوع مقدم الشرطية، وتاليها تالي الشرطية، وهي دائماً كل ما كان كل "ج" "أ" فـ "د" "ر") فـ (كل) "ب" "أ") لضرورة دخول كل "ب" تحت "ج" وإذا كان كل "ب" "أ") فـ (بعض "أ" ب) وهو شرط اللزوم.
It has conditions and properties. As for its conditions: that the antecedent of the conditional not be universal affirmative, and that the categorical be universal affirmative if the antecedent of the conditional is negative, regardless of its quantity, and that the categorical be affirmative regardless of its quantity if the antecedent is particular affirmative. As for the first condition: because if the condition for the necessity of the consequent is that (every A is B), the condition for the necessity of the consequent from the relation of the major to the minor upon eliminating the middle is not realized regardless of the quantity and quality of the categorical. We will clarify that if the categorical is universal affirmative and negative, and you know it in the particular. If the categorical is universal affirmative, like our saying: (every B is C), then B, even though it falls under C, the relation of A to every C does not realize the condition for the necessity of the consequent because it is possible that A is more general than C and than B. As for if it is negative, i.e., C is negated of every B, then the relation of A to every C or some C, whether by affirmation or negation, does not realize with it the condition for the necessity of the consequent. As for if it is by affirmation, because of the impossibility of realizing the condition since some A is qualified by C which is not B. And as for if it is by negation, because of the non-impossibility of negating A from B and from C. As for the second condition: because if the antecedent is negative and B in it is negated of every A or of some A, then if the categorical is universal affirmative, what is the condition for the necessity of the consequent is realized from the aspect that every B falls under C. So if no C is A, then no A is C, and every B falls under C, and no A is B, and not every A is B, which is the condition of necessity. And if the categorical were particular affirmative or negative, the condition would not be realized. As for if it is affirmative, i.e., C is affirmed of some B, and the relation of A to C upon eliminating the middle term is either by affirmation or negation. If it is by affirmation, because of the impossibility of realizing the condition for the necessity of the consequent if the condition is universal negative, and because of the lack of necessity of the condition if the condition is particular negative. And as for if the relation, i.e., the relation of A to C, is by negation, then because of the lack of necessity of the condition, for even if A is negated of some B which is C, it does not necessarily follow that the other part of B is negated of it. And as for if the categorical is particular negative, then because of the lack of necessity of the condition from the relation of A to C whether negation or affirmation. And upon this the impossibility of necessity becomes clear when the categorical is also universal negative. As for the third condition: because if the antecedent is particular, i.e., B is affirmed of some A, then if (every B is C) or (some B is C), then if (every C is A), then (some A is B), which is the condition of necessity. And if the categorical were negative, this condition would not be realized. As for its properties: the possibility of the conclusion being universal even if the antecedent of the conditional and the categorical are particular, and vice versa, and that the antecedent of the conclusion is only universal, whether the categorical and the antecedent of the conditional are both universal or particular, or one is universal and the other particular. As for its productive pairings, they are sixteen pairings: The first pairing is from two universal affirmatives, and the antecedent of the conditional is universal negative, like our saying: (every B is C) and (everything of which no A is B, then D is R). Its conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional, its antecedent is universal negative composed of the predicate of the categorical and the subject of the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the consequent of the conditional, and it is always: everything of which no C is A, then D is R. Because if no C is A, then no A is C, and every B falls under C, so no A is B, which is the condition of necessity. The second pairing is from two universal affirmatives, and the antecedent of the conditional is particular affirmative, like our saying: (every B is C) and (always: everything of which some A is B, then D is R). Its conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional, its antecedent is universal affirmative composed of the predicate of the categorical and the subject of the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the consequent of the conditional, and it is always: everything of which every C is A, then D is R, so every B is A, due to the necessity of every B falling under C, and if every B is A, then some A is B, which is the condition of necessity.
Source pages p. 331

Commentary
This passage defines the conditions (shurut) and properties (khawass) of a mixed syllogism combining a conditional premise (shartiyya) and a categorical premise (hamliyya), then gives the first two of sixteen productive pairings (qara'in). The author lists three conditions: (1) The antecedent of the conditional must not be universal affirmative; (2) If the antecedent is negative, the categorical must be universal affirmative; (3) If the antecedent is particular affirmative, the categorical must be affirmative regardless of quantity. Each condition is justified by considering the logical relationships between the terms. For example, the first condition is explained by showing that if the categorical were universal affirmative, the relation of the major to the middle might not guarantee the necessity. The properties include that the conclusion can be universal even if both premises are particular, and that the antecedent of the conclusion is always universal. Then the first two pairings are given: In the first, the categorical is 'every B is C' and the conditional's antecedent is 'no A is B'; the conclusion is a conditional with antecedent 'no C is A' and the same consequent. In the second, the categorical is 'every B is C' and the conditional's antecedent is 'some A is B'; the conclusion has antecedent 'every C is A'. These examples use abstract variables (A, B, C, D, R) but can be understood with Islamic examples: e.g., for the first, if 'every intoxicant is unlawful' and 'if no wine is intoxicant then it is permissible', we conclude 'if no unlawful is intoxicant then it is permissible'. The conditions ensure the middle term (B) connects correctly.

Key Terms
الاقتران (al-iqtiran): A productive pairing of premises that yields a valid conclusion; one of sixteen configurations. [Note: This term is specific to this logical work; not commonly used outside.]
الخواص (al-khawass): Properties of the mixed syllogism, specifically that the conclusion can be universal and its antecedent is always universal. [Note: No significant shift.]
النتيجة (al-natija): The conclusion of the syllogism. [Note: Standard term.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the first productive pairing, if the categorical is 'every B is C' and the conditional antecedent is 'no A is B', what is the antecedent of the conclusion?
A) every C is A
B) no C is A
C) some C is A
D) every A is C

Key Takeaway
The mixed syllogism yields a conditional conclusion whose antecedent is always universal, and there are exactly sixteen valid pairings.
