The Syllogism
advanced
7 min
Conditions for Valid Compound Syllogisms (Categorical + Conditional)
Author states three conditions for validity of compound syllogisms involving a categorical and a conditional premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three conditions for a valid compound syllogism combining a categorical premise and a conditional premise, and apply them to test the validity of a given inference.

Core Idea
Not every combination of categorical and conditional premise yields valid inference; specific conditions govern the implication.

This passage from Daqaiq al-Haqaiq continues the discussion of categorical syllogisms, focusing on compound syllogisms that combine a categorical premise (حملیة) with a conditional premise (شرطیة). The author states three conditions that must be met for such a compound to yield a valid conclusion. Although the first and second conditions are partially lost, the third condition is fully preserved and provides a clear logical rule. Understanding these conditions is essential for constructing valid arguments in Islamic sciences, particularly in kalam and usul al-fiqh, where compound syllogisms are frequently used.

Guided Path
1
Identify the components of the compound syllogism
In the passage, locate the categorical premise (حملیة) and the conditional premise (شرطیة). Note that the conditional has an antecedent (مقدم) and a consequent (تالي). The variables (أ، ب، ج) represent terms: A is the subject of the antecedent, B is the predicate of the antecedent, and C is a middle term.
The categorical premise is typically of the form 'every B is C' or 'some B is C', while the conditional is 'if something is A then it is D' but here D is not used; instead the condition uses C.
2
Extract the three conditions
The passage mentions three conditions. The first (partially preserved) deals with a particular negative categorical premise. The second is missing. The third condition is fully stated: if the antecedent of the conditional is particular (i.e., 'some A is B'), then the categorical premise must be affirmative (either 'every B is C' or 'some B is C') and also 'every C is A' must hold, in order for the implication to produce a valid conclusion.
The third condition essentially ensures that the antecedent of the conditional is affirmed by the categorical chain.
3
Formalize the third condition
Write the third condition using the variables: If (some A is B) [antecedent particular] and (every B is C) or (some B is C) [categorical affirmative] and (every C is A), then (some A is B) is the condition of implication (شرط اللزوم). This shows that the conclusion (some A is B) follows from the premises.
The condition requires that the middle term C links A and B appropriately.
4
Apply to an Islamic example
Consider a compound syllogism: Let أ = 'thing that is forbidden (حرام)', ب = 'date-wine (نبيد)', ج = 'thing that is intoxicating (مسكر)'. Then the condition states: If (some forbidden thing is date-wine) and (every date-wine is intoxicating or some date-wine is intoxicating) and (every intoxicating thing is forbidden), then (some forbidden thing is date-wine) holds as a valid conclusion. This illustrates the logical structure required.
The example shows how the third condition ensures the syllogism is valid.

تتمة الفن الأول: في تعريف صور القياسات الاقترانية
الشرط، فإنه وإن كان "أ" مسلوباً عن بعض "ب" وهو ما هو "ج" فليس يلزم أن يكون البعض الآخر من "ب" مسلوباً عن
لنه
وأما إذا كانت الحَمْلية جزئية سالبة فلما فيه من عدم لزوم الشرط من نسبة "أ" إلى "ج" كان سلباً أو إيجاباً، وعلى هذا تبين امتناع اللزوم عند كون الحملية كلية سالبة أيضاً.
وأما الشرط الثالث: فلأنه إذا كان المقدم جزئياً أي "ب" فيه ثابت لبعض "أ" فإذا كان ( كل "ب" "ج" ) أو (بعض "ب" "ج") فإذا كان ( كل "ج" "أ") فـ (بعض "أ" "ب") وهو شرط اللزوم، ولو كانت الحَمْلية سالبة لم يكن هذا الشرط متحققاً.

Continuation of the first art: On defining the forms of categorical syllogisms. Condition: For if 'A' is denied of some 'B' which is 'C', then it does not follow that the other part of 'B' is denied of [it?]. And as for when the categorical premise is particular negative, due to the lack of the condition from the relation of 'A' to 'C', whether it is denial or affirmation, and upon this is shown the impossibility of implication when the categorical is also universal negative. And as for the third condition: Because if the antecedent is particular, i.e., 'B' is affirmed of some 'A', then if (every 'B' is 'C') or (some 'B' is 'C'), then if (every 'C' is 'A'), then (some 'A' is 'B') – which is the condition of implication. And if the categorical were negative, this condition would not be realized. And as for its properties: the possibility of the conclusion being universal even if the antecedent of the conditional and the categorical are particular, and vice versa; and that the antecedent of the conclusion must be universal, whether both categorical and antecedent of conditional are universal or particular, or one is universal and the other particular.
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of categorical syllogisms, specifically compound syllogisms that combine a categorical premise (حملية) with a conditional premise (شرطية). The author explains the conditions (شروط) under which such a compound yields a valid conclusion. The first part, though partially missing, deals with a scenario where the categorical premise is particular negative; it asserts that no valid implication follows. The third condition is fully stated: if the antecedent of the conditional is particular (i.e., 'B' is affirmed of some 'A'), then in order for the implication to hold, the categorical premise must be such that it connects the terms to satisfy the antecedent. Then the author discusses properties of these compound syllogisms, such as the ability to produce a universal conclusion even from particular premises, and the condition that the antecedent of the conclusion must be universal. This is a technical classification of valid forms.

Key Takeaway
The validity of a compound syllogism with a conditional and categorical premise depends on specific conditions, including the quantity and quality of the premises as articulated in the third condition.

The Syllogism

First Five Productive Forms of Compound Syllogisms
Author enumerates and explains five of sixteen valid compound syllogistic forms with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the pattern of any of the first five compound syllogistic forms and construct the valid conditional conclusion from a given categorical and conditional premise.

Core Idea
Compound syllogisms combine a categorical premise with a conditional premise to yield a new conditional through term substitution.

In this dense passage, the author systematically presents the first five of sixteen valid forms of compound syllogisms. Each form specifies a combination of a universal affirmative categorical premise with a conditional premise whose antecedent varies in quantity and quality. Understanding the pattern of constructing the conclusion's antecedent by replacing the categorical's subject with its predicate is key to mastering these forms. We will walk through the first form in detail and then see how the pattern extends to the others.

Guided Path
1
Identify the categorical premise
Locate the universal affirmative categorical premise (كل ب ج) in each form. It is always a universal affirmative (كلي موجبة حملية). For example, in the first form: 'كل ب ج' (Every B is C).
2
Identify the conditional premise
Find the conditional premise (شرطية) and note its antecedent (مقدم). The antecedent varies: first form uses universal negative (لا شيء من أ ب), second uses particular affirmative (بعض أ ب), third uses particular negative (بعض أ ليس ب), fourth uses universal negative with a negative conditional, fifth uses particular affirmative with negative conditional.
The consequent is always 'فـ د ر' (then D is R).
3
Determine the form number
Based on the quantity and quality of the antecedent and whether the conditional is affirmative or negative, identify which of the five forms it matches. The first three have affirmative conditional premises; the last two have negative conditional premises.
The form number determines the type of antecedent in the conclusion.
4
Construct the conclusion's antecedent
The conclusion is a universal (or particular) conditional. Its antecedent is formed by replacing the subject of the conditional's antecedent (A) with the categorical's predicate (C), while keeping the same quantifier and negation. For form 1: from 'لا شيء من أ ب' to 'لا شيء من ج أ'. For form 2: from 'بعض أ ب' to 'كل ج أ' (note the transformation).
The author explains the transformation involves using the categorical to relate B and C.
5
Verify the condition of implication
The author proves validity by showing that the antecedent of the conclusion implies the antecedent of the original conditional, using the categorical premise. For form 1: 'لا شيء من ج أ' implies 'لا شيء من أ ج' (by conversion), and since 'كل ب ج', it follows that 'لا شيء من أ ب', which is the original antecedent. Thus the implication holds.
This step confirms the logical necessity of the conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قرائنه المنتجة فستة عشر اقتراناً:
الاقتران الأول من كليتين موجبتين، والمقدم من الشرطية كلي سالب كقولنا: (كل "ب" "ج") و(كل ما كان لا شيء من "أ" "ب" فـ "د" "ر") ونتيجته كلية موجبة متصلة، مقدمها كلي سالب تأليفه من محمول الحَمْلية وموضوع مقدم الشرطية، وتاليها تالي الشرطية وهي دائماً كل ما كان لا شيء من "ج" "أ" ف "د" "," لأنه إذا كان (لا) شيء من "ج" "أ") فـ (لا شيء من "أ" "ج") وكان كل "ب" داخلاً تحت "ج" ف (لا شيء من "أ" "ب") وهو شرط اللزوم.
الاقتران الثاني من كليتين موجبتين، والمقدم من الشرطية جزئي موجب. كقولنا: (كل "ب" "ج") و(دائماً كل ما كان بعض "أ" "ب" فـ "د" "ر") ونتيجته كلية موجبة متصلة، مقدمها كليّ موجب تأليفه من محمول الحَمْلية وموضوع مقدم الشرطية، وتاليها تالي الشرطية، وهي دائماً كل ما كان كل "ج" "أ" فـ "د" "ر") فـ (كل) "ب" "أ") لضرورة دخول كل "ب" تحت "ج" وإذا كان كل "ب" "أ") فـ (بعض "أ" ب) وهو شرط اللزوم.
الاقتران الثالث من كليتين موجبتين، والمقدم من الشرطية جزئي سالب كقولنا: (كل "ب" "ج") و(دائماً كل ما كان بعض "أ" ليس "ب" فـ "د" "ر") ونتيجته كلية موجبة متصلة مقدمها كلي سالب تأليفه من محمول الحَمْلية وموضوع مقدم الشرطية، وتاليها تالي الشرطية، وهي دائماً كل ما كان لا شيء من "ج" "أ" فـ "د" "ر") لأنه إذا كان لا شيء من "ج" "أ") فـ (لا شيء من "أ" "ج") وكان كل "ب" داخلاً تحت "ج" فقد تحقق ما هو شرط في لزوم التالي.
الاقتران الرابع من كليّة موجبة حَمْلية وكليّة سالبة شرطية، مقدمها كلي سالب كقولنا : (كل "ب" "ج") و(ليس البتة إذا كان لا شيء من "أ" "ب" فـ "د" "ر") ونتيجته كلية سالبة متصلة، مقدمها كلي سالب تأليفه من محمول الحملية وموضوع مقدم الشرطية، وتاليها تالي الشرطية، وهي ليس البتة إذا كان لا شيء من "ج" "أ" ف "د" "ر" لأنه إذا كان لا شيء من "ج" "أ") فـ (لا شيء من "أ" "ج") وكان كل "ب" داخلاً تحت "ج" فقد تحقق ما هو شرط اللزوم.
الاقتران الخامس من كليّة موجبة حَمْلية وكلية سالبة شرطية، مقدمها جزئي موجب، كقولنا : (كل "ب" "ج") و (ليس البتة إذا كان بعض "أ" "ب" فـ "د" "ر") ونتيجته كلية سالبة متصلة مقدمها كلي موجب تأليفه من محمول الحملية وموضوع مقدم الشرطية وتاليها تالي الشرطية وهي ليس البتة إذا كان كل "ج" "أ" فـ "د" "ر" لأنه إذا كان (بعض "ب" "ج") و(كل "ج" "أ") فـ (بعض "ب" "أ") و(بعض "أ" "ب") فقد تحقق ما هو الشرط.
And as for its productive conjunctions, they are sixteen conjunctions: The first conjunction: from two universal affirmatives, and the antecedent of the conditional is universal negative, as in our saying: 'Every B is C' and 'Every thing that is nothing of A is B, then D is R' – and its conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional, whose antecedent is a universal negative composed from the predicate of the categorical and the subject of the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the consequent of the conditional, and it is always: 'Every thing that is nothing of C is A, then D is R', because if (nothing of C is A) then (nothing of A is C) and every B is included under C, so (nothing of A is B) – which is the condition of implication. The second conjunction: from two universal affirmatives, and the antecedent of the conditional is particular affirmative, as in our saying: 'Every B is C' and 'Always, every thing that is some A is B, then D is R' – and its conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional, whose antecedent is a universal affirmative composed from the predicate of the categorical and the subject of the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the consequent of the conditional, and it is always: 'Every thing that is every C is A, then D is R', which yields (every B is A) by necessity of every B being under C; and if (every B is A) then (some A is B) – which is the condition of implication. The third conjunction: from two universal affirmatives, and the antecedent of the conditional is particular negative, as in our saying: 'Every B is C' and 'Always, every thing that is some A is not B, then D is R' – and its conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional, whose antecedent is a universal negative composed from the predicate of the categorical and the subject of the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the consequent of the conditional, and it is always: 'Every thing that is nothing of C is A, then D is R', because if nothing of C is A, then nothing of A is C, and every B is included under C, so what is a condition for the implication of the consequent is realized. The fourth conjunction: from a universal affirmative categorical and a universal negative conditional, whose antecedent is universal negative, as in our saying: 'Every B is C' and 'It is not at all that if nothing of A is B, then D is R' – and its conclusion is a universal negative conditional, whose antecedent is a universal negative composed from the predicate of the categorical and the subject of the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the consequent of the conditional, and it is: 'It is not at all that if nothing of C is A, then D is R', because if nothing of C is A, then nothing of A is C, and every B is included under C, so the condition of implication is realized. The fifth conjunction: from a universal affirmative categorical and a universal negative conditional, whose antecedent is particular affirmative, as in our saying: 'Every B is C' and 'It is not at all that if some A is B, then D is R' – and its conclusion is a universal negative conditional, whose antecedent is a universal affirmative composed from the predicate of the categorical and the subject of the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the consequent of the conditional, and it is: 'It is not at all that if every C is A, then D is R', because if (some B is C) and (every C is A) then (some B is A) and (some A is B) – so the condition is realized.
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This passage enumerates and explains the first five of sixteen valid forms (اقترانات) of compound syllogisms that combine a categorical premise (حملية) and a conditional premise (شرطية). Each form is described with a general pattern and an example using placeholder letters (أ، ب، ج، د, ر). The author shows how the conclusion is derived by transforming the premises. In each case, the conclusion is a conditional statement whose antecedent is constructed from the predicate of the categorical and the subject of the conditional's antecedent, and whose consequent is the same as the conditional's consequent. The author then proves the validity by showing that the condition (شرط اللزوم) is satisfied. For example, in the first form, if (everything that is not A is B implies D is R) and (every B is C), then we deduce (everything that is not A is C implies D is R). The reasoning uses the fact that if nothing of C is A, then nothing of A is C, and since every B is C, nothing of A is B, which matches the antecedent of the conditional premise. This is a detailed demonstration of how such compound syllogisms work.

Checkpoint
In the third productive form, what is the antecedent of the conditional premise?
A) Universal negative (لا شيء من أ ب)
B) Particular affirmative (بعض أ ب)
C) Particular negative (بعض أ ليس ب)
D) Universal affirmative (كل أ ب)

Key Takeaway
Compound syllogisms of this type produce a conditional conclusion by substituting the categorical's predicate into the conditional antecedent, and their validity is proven by showing that the conclusion's antecedent implies the original conditional antecedent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the categorical premise 'كل خمر حرام' (Every khamr is haram) and the conditional premise 'إذا كان شراب ليس خمراً فهو حلال' (If a drink is not khamr, then it is halal), identify which of the five forms this matches (form 1: universal affirmative categorical, universal negative antecedent) and write the conclusion using the pattern.
?
Using the second form pattern, take the categorical 'كل ب ج' with B = intoxicant, C = forbidden, and conditional 'إذا كان بعض أ ب ففيه حد' (If some A is B, then it incurs a hadd). Construct the conclusion and verify the condition of implication by showing that if every C is A, then some A is B (since every B is C, every B is A, and thus some A is B).

The Syllogism
advanced
15 min
Compound Syllogisms of Categorical and Conditional Premises
Enumerates compound syllogisms with proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive a compound syllogism's conclusion from a categorical premise and a conditional premise using conversion and immediate inference.

Core Idea
Compound syllogisms link terms across categorical and conditional premises via conversion and syllogistic reasoning.

This passage systematically presents eight compound syllogisms, each combining one categorical premise (Every/Some B is C) with a conditional premise relating A and B. The conclusion always relates C and A. Understanding this pattern allows you to construct and verify complex arguments that mix proposition types, a key skill in Islamic logical analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the categorical premise
Locate the ḥamliyya proposition, usually 'Every B is C' or 'Some B is C'. Note its quantifier (universal/particular) and quality (affirmative/negative).
In this passage, the categorical is always affirmative.
2
Identify the conditional premise
Find the sharṭiyya proposition: it has an antecedent relating A and B, and a consequent relating D and R. Note its quantifier (universal/particular) and quality (affirmative/negative) of the antecedent.
The conditional's antecedent may be positive or negative, universal or particular.
3
Determine the structure of the conclusion
The conclusion is a conditional whose antecedent is composed of the predicate of the categorical (C) and the subject of the conditional's antecedent (A). Its quantifier and quality mirror that of the conditional's antecedent. The consequent is the same D R.
For example, if conditional's antecedent is 'none A is B', the conclusion's antecedent is 'none C is A'.
4
Apply the proof pattern using conversion
The proof assumes the conclusion's antecedent and uses the categorical to derive the conditional's antecedent via conversion and syllogism. For universal affirmative categorical, use conversion (e.g., 'Every B is C' converts to 'some C is B') but here they use a syllogism: from 'Every C is A' and 'Every B is C' deduce 'Every B is A', then convert to 'some A is B' if needed.
The specific conversions vary by mood; check the text for details.
5
Write the final conclusion
State the conditional conclusion exactly as derived, ensuring the antecedent relates C and A and the consequent is D R. Verify that the proof shows the implication is sound.
Example from 7th: from 'Every B is C' and 'If none A is B then D R', get 'If none C is A then D R'.

The seventh conjunction: from a universal affirmative categorical and a particular affirmative conditional, whose antecedent is a universal negative, as we say: 'Every B is C' and 'It may be that if none of A is B, then D is R'. Its conclusion is a particular affirmative conditional, whose antecedent is a universal negative composed of the predicate of the categorical and the subject of the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the consequent of the conditional. It is: 'It may be that if none of C is A, then D is R' because if none of C is A, then (none of A is C) and every B falls under C, so (none of A is B), which is the condition of implication. The eighth conjunction: from a universal affirmative categorical and a particular affirmative conditional, whose antecedent is a particular affirmative, as we say: 'Every B is C' and 'It may be that if some A is B, then D is R'. Its conclusion is a particular affirmative conditional, whose antecedent is a universal affirmative composed of the predicate of the categorical and the subject of the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the consequent of the conditional. It is: 'It may be that if every C is A, then D is R' because if (every C is A) and (every B is C), then (every B is A) and its converse (some A is B), which is the condition of implication. The ninth conjunction: from a universal affirmative categorical and a particular affirmative conditional, whose antecedent is a particular negative, as we say: 'Every B is C' and 'It may be that if some A is not B, then D is R'. Its conclusion is a particular affirmative conditional, whose antecedent is a universal negative composed of the predicate of the categorical and the subject of the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the consequent of the conditional. It is: 'It may be that if none of C is A, then D is R' because if every B falls under C and (none of C is A), then (none of B is A) and its converse (none of A is B), and in this the condition of implication of the consequent is realized. The tenth conjunction: from a universal affirmative categorical and a particular negative conditional, whose antecedent is a universal negative, as we say: 'Every B is C' and 'Not always if none of A is B, then D is R'. Its conclusion is a particular negative conditional, whose antecedent is a universal negative composed of the predicate of the categorical and the subject of the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the consequent of the conditional. It is: 'Not always if none of C is A, then D is R' because if every B falls under C and (none of C is A), then (none of B is A) and its converse (none of A is B), which is the condition for implication. The eleventh conjunction: from a universal affirmative categorical and a particular negative conditional, whose antecedent is a universal affirmative, as we say: 'Every B is C' and 'Not always if some A is B, then D is R'. Its conclusion is a particular negative conditional, whose antecedent is a universal affirmative composed of the predicate of the categorical and the subject of the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the consequent of the conditional. It is: 'Not always if every C is A, then D is R' because if (every B is C) and (every C is A), then (every B is A) and its converse (some A is B), which is the condition for implication. The twelfth conjunction: from a universal affirmative categorical and a particular negative conditional, whose antecedent is a particular negative, as we say: 'Every B is C' and 'Not always if some A is not B, then D is R'. Its conclusion is a particular negative conditional: 'Not always if none of C is A, then D is R' because if (every B is C) and (none of C is A), then (none of B is A) and its converse (none of A is B), and in this the condition of implication is realized. The thirteenth conjunction: from a particular affirmative categorical and a universal affirmative conditional, whose antecedent is a particular affirmative, as we say: 'Some B is C' and 'Always, if some A is B, then D is R'. Its conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional, whose antecedent is a universal affirmative composed of the predicate of the categorical and the subject of the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the consequent of the conditional. It is: 'Always, if every C is A, then D is R' because if (some B is C) and (every C is A), then (some B is A) and its converse (some A is B), which is the condition. The fourteenth conjunction: from a particular affirmative categorical and a universal negative conditional, whose antecedent is a particular affirmative, as we say: 'Some B is C' and 'Not at all if some A is B, then D is R'. Its conclusion is a universal negative conditional, whose antecedent is a universal affirmative composed of the predicate of the categorical and the subject of the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the consequent of the conditional. It is: 'Not at all if every C is A, then D is R' because if (some B is C) and (every C is A), then (some B is A) and its converse (some A is B), which is the condition.
Source pages p. 334

Commentary
This passage defines eight compound syllogisms (iqtiṙānāt) from the seventh to the fourteenth, each combining one categorical (ḥamliyya) premise with one conditional (sharṭiyya) premise. The categorical premise is always in the form 'Every B is C' or 'Some B is C'. The conditional premise varies in quantity (universal/particular) and quality (affirmative/negative) and involves terms A, B, D, R. The antecedent of the conditional always relates A and B; the consequent relates D and R. The conclusion is always a conditional whose antecedent relates C and A (the predicate of the categorical and the subject of the conditional's antecedent) and whose consequent is the same D R. The author provides a proof for each by converting the categorical and the conditional's antecedent, then using immediate inferences (conversion, 'aks) to show that the condition (شرط اللزوم) for the implication holds. For example, in the seventh conjunction, from 'Every B is C' and 'If none A is B then D is R', we derive 'If none C is A then D is R' because: if none C is A, then by conversion none A is C; and since every B is C, none A is B; that matches the antecedent of the given conditional. The eighth uses 'If some A is B then D is R' and 'Every B is C' to get 'If every C is A then D is R', relying on 'Every C is A' implying 'Every B is A' then 'Some A is B'. The pattern continues through all eight, each with a specific combination of quantity/quality. The author logically exhausts the possible moods of this mixed syllogism, demonstrating the power of combining categorical and conditional reasoning. Note that the categorical premise always has 'B' as subject and 'C' as predicate; the conditional's antecedent relates 'A' and 'B' (positive or negative); the conclusion relates 'C' and 'A'. This systematic enumeration is typical of post-Avicennan logic texts.

Key Terms
اقتران
حملي
شرطي
مقدم
تالي
شرط اللزوم

Checkpoint
In the seventh conjunction, the categorical premise is 'Every B is C' and the conditional premise is 'If none A is B then D is R'. What is the antecedent of the conclusion?
A) 'If none A is B'
B) 'If none C is A'
C) 'If none A is C'
D) 'If some A is B'

Key Takeaway
Compound syllogisms combine categorical and conditional premises by using the categorical to bridge the terms in the conditional's antecedent, enabling a new conditional conclusion about C and A.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: Given the categorical premise 'Every act that causes harm (B) is prohibited (C)' and the conditional premise 'If some action (A) is an act that causes harm (B), then it is sinful (D R)', derive the conclusion of the eighth conjunction (categorical universal affirmative, conditional particular affirmative antecedent universal?). Actually, the eighth has conditional antecedent particular affirmative: 'If some A is B'. So for this task: What is the conclusion? (Use the pattern: antecedent of conclusion is 'Every C is A'). Verify by showing that if 'Every C is A' and 'Every B is C', then 'Every B is A' and by conversion 'Some A is B', which is the conditional's antecedent.
?
Task 2: Choose one of the fourteen conjunctions (e.g., the seventh) and write out the proof step by step using Arabic terms and conversion rules, explaining how the condition of implication is satisfied.

The Eleventh Conjunction: Mixed Syllogism with Conversion
Author defines a mixed syllogism with conversion step

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the eleventh conjunctive syllogism and verify its validity using the categorical conversion step.

Core Idea
Mixed syllogisms require converting the categorical premise to form a valid conditional chain.

This lesson unpacks a complex mixed syllogism from the Daqāʾiq al-Ḥaqāʾiq, where the author combines a universal affirmative categorical premise with a particular negative conditional premise. The logical steps involve converting the categorical to derive the antecedent for the conditional conclusion. Understanding this conjunction sharpens your ability to analyze compound arguments in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises
The first premise is a universal affirmative categorical: (Every B is C). The second is a particular negative conditional: (Not every thing that is some A is B is D is R). Note the structure: the conditional's antecedent is 'some A is B' and its consequent is 'D is R'.
The variables B, C, A, D, R are placeholders for any terms (e.g., in Islamic law, B could be 'intoxicant', C 'forbidden').
2
Find the conclusion
The conclusion is also a particular negative conditional: (Not every thing that is every C is A is D is R). Its antecedent is 'every C is A' (universal affirmative), composed from the predicate of the categorical (C) and the subject of the conditional's antecedent (A).
The consequent remains the same as in the second premise (D is R).
3
Understand the proof
The author shows: since (Every B is C) and (Every C is A) would imply (Every B is A) by a simple categorical syllogism. Then convert (Every B is A) to its converse (Every A is B). This converse becomes the condition: if every C is A, then every A is B. Combined with the conditional premise, the conclusion follows.
The conversion step is essential to link the categorical to the conditional structure.
4
Verify the logical flow
Start with (Every B is C). Assume (Every C is A) leads to (Every B is A). Converse to (Every A is B). Now, the conditional premise says: Not (if some A is B then D is R). But the conversion shows that (some A is B) does occur when (Every C is A). Therefore, the implication fails, yielding the conclusion that not every C is A leads to D is R.
This is a form of modus tollens: the conditional is denied by affirming its antecedent's impossibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاقتران الحادي عشر من كليّة موجبة حَمْلية وجزئية سالبة شرطية، مقدمها كلي موجب، كقولنا : (كل "ب" "ج" ) و ( ليس كل ما كان بعض "أ" "ب" فـ "د" "ر")، ونتيجته جزئية سالبة شرطية، مقدمها كلي موجب تأليفه من محمول الحملية وموضوع مقدم الشرطية، وتاليها تالي الشرطية، وهي ليس كل ما كان كل "ج" "أ" فـ "د" "ر" لأنه إذا كان (كل "ب" "ج") وكان (كل "ج" "أ") فـ (كل "ب" "أ") وعكسه "أ" "ب") وهو الشرط للزوم.
The eleventh conjunction [consists] of a universal affirmative categorical and a particular negative conditional, its antecedent universal affirmative, as our saying: (Every B is C) and (Not every thing that is some A is B is D is R), and its conclusion is a particular negative conditional, its antecedent universal affirmative composed from the predicate of the categorical and the subject of the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the consequent of the conditional, and it is: Not every thing that is every C is A is D is R, because if (Every B is C) and (Every C is A) then (Every B is A) and its converse (A is B) which is the condition for the implication.
Source pages p. 335

Commentary
This passage defines the eleventh conjunction, a mixed syllogism combining a universal affirmative categorical premise (Every B is C) with a particular negative conditional premise (Not every thing that is some A is B is D is R). The conclusion is a particular negative conditional: Not every thing that is every C is A is D is R. The proof works by converting the categorical premise: from Every B is C and Every C is A we infer Every B is A, then convert to its converse (Every A is B), which then serves as the antecedent condition (if every C is A, then every A is B) to derive the conclusion by modus tollens from the conditional premise. The author uses the standard logical variables (أ, ب, ج, د, ر) to illustrate the structure.

C) The predicate of the categorical and the consequent of the conditional
D) The subject of the categorical and the consequent of the conditional

Key Takeaway
The eleventh conjunction demonstrates how a mixed syllogism with a categorical and a conditional premise can be validated by converting the categorical to create the necessary condition for the conclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the categorical premise 'Every act that is harmful is forbidden' and the conditional premise 'Not every act that is some transaction is harmful is valid'. Using the pattern of the eleventh conjunction, determine the conclusion and explain the conversion step.
?
Construct a concrete Islamic legal example where a universal affirmative categorical (e.g., 'Every intoxicant is forbidden') and a particular negative conditional (e.g., 'Not every drink that is a beverage is an intoxicant is permissible') are combined. Show how the conversion of the categorical yields the antecedent of the conditional conclusion.

Conditions and Properties of Mixed Syllogisms in the First Figure
Author states the general rules for mixed syllogisms with categorical and conditional premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions and properties of a mixed syllogism (categorical + conditional) in the first figure and determine whether a given mixed syllogism satisfies them.

Core Idea
Mixed syllogisms in the first figure have specific conditions: the categorical must be affirmative, and the consequent's quantity depends on the conditional's quality.

In the previous lessons, you learned about pure categorical and pure conditional syllogisms. Now, we combine both types: one premise is categorical (ḥamliyya) and the other is conditional (sharṭiyya). This passage from the text lays down the rules for such mixed syllogisms when they are in the first figure. Understanding these conditions and properties is essential for evaluating arguments in Islamic jurisprudence and theology where both categorical and conditional statements appear.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of mixed syllogism
Determine which premises are categorical and which are conditional. The passage specifies that the categorical is the minor premise (ṣughrā) and the sharing is between the categorical and the consequent (tālī) of the conditional.
The categorical is always the minor premise in this configuration.
2
Check the condition: categorical must be affirmative
The first condition is 'إيجاب الحَمْلية' – the categorical premise must be affirmative. If it is negative, the syllogism is not valid in this figure.
Affirmation here means the proposition is affirmative (subject is predicate), not negative.
3
Check the condition: quantity of the consequent
The second condition concerns the consequent of the conditional. If the conditional is affirmative (muttaṣila mūjiba), the consequent must be universal (kulliyya). If the conditional is negative (saliba), the consequent must be particular (juz’iyya).
The quantity of the consequent is determined by the quality of the conditional.
4
Explore the properties of the first figure
The passage mentions two properties: (1) The conclusion can be universal even if the categorical premise is particular. (2) The conclusion can be particular even if both the categorical and the consequent are universal. This flexibility distinguishes the first figure.
These properties show that the first figure can yield conclusions of varying quantity.
5
Apply the rules to a concrete example
Take a fiqh example: categorical 'Every intoxicant is forbidden (ḥarām)' (affirmative universal), conditional 'If it is grape juice (nabīdh), then it is intoxicating' (affirmative, so consequent must be universal: 'it is intoxicating' is universal). The conditions are satisfied, so the conclusion 'Therefore, grape juice is forbidden' follows, and it can be universal or particular according to the properties.
This shows how the rules work in practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحكم النتيجة في الإطلاق والجهة حكم الشرطية في جميع هذه الاقترانات. وأما إن كان الاشتراك بين الحَمْلية وتالي الشرطية، والحَمْلية صغرى، فقد يكون التأليف منهما أيضاً على نمط الأشكال الأربعة، فإن كانت على نمط الشكل الأول فله شروط وخواص. أما شروطه فإيجاب الحَمْلية، وكليّة التالي من الشرطية إن كانت المتصلة موجبة، وجزئية إن كانت سالبة، وقد عرفت كمية ذلك في الحمليات. وأما خواصه، فجواز كون النتيجة كليّة وإن كانت الحَمْلية جزئية، وجواز كونها جزئية، إن كانت الحَمْلية والتالي كليين. وأما قرائنه المنتجة فستة عشر اقتراناً:
And the ruling of the conclusion in absoluteness and modality is the ruling of the conditional in all these pairings. As for when the sharing is between the categorical and the consequent of the conditional, and the categorical is minor, then the composition from them may also be according to the pattern of the four figures. If it is according to the pattern of the first figure, then it has conditions and properties. As for its conditions: the affirmation of the categorical, and the universality of the consequent from the conditional if the conjunctive conditional is affirmative, and its particularity if it is negative, and you have known the quantity of that in the categoricals. And as for its properties: the permissibility of the conclusion being universal even if the categorical is particular, and the permissibility of its being particular if the categorical and the consequent are both universal. And as for its productive pairings, they are sixteen pairings:
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage defines the rules for mixed syllogisms where one premise is categorical (حملیة) and the other is conditional (شرطية), specifically when the shared term is between the categorical and the consequent (تالي) of the conditional, and the categorical is the minor premise. The author states that such combinations can be arranged in any of the four figures. For the first figure, there are specific conditions and properties. The conditions are: the categorical must be affirmative, and the consequent of the conditional must be universal if the conditional is affirmative, or particular if the conditional is negative. These conditions ensure a valid inference. The properties are: the conclusion can be universal even if the categorical is particular, and it can be particular even if both premises are universal. This flexibility is a special feature of the first figure. The author then notes that there are sixteen productive pairings in this figure, which will be enumerated. An example from fiqh: if the categorical is 'Every intoxicant is prohibited' and the conditional is 'If it is grape juice, then it is intoxicating', the consequent 'it is intoxicating' is universal, so the conclusion can be universal.

الشرطية

Key Takeaway
Mixed syllogisms in the first figure require the categorical premise to be affirmative and the consequent's quantity to match the conditional's quality, while the conclusion's quantity enjoys flexibility.

First Seven Pairings of Mixed Syllogisms (First Figure)
Author enumerates the first seven productive pairings of categorical and conditional premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the conclusion for any of the first seven mixed syllogism pairings in the first figure given a universal affirmative categorical premise and a conditional premise with specified quantity and quality.

Core Idea
The conclusions of mixed syllogisms in the first figure are always conditional propositions derived by applying the categorical to the consequent of the conditional.

In this lesson, we examine the first seven productive pairings where a universal affirmative categorical premise combines with a varying conditional premise to yield a valid conditional conclusion. These patterns, foundational in Islamic logic, allow you to derive rulings in fiqh and kalam by linking conditions to categorical truths. Mastering them enables you to move from general principles to conditional judgments with precision.

Guided Path
1
Identify the categorical premise
In these pairings, the categorical is always universal affirmative: 'Every B is C' (كل ب ج). Note the terms B and C.
2
Identify the conditional premise
The conditional has the form 'If D is R then ...' where the consequent is either universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, or particular negative. Note its quantity and quality.
3
Set the conclusion's antecedent
The antecedent of the conclusion is exactly the antecedent of the conditional premise (D is R).
4
Derive the conclusion's consequent
Apply the categorical 'Every B is C' to the consequent of the conditional: replace every occurrence of C with B, keeping the same quantity and quality. For example, if the consequent is 'every C is A', the conclusion consequent becomes 'every B is A'.
For pairings 5-7, the consequent may be negative or particular; the replacement rule still holds after necessary conversions as mentioned in the text.
5
Verify underlying syllogism
Check that the categorical and the conditional's consequent form a valid categorical syllogism in the first figure (Barbara or Celarent, etc.), ensuring the conclusion about B and A follows. Then reattach the antecedent to form the conditional conclusion.

The first pairing: from a universal affirmative categorical and a universal affirmative conditional whose consequent is universal affirmative, like our saying: (Every B is C) and (Always every that is C is B? [corrupt] ... and its conclusion is a universal affirmative conjunctive, its antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional and its consequent is the result of pairing the consequent with the categorical if they were alone, and it is (Always every that is D is R then every C is A) because if (Every C is A) holds when (D is R) and every B is C holds then every B is A.) The second pairing: from a universal affirmative categorical and a universal affirmative conditional whose consequent is universal negative, like our saying: (Every B is C) and (Always every that is D is R then no C is A) and its conclusion is a universal affirmative conjunctive ... always every that is D is R then no B is A). The third pairing: from a universal affirmative categorical and a particular affirmative conditional whose consequent is universal affirmative, like our saying: (Every B is C) and (It may be that if D is R then every C is A) and its conclusion is a particular affirmative conjunctive ... it may be that if D is R then every B is A). The fourth pairing: from a universal affirmative categorical and a particular affirmative conditional whose consequent is universal negative, like our saying: (Every B is C) and (It may be that if D is R then no C is A) and its conclusion is a particular affirmative conjunctive ... it may be that if D is R then no B is A). The fifth pairing: from a universal affirmative categorical and a universal negative conditional whose consequent is particular negative, like our saying: (Every B is C) and (Not at all if D is R then some C is not A) and its conclusion is a universal negative conjunctive ... not at all if D is R then some B is not A) and its explanation is by converting the conjunctive to affirmative and taking the necessary of the conclusion. The sixth pairing: from a universal affirmative categorical and a universal negative conditional whose consequent is particular affirmative, like our saying: (Every B is C) and (Not at all if D is R then some C is A) and its conclusion is a universal negative conjunctive ... not at all if D is R then some B is A) and its explanation is also by converting the conjunctive to affirmative and taking the necessary of the conclusion. The seventh pairing: from a universal affirmative categorical and a particular negative conditional whose consequent is particular negative, like our saying: (Every B is C) and (Not every that is D is R then some C is not A) and its conclusion is a particular negative conjunctive ... not every that is D is R then some B is not A) and its explanation is as before.

Commentary
This passage lists the first seven of sixteen productive pairings (اقترانات) for mixed syllogisms in the first figure, where the categorical premise is always universal affirmative and the conditional premise varies. Each pairing is a pattern: given a categorical (كل ب ج) and a conditional with a specific quantity and quality, the conclusion is a conditional proposition whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional premise and whose consequent is derived by applying the categorical to the consequent. For example, the first pairing: categorical (كل ب ج) and conditional (دائماً إذا كان د ر فكل ج أ) yields conclusion (دائماً إذا كان د ر فكل ب أ). The reasoning: if all C are A and all B are C, then all B are A. The author uses variables ب, ج, أ, د, ر to represent terms. The other pairings follow similar patterns, adjusting for negative and particular premises. The fifth and sixth pairings require a conversion of the conditional to its affirmative equivalent to derive the conclusion. These forms are essential for constructing valid arguments in Islamic logic, e.g., in fiqh: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden; when it is fermented, it is intoxicating; therefore when it is fermented, it is forbidden.'

A) Always if D is R then every B is A

C) Always if D is R then no B is A
D) Always if D is R then every A is B

Key Takeaway
The first seven pairings demonstrate a systematic method: given a universal affirmative categorical and a conditional premise, the conclusion's consequent replaces the categorical's predicate with its subject, preserving the conditional's quantity and quality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Syllogism
advanced
15 min
Mixed Syllogisms: Categorical plus Conditional in First Figure
Enumerates eight valid mixed syllogisms with universal affirmative categorical.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive the conclusion of a mixed syllogism (universal affirmative categorical + conditional) in the first figure by identifying the middle term and performing substitution.

Core Idea
In mixed syllogisms, replace the middle term in the conditional's consequent with the categorical's subject.

Mixed syllogisms combine a categorical proposition (hamliyya) with a conditional proposition (shartiyya). When both premises are in the first figure, the conclusion is itself a conditional whose antecedent is the same as the conditional's antecedent, and whose consequent is obtained by combining the categorical with the conditional's consequent. This lesson will walk you through the general pattern using the author's eight conjunctions as examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the categorical premise
The categorical is universal affirmative, e.g., 'Every B is J'. Note its subject (B) and predicate (J). The predicate J serves as the middle term connecting the two premises.
2
Identify the conditional premise
The conditional has the form 'If D is R then [relation of J to A]'. The relation can be universal/particular and affirmative/negative. Write down the antecedent and the consequent.
3
Combine the categorical with the conditional's consequent
Treat the conditional's consequent as a categorical proposition about J and A. Combine it with the categorical 'Every B is J' using first-figure (Barbara or Celarent etc.) to get a new categorical about B and A. For example, from 'Every B is J' and 'Every J is A' we get 'Every B is A'.
4
Form the conclusion as a conditional
The conclusion is a conditional with the same antecedent as the original conditional, and the consequent is the categorical you just derived (about B and A). The quantity and quality of the conclusion match those of the original conditional.
5
Apply to the eight conjunctions
The author lists eight cases based on the quantity and quality of the conditional and its consequent. Practise each by following steps 1-4. Notice how the conclusion's consequent always replaces J with B.
For universal negative conditionals, the proof involves converting the conditional to affirmative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاقتران الثالث من كليّة موجبة حَمْلية وجزئية موجبة شرطية تاليها كلي موجب، كقولنا : (كل "ب" "ج") وقد يكون إذا كان "د" "ر" فكل "ج" "أ") ونتيجته جزئية موجبة متصلة، مقدمها مقدم الشرطية، وتاليها نتيجة اقتران التالي بالحَمْلية لو انفردا، وهي قد يكون إذا كان "د" "ر" فكل "ب" "أ").
الاقتران الرابع من كلية موجبة حَمْلية وجزئية موجبة شرطية، تاليها كلي سالب كقولنا: (كل "ب" "ج ") و(قد يكون إذا كان "د" "ر" فلا شيء من "ج" "أ") ونتيجته جزئية موجبة متصلة، مقدمها مقدم الشرطية، وتاليها تالي نتيجة اقتران التالي بالحَمْلية، وهي (قد يكون إذا كان "د" ر" فلا شيء من "ب" "أ").
الاقتران الخامس من كليّة موجبة حَمْلية وكليّة سالبة شرطية تاليها جزئي سالب كقولنا: (كل "ب" "ج") و(ليس البتة إذا كان "د" "ر" فبعض "ج" ليس "أ") ، ونتيجته كلية سالبة متصلة، مقدمها مقدم الشرطية، وتاليها تالي نتيجة اقتران التالي بالحَمْلية وهي ليس البتة إذا كان "د" "ر" فبعض "ب" ليس "أ") وبيانه بقلب المتصلة إلى الإيجاب وأخذ لازم النتيجة.
الاقتران السادس من كليّة موجبة حَمْلية وكلية سالبة شرطية، تاليها جزئي موجب، كقولنا: (كل "ب" "ج") و(ليس البتة إذا كان "د" "ر" فبعض "ج" "أ") ونتيجته كلية سالبة متصلة، مقدمها مقدم الشرطية، وتاليها تالي نتيجة اقتران التالي بالحملية وهي ليس البتة إذا كان "د" "ر" فبعض "ب" "أ") وبيانه بقلب المتصلة إلى الإيجاب أيضاً وأخذ لازم النتيجة.
الاقتران السابع من كليّة موجبة حملية وجزئية سالبة شرطية، تاليها جزئي سالب ، كقولنا: (كل "ب" "ج") و(ليس كل ما كان "د" "ر" فبعض "ج" ليس "أ") ونتيجته جزئية سالبة متصلة مقدمها مقدم الشرطية، وتاليها تالي نتيجة اقتران التالي بالحَمْلية، وهي ليس) كل ما كان "د" "ر" فبعض "ب" ليس "أ") وبيانه كما سبق.
الاقتران الثامن من كليّة موجبة حَمْلية وجزئية سالبة شرطية تاليها جزئي ،موجب كقولنا : (كل) "ب" "ج") و(ليس كل ما كان "د" "ر" فبعض "ج" "أ") ونتيجته جزئية سالبة شرطية، مقدمها جزئي سالب وتاليها تالي نتيجة اقتران التالي بالحَمْلية وهي ليس كل ما كان "د" "ر" فبعض "ب" "أ") وبيانه أيضاً كما عرف.
وأما إن كانت الحَمْلية جزئية فقد تأتلف منها مع الشرطية على النحو المذكور في الكلية اقترانات أُخر ثمانية، إلا أن تالي النتيجة في كلها لا يكون إلا جزئياً، فاعتبره بنفسك.
The third conjunction from a universal affirmative categorical and a particular affirmative conditional whose consequent is universal affirmative, like our saying: (Every 'B' is 'J') and (It may be that if 'D' is 'R', then every 'J' is 'A') and its result is a particular affirmative conditional, whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and whose consequent is the result of combining the consequent with the categorical if they were separate, and it is: (It may be that if 'D' is 'R', then every 'B' is 'A'). The fourth conjunction from a universal affirmative categorical and a particular affirmative conditional whose consequent is universal negative, like our saying: (Every 'B' is 'J') and (It may be that if 'D' is 'R', then nothing of 'J' is 'A') and its result is a particular affirmative conditional, whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and whose consequent is the consequent of the result of combining the consequent with the categorical, and it is: (It may be that if 'D' is 'R', then nothing of 'B' is 'A'). The fifth conjunction from a universal affirmative categorical and a universal negative conditional whose consequent is particular negative, like our saying: (Every 'B' is 'J') and (Never, if 'D' is 'R', then some 'J' is not 'A'), and its result is a universal negative conditional, whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and whose consequent is the consequent of the result of combining the consequent with the categorical, and it is: (Never, if 'D' is 'R', then some 'B' is not 'A'), and its explanation is by converting the conditional into affirmative and taking the necessary consequence of the result. The sixth conjunction from a universal affirmative categorical and a universal negative conditional whose consequent is particular affirmative, like our saying: (Every 'B' is 'J') and (Never, if 'D' is 'R', then some 'J' is 'A') and its result is a universal negative conditional, whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and whose consequent is the consequent of the result of combining the consequent with the categorical, and it is: (Never, if 'D' is 'R', then some 'B' is 'A'), and its explanation is by converting the conditional into affirmative also and taking the necessary consequence of the result. The seventh conjunction from a universal affirmative categorical and a particular negative conditional whose consequent is particular negative, like our saying: (Every 'B' is 'J') and (Not every time if 'D' is 'R' then some 'J' is not 'A') and its result is a particular negative conditional, whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and whose consequent is the consequent of the result of combining the consequent with the categorical, and it is: (Not every time if 'D' is 'R' then some 'B' is not 'A'), and its explanation is as before. The eighth conjunction from a universal affirmative categorical and a particular negative conditional whose consequent is particular affirmative, like our saying: (Every 'B' is 'J') and (Not every time if 'D' is 'R' then some 'J' is 'A') and its result is a particular negative conditional, whose antecedent is particular negative, and whose consequent is the consequent of the result of combining the consequent with the categorical, and it is: (Not every time if 'D' is 'R' then some 'B' is not 'A'), and its explanation is also as known. And if the categorical is particular, then there are other eight conjunctions composed from it with the conditional in the manner mentioned for the universal, except that the consequent of the result in all of them is only particular, so consider it yourself.
Source pages p. 337

Commentary
This passage continues the classification of mixed syllogisms (qiyas iqtirani) that combine a categorical premise (hamliyya) with a conditional premise (shartiyya). The author is working through the first figure (al-shakl al-awwal) and enumerates eight valid conjunctions (iqtiranat) when the categorical is universal affirmative (kulliyya mujiba). Each conjunction is defined by the quantity and quality of the conditional and its consequent. The pattern is: given a categorical like 'Every B is J', and a conditional like 'If D is R then (some relation between J and A)', the conclusion is a conditional where the antecedent is the same as the conditional's antecedent, and the consequent is derived by replacing J with B in the conditional's consequent. The result is always a conditional of the same quality and quantity as the conditional premise, but with the subject of the consequent replaced. The author provides examples using variables (B, J, D, R, A) and explains the proof for some by converting the conditional. At the end, he notes that if the categorical is particular, there are also eight conjunctions but the result's consequent is always particular. This is a detailed technical exposition aimed at students of logic.

In mixed first-figure syllogisms, the conclusion is a conditional whose consequent results from substituting the categorical's subject into the conditional's consequent.

Second Figure Mixed Syllogisms: Conditions and First Two Conjunctions
Author states condition and first three conjunctions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a valid second figure mixed syllogism by applying its condition and substituting the categorical's subject into the conditional's consequent.

Core Idea
Second figure mixed syllogisms produce conditional conclusions by substituting the categorical subject into the conditional's consequent.

In this passage, the author transitions to mixed syllogisms in the second figure, where one premise is categorical and the other is conditional. He first states the general condition for productivity, then enumerates the productive conjunctions. The first two conjunctions are explained in full; understanding them is essential for evaluating combined categorical-conditional arguments in the Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises
Locate the categorical premise (hamliyya) and the conditional premise (shartiyya) in the argument. For example, in the first conjunction: 'Every B is J' (categorical) and 'Always, if something is D, then nothing of A is J' (conditional).
2
Check the condition for the second figure
The categorical and the conditional's consequent must satisfy the same conditions as in categorical second figure syllogisms: the middle term (here J) is predicate in both premises. Additionally, to ensure universality in the conditional's consequent, it must be made particular if needed, as in the first figure.
The author says 'save that the conditional is a consequent' – meaning the conditional itself is the consequent premise? Actually, the conditional as a whole is one premise, but its consequent (tali) is what interacts with the categorical.
3
Determine the conclusion's form
The conclusion is a conditional with the same antecedent as the conditional premise. Its consequent is derived by substituting the categorical's subject (B) for the categorical's predicate (J) in the conditional's consequent.
For the first conjunction: conditional's consequent is 'nothing of A is J'. Substituting B for J gives 'nothing of B is A'.
4
Verify using conversion or immediate inference
The author explains that the result's validity is shown by converting the conditional's consequent. For the first conjunction, convert 'nothing of A is J' to 'nothing of J is A', then with 'Every B is J' and a second figure categorical syllogism, derive 'nothing of B is A'.
This step highlights the inner categorical reasoning behind the mixed syllogism.

Commentary
This passage moves to mixed syllogisms in the second figure (al-shakl al-thani). However, because the conditional is present as a premise, the universality of the consequent is ensured by making it particular as in the first figure. The special property (khassa) of the second figure in mixed form is the same as in categorical second figure, i.e., the middle term is predicate in both premises. The proof involves converting the consequent. The second conjunction uses a universal negative conditional with a particular affirmative consequent, yielding a universal negative conditional. The explanation involves converting the conditional into affirmative. The third conjunction is partially given, starting a particular affirmative conditional with a universal negative consequent. The passage breaks off due to page end. This methodically constructs the valid forms for the second figure mixed syllogisms.

Eighth Conjunction: Mixed Syllogism with Universal Affirmative Categorical
Defines the eighth productive mood of the first figure mixed syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the conclusion of a mixed syllogism in the first figure given a universal affirmative categorical premise and a particular negative conditional premise.

Core Idea
In mixed syllogisms, the categorical modifies the consequent to produce a new conditional conclusion.

This lesson completes the first figure of mixed syllogisms with its eighth productive mood. Here, a universal affirmative categorical (كلية موجبة) is combined with a particular negative conditional (جزئية سالبة شرطية) whose consequent is particular affirmative. You will learn how to derive the conclusion by conjoining the categorical with the consequent of the conditional, preserving the antecedent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises
Locate the categorical premise (e.g., 'Every B is J') and the conditional premise (e.g., 'If not every D is R, then some J is A'). Note the antecedent of the conditional is particular negative.
2
Extract the consequent of the conditional
The consequent is 'some J is A' (particular affirmative). This is the part that will be combined with the categorical.
3
Combine the consequent with the categorical
Apply the first figure categorical syllogism to the categorical premise and the consequent: from 'Every B is J' and 'some J is A', infer 'some B is A'.
This is valid because the middle term J appears as predicate in the categorical and subject in the consequent.
4
Form the conclusion
The conclusion is a new conditional with the same antecedent as the original conditional and the newly inferred consequent: 'If not every D is R, then some B is A'.
The result is a particular negative conditional, matching the form given in the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاقتران الثامن من كلية موجبة حمليّة وجزئية سالبة شرطية تاليها جزئي موجب كقولنا: (كل ب ج) و(ليس كل ما كان د ر فبعض ج أ) ونتيجته جزئية سالبة شرطية، مقدمها جزئي سالب وتاليها تالي نتيجة اقتران التالي بالحمليّة وهي (ليس كل ما كان د ر فبعض ب أ) وبيانه أيضاً كما عرف.
The eighth conjunction: from a universal affirmative categorical and a particular negative conditional whose consequent is particular affirmative, as we say: 'Every B is J' and 'If not every D is R, then some J is A' and its result is a particular negative conditional, whose antecedent is particular negative and its consequent is the consequent of the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, which is 'If not every D is R, then some B is A' and its explanation is as previously known.
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage defines the eighth and final productive conjunction of the first figure of mixed syllogisms (qiyas iqtirani). The first figure in mixed syllogisms is defined by the position of the middle term. Here, the author gives a specific mood: the categorical premise is universal affirmative ('Every B is J'), and the conditional premise is particular negative with a particular affirmative consequent ('If not every D is R, then some J is A'). The conclusion is a particular negative conditional: 'If not every D is R, then some B is A'. The author explains that the conclusion is derived by conjoining the consequent of the conditional with the categorical premise, then taking the result as the consequent of a new conditional with the same antecedent. The explanation (bayān) is said to be as previously known, referring to the earlier method of proof by conversion or syllogistic reasoning. This mood is productive because it satisfies the conditions of the first figure.

Key Terms
اقتران (iqtirān): A pair of premises in a syllogism; here specifically a mood of a mixed syllogism combining a categorical and a conditional.
حمليّة (ḥamliyya): A categorical proposition (subject-predicate).
شرطية (sharṭiyya): A conditional proposition (if-then).
تالي (tālī): The consequent of a conditional.
مقدم (muqaddam): The antecedent of a conditional.
نتيجة (natīja): The conclusion of a syllogism.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway

Second Figure of Mixed Syllogisms: Conditions and First Conjunction
Defines conditions and enumerates productive conjunctions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the condition for a valid mixed syllogism in the second figure and derive the conclusion for the first productive conjunction.

Core Idea
The second figure of mixed syllogisms requires the categorical and consequent to satisfy categorical conditions.

In the second figure of mixed syllogisms, one premise is categorical and the other is conditional. The author specifies that the categorical premise and the consequent of the conditional must meet the usual conditions for categorical syllogisms (like distribution). He then lists 32 productive conjunctions. This lesson focuses on the overall condition and works through the first conjunction in detail, showing how the conclusion is derived using conversion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the pattern of the second figure
In a mixed syllogism of the second figure, the categorical premise and the consequent of the conditional share the middle term in a way that mirrors the second figure of categoricals (where the middle term is predicate in both premises). The categorical premise is sometimes called 'the categorical' and the conditional's consequent is treated as the other categorical.
The conditional premise itself is not directly a categorical, but its consequent acts as one.
2
Apply the condition for productivity
The text states: 'the categorical and the consequent must be upon the conditions considered in categoricals'. This means the categorical and the consequent must be either universal or particular, affirmative or negative, and satisfy the rules of categorical syllogisms for validity (e.g., no undistributed middle).
The conditional's status as 'consequent' means it is the part that gets combined with the categorical, hence its quantity/quality matters.
3
Understand how universality is managed
The author says 'the universality in the consequent is realized only by making it particular'. This likely refers to a technique to ensure the universal claim holds, perhaps by treating the conditional as having a particularized meaning in the context of the syllogism.
This is a subtle point; for this lesson, note that the consequent's quantity can be adjusted.
4
Examine the first conjunction
The first conjunction has: a universal affirmative categorical ('Every B is J'), and a universal affirmative conditional whose consequent is universal negative ('Always, if D is R then no A is J'). The conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional: 'Always, if D is R, then some B is A'.
The conclusion's consequent uses 'some' (particular) even though the original consequent was universal negative. The explanation mentions conversion of the consequent.
5
Verify the deduction via conversion
The author explains that the conclusion follows 'by conversion of the consequent'. Since the original consequent is 'no A is J' (universal negative), its converse (by conversion) is 'no J is A' (also universal negative). Combined with 'Every B is J', we get 'some B is not A'? Wait, let's check: From 'Every B is J' and 'No A is J', we can deduce in the second figure of categoricals that 'No B is A'. However, the conclusion here is 'some B is A'? That seems contradictory. Actually, look at the phrase: the result is 'some B is A'? In the translation: 'which is Always, every D is R, so some B is A'. That is a conditional, not a simple categorical. The deduction might involve a different operation. The formalization will clarify.
The full derivation is complex; focus on the systematic pattern.

الاقتران الأول من كلية موجبة حَمْلية وكلية موجبة شرطية تاليها كلي سالب كقولنا: (كل ب ج) و(دائماً كل ما كان د فلا شيء من أ ج) ونتيجته كلية موجبة متصلة، مقدمها مقدم الشرطية، وتاليها نتيجة اقتران التالي بالحملية وهي (دائماً كل ما كان د شيء من ب أ) وبيانه بعكس التالي.
الاقتران الثاني من كلية موجبة حَمْلية وكلية سالبة شرطية، تاليها جزئي موجب، كقولنا: (كل ب ج) و(ليس البتة إذا كان د ر فبعض أ ج) ونتيجته كلية سالبة متصلة، مقدمها مقدم الشرطية، وتاليها نتيجة اقتران التالي بالحَمْلية، وهي (ليس البتة إذا كان د ر فبعض ب أ) وبيانه بقلب المتصلة إلى الإيجاب وأخذ لازم النتيجة.
الاقتران الثالث من كليّة موجبة حَمْلية وجزئية موجبة شرطية تاليها كلي سالب كقولنا: (كل ب ج) و(قد يكون إذا كان د فلا شيء من أ ج) ونتيجته جزئية موجبة متصلة، مقدمها مقدم الشرطية، وتاليها نتيجة اقتران التالي بالحَمْلية، وهي (قد يكون إذا كان د فلا شيء من أ) وبيانه بعكس التالي.
الاقتران الرابع من كليّة موجبة حَمْلية وجزئية سالبة شرطية، تاليها جزئي موجب كقولنا: (كل ب ج) و(ليس كل ما كان د ر فبعض أ ج) ونتيجته جزئية سالبة متصلة، مقدمها مقدم الشرطية، وتاليها نتيجة اقتران التالي بالحَمْلية، وهي (ليس كل ما كان د ر فبعض ب أ) وبيانه أيضاً كما سبق.
الاقتران الخامس من كليّة سالبة حَمْلية وكليّة موجبة متصلة تاليها كلي موجب، كقولنا: (لا شيء من ب ج) و(دائماً كل ما كان د ر فكل أ ج) ونتيجته كليّة موجبة متصلة، مقدمها مقدم الشرطية، وتاليها نتيجة اقتران التالي بالحَمْلية، وهي دائماً كل ما كان د ر فلا شيء من ب أ) وبيانه بعكس الحَمْلية وجعلها مكان الكبرى، ثم بعكس النتيجة.
الاقتران السادس من كلية سالبة حَمْلية وكليّة سالبة شرطية تاليها جزئي سالب، كقولنا: (لا شيء من ب ج) و(ليس البتة إذا كان د ر فليس كل أ ج) ونتيجته كليّة سالبة شرطية، مقدمها مقدم الشرطية، وتاليها نتيجة اقتران التالي بالحَمْلية، وهي (ليس البتة إذا كان د ر فليس كل ب أ) وبيانه بقلب المتصلة إلى الإيجاب، ثم بعكس الحَمْلية وجعلها مكان الكبرى، ثم بعكس تالي النتيجة وأخذ لازمها.
الاقتران السابع من كلية سالبة حَمْلية وجزئية موجبة شرطية، تاليها كلي موجب، كقولنا: (لا شيء من ب ج) و(قد يكون إذا كان د ر فكل أ ج) ونتيجته جزئية موجبة شرطية، مقدمها مقدم الشرطية، وتاليها نتيجة اقتران التالي بالحَمْلية، وهي (قد يكون إذا كان د فلا شيء من ب أ) وبيانه كبيان الذي قبله.
And if it is according to the pattern of the second figure, its condition for productivity is that the categorical and the consequent are upon the conditions considered in categoricals, except that the conditional is a consequent, so the universality in the consequent is realized only by making it particular, as preceded in the first figure, while preserving the difference in quantity and quality. Its property in productivity is what its property was in categoricals as well, and its productive conjunctions are thirty-two conjunctions.
The first conjunction: from a universal affirmative categorical and a universal affirmative conditional whose consequent is universal negative, as we say: 'Every B is J' and 'Always, every D is R, so no A is J' and its result is a universal affirmative conditional, whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, which is 'Always, every D is R, so some B is A' and its explanation is by conversion of the consequent.
The second conjunction: from a universal affirmative categorical and a universal negative conditional, whose consequent is particular affirmative, as we say: 'Every B is J' and 'Not at all, if D is R, then some A is J' and its result is a universal negative conditional, whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, which is 'Not at all, if D is R, then some B is A' and its explanation is by converting the conditional to affirmation and taking the necessary of the result.
The third conjunction: from a universal affirmative categorical and a particular affirmative conditional whose consequent is universal negative, as we say: 'Every B is J' and 'It may be that if D is R, then no A is J' and its result is a particular affirmative conditional, whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, which is 'It may be that if D is R, then no A' and its explanation is by conversion of the consequent.
The fourth conjunction: from a universal affirmative categorical and a particular negative conditional, whose consequent is particular affirmative, as we say: 'Every B is J' and 'Not every D is R, then some A is J' and its result is a particular negative conditional, whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, which is 'Not every D is R, then some B is A' and its explanation is also as has preceded.
The fifth conjunction: from a universal negative categorical and a universal affirmative conditional whose consequent is universal affirmative, as we say: 'No B is J' and 'Always, every D is R, so every A is J' and its result is a universal affirmative conditional, whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, which is 'Always, every D is R, so no B is A' and its explanation is by conversion of the categorical and making it in place of the major premise, then by conversion of the result.
The sixth conjunction: from a universal negative categorical and a universal negative conditional whose consequent is particular negative, as we say: 'No B is J' and 'Not at all, if D is R, then not every A is J' and its result is a universal negative conditional, whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, which is 'Not at all, if D is R, then not every B is A' and its explanation is by converting the conditional to affirmation, then by conversion of the categorical and making it in place of the major premise, then by converting the consequent of the result and taking its necessary.
The seventh conjunction: from a universal negative categorical and a particular affirmative conditional, whose consequent is universal affirmative, as we say: 'No B is J' and 'It may be that if D is R, then every A is J' and its result is a particular affirmative conditional, whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, which is 'It may be that if D is R, then no B is A' and its explanation is like the explanation of that before it.
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage defines the second figure of mixed syllogisms (al-shakl al-thani) and lists seven of its eight productive conjunctions (the eighth is cut off). The author first states the condition for productivity: the categorical premise and the consequent of the conditional must satisfy the conditions of categorical syllogisms (e.g., universal/particular, affirmative/negative), but the conditional itself is the consequent of a larger conditional? Actually, the text is technical: 'أن تكون الحمْلية والتالي على الشروط المعتبرة في الحمْليات إلا أن تكون الشرطية تالية' means the categorical and the consequent must meet the conditions for categoricals, except that the conditional is a consequent (i.e., the conditional premise is treated as a consequent in the compound?). Then he says universality in the consequent is achieved by making it particular, as in the first figure, while preserving differences in quantity and quality. Its property in productivity is the same as in categorical syllogisms. The productive conjunctions are 32 (presumably 8 from universal categorical + 8 from particular categorical, but he only details 8 in this section). He then enumerates the first seven conjunctions of the set where the categorical is universal. Each conjunction gives a specific mood: categorical premise (universal affirmative or negative) combined with a conditional premise (universal/particular, affirmative/negative), with a specific conclusion. The author provides an example using letters (B, J, D, R, A) and then describes the proof (bayān) for each, often involving conversion (ˈaks) or other transformations. For instance, the first conjunction takes a universal affirmative categorical and a universal affirmative conditional with a universal negative consequent, and derives a universal affirmative conditional conclusion. The explanation is 'by conversion of the consequent'. The subsequent conjunctions follow a pattern, varying the quantity and quality of the premises and consequent. This systematic enumeration shows the rigor of Aristotelian logic as adapted in the Islamic tradition, focusing on the formal conditions for valid syllogisms.

The second figure of mixed syllogisms requires the categorical premise and the consequent of the conditional to obey the same formal conditions as categorical syllogisms, enabling 32 productive conjunctions.

The Syllogism
intermediate
12 min
Mixed Syllogisms with Particular Categoricals: Ninth to Sixteenth Conjunctions
Enumerates eight valid moods with particular categorical premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the conclusion for any mixed categorical-conditional syllogism from the ninth to sixteenth conjunctions by identifying the premises and applying conversion or negation operations.

Core Idea
The ninth to sixteenth conjunctions handle particular categorical premises; the conclusion is built by combining the conditional's antecedent with a converted or negated consequent.

This passage systematizes eight valid moods where one premise is a particular categorical (e.g., 'Some B is C') and the other is a universal or particular conditional (e.g., 'Always if D is R then no A is B'). These moods are essential for evaluating arguments in Islamic jurisprudence that combine a general conditional rule with a specific case. Understanding them equips you to check the validity of inferences in fiqh and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the categorical premise's quantity and quality
In these conjunctions, the categorical premise is always particular (جزئية). Note whether it is affirmative (موجبة) or negative (سالبة). For example, 'Some B is C' is particular affirmative; 'Not every B is C' is particular negative.
2
Identify the conditional premise's quantity, quality, and consequent's type
The conditional premise may be universal (كليّة) or particular (جزئية), affirmative (موجبة) or negative (سالبة). Its consequent (تالي) has its own quantity and quality. For instance, 'Always whenever D is R, no A is B' has a universal negative consequent.
3
Determine the conclusion's structure
The conclusion is always a conditional (متّصلة) with the same antecedent (مقدّم) as the conditional premise. The consequent is derived by combining the categorical premise's predicate with the conditional's consequent. For example, if categorical is 'Some B is C' and conditional is 'Always if D is R then no A is B', the conclusion is 'Always if D is R then some B is not A'.
The consequent often involves conversion (عكس) or negation of the original consequent.
4
Recognize the explanation method used
The author gives a brief بيان (explanation) for each conjunction. Common techniques include conversion of the consequent (عكس التالي) or inverting the conditional to affirmation (قلب المتصلة إلى الإيجاب). Note how the operations preserve the conclusion's validity.
5
Apply to a concrete Islamic example
Map the abstract variables to a fiqh context. For instance, let B = 'acts', C = 'obligatory', A = 'permissible acts', D = 'act is commanded', R = (no matter). Then 'Some acts are obligatory' and 'Always if an act is commanded, no permissible act is obligatory' yields 'Always if an act is commanded, some obligatory acts are not permissible'. This pattern checks reasoning about acts being commanded.

الاقتران التاسع من جزئية موجبة حَمْلية وكليّة موجبة شرطية تاليها كلي سالب كقولنا: (بعض "ب" "ج") و(دائماً كل ما كان "د ر" فلا شيء من "أ" "ب") ونتيجته كلية موجبة شرطية، مقدمها مقدم الشرطية وتاليها نتيجة
