تاليها جزئي موجب، كقولنا: (بعض "ب" "ج") و(ليس كل ما كان "د" "ر" فبعض "أ" "ج") ونتيجته موجبة متصلة، مقدمها مقدم الشرطية، وتاليها نتيجة اقتران التالي بالحَمْلية وهي ليس كل ما كان "د" "ر" فبعض "ب" "أ") الاقتران الذي قبله. الاقتران السادس عشر من جزئية سالبة حَمْلية وجزئية سالبة شرطية تاليها جزئي سالب، كقولنا: (ليس كل "ب" "ج") و(ليس كل ما كان "د" "ر" فبعض "أ" ليس "ج") ونتيجته جزئية سالبة شرطية، مقدمها الثالث فشروطه وخواصه على ما سبق في الحَمْليات.
The ninth conjunction: from a particular affirmative categorical and a universal affirmative conditional whose consequent is a universal negative, such as our saying: (Some B is C) and (Always whenever D is R, no A is B). Its conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional, whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional and whose consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, namely: (Always whenever D is R, some B is not A). Its explanation is by conversion of the consequent. The tenth conjunction: from a particular affirmative categorical and a universal negative conditional whose consequent is a particular affirmative, such as our saying: (Some B is C) and (Absolutely not if D is R then some A is C). Its conclusion is a universal negative conditional, whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional and whose consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, namely: (Absolutely not if D is R then some B is not A). Its explanation is by inverting the conditional to affirmation and taking the necessary of the conclusion. The eleventh conjunction: from a particular affirmative categorical and a particular affirmative conditional, whose consequent is a universal negative, such as our saying: (Some B is C) and (It may be that if D is R then no A is C). Its conclusion is a particular affirmative conditional, whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional and whose consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, namely: (It may be that if D is R then some B is not A). Its explanation is by conversion of the consequent. The twelfth conjunction: from a particular affirmative categorical and a particular negative conditional, whose consequent is a particular affirmative, such as our saying: (Some B is C) and (Not every D is R then some A is C). Its conclusion is an affirmative conditional, whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional and whose consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, namely: (Not every D is R then some B is A). Its explanation is as if the categorical were universal. The thirteenth conjunction: from a particular negative categorical and a universal affirmative conditional, whose consequent is a universal affirmative, such as our saying: (Not every B is C) and (Always every D is R then every A is C). Its conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional, whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional and whose consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, namely: (Always every D is R then not every B is A). Its explanation is as follows: if it is true that (not every B is C) and (every D is R then every A is C), then it is necessary that if D is R then not every B is A, because every A is necessarily included under C. And if A were constant for every B, then C would be constant for every B, but it was said (not every B is C), which is a contradiction. The fourteenth conjunction: from a particular negative categorical and a universal negative conditional, whose consequent is a particular negative, such as our saying: (Not every B is C) and (Absolutely not if D is R then not every A is C). Its conclusion is a universal negative conditional, whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional and whose consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, namely: (Absolutely not if D is R then not every B is not A). Its explanation is by inverting the conditional to affirmation, then you do as we taught you in what was said. The fifteenth conjunction: from a particular negative categorical and a particular affirmative conditional, whose consequent is a universal affirmative, such as our saying: (Not every B is C) and (It may be that if D is R then every A is C). Its conclusion is a particular affirmative conditional, whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional and whose consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, namely: (It may be that if D is R then not every B is A). Its explanation is like the explanation of the conjunction before it. The sixteenth conjunction: from a particular negative categorical and a particular negative conditional, whose consequent is a particular negative, such as our saying: (Not every B is C) and (Not every D is R then some A is not C). Its conclusion is a particular negative conditional, whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional and whose consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, namely: (Not every D is R then every B is A). Its explanation is like the explanation of what is before it. This is if the modality of the categorical and the consequent are pure of one kind, or if their modalities are overlapping. But if their modalities are not overlapping, then from the categorical and the conditional there may be composed sixteen conjunctions, which are those whose consequents are equal to the categorical in quality, so consider it. And if the sharing between the categorical and the consequent of the conditional is in the manner of the third figure, then its conditions and properties are as previously in the categoricals.
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage enumerates eight valid moods (from the ninth to the sixteenth) of mixed conjunctive syllogisms, where one premise is categorical (حملیة) and the other is conditional (شرطیة). The author systematically presents each conjunction (اقتران) with its specific quantities and qualities of premises, provides an example using placeholder letters (أ، ب، ج، د، ر), states the resulting conclusion, and gives a brief explanation (بیان) of how the conclusion is derived, often using conversion (عكس) or other logical operations. The passage concludes with a general remark about the modalities of the premises and notes that if the modalities are not overlapping, there can be sixteen such conjunctions, and that if the shared term follows the pattern of the third figure, the rules already discussed for categorical syllogisms apply. The examples are purely formal but can be mapped to Islamic legal reasoning, e.g., "Some actions are obligatory" and "Whenever an action is commanded, it is good" leading to "Some commanded actions are not obligatory" – though the author uses abstract variables. The teacher should emphasize that this meticulous enumeration demonstrates the logicians' concern for completeness in valid argument forms.

A) Always whenever D is R, some B is not A
B) Some B is A

Key Takeaway
The ninth to sixteenth conjunctions complete the mixed syllogism system by showing how particular categorical premises combine with conditional premises through operations on the consequent.

The 14th Conjunction: Particular Negative Categorical + Universal Negative Conditional
Author defines the 14th conjunction with example and demonstration method.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive the conclusion of the 14th conjunction, which combines a particular negative categorical with a universal negative conditional, and state its logical form.

Core Idea
The 14th conjunction yields a universal negative conditional by integrating the categorical with the conditional's consequent.

The 14th conjunction is the first pattern combining a particular negative categorical with a conditional. Here, the conditional is universal negative with a particular negative consequent. Understanding this is essential for analyzing compound arguments in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises
The categorical is 'Not every B is J' (particular negative). The conditional is 'It is never the case that if D is R, then not every A is J' (universal negative conditional with particular negative consequent).
Note the quality and quantity of each premise.
2
Structure the conclusion
The conclusion is a conditional. Its antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional premise: 'D is R'. Its consequent is derived by combining the categorical premise with the consequent of the conditional using a categorical syllogism.
3
Derive the consequent
The demonstration involves converting the conditional to affirmative (قلب المتصلة إلى الإيجاب) and applying the rules you already know. The result is 'Not every B is not A', which is equivalent to 'Some B is A'.
This becomes the consequent of the final conditional.
4
Assemble the full conclusion
The final conclusion is 'It is never the case that if D is R, then not every B is not A' (a universal negative conditional).
This matches the author's statement.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاقتران الرابع عشر من جزئية سالبة حَمْلية وكليّة سالبة شرطية تاليها جزئي سالب كقولنا: (ليس كل "ب" "ج") و(ليس البتة إذا كان "د" "ر" فليس كل "أ" "ج") ونتيجته كلية سالبة متصلة مقدمها مقدم الشرطية وتاليها نتيجة اقتران التالي بالحَمْلية، وهي ليس البتة إذا كان "د" "ر" فليس كل "ب" ليس ("أ") وبيانه بقلب المتصلة إلى الإيجاب، ثم إن تفعل كما عرفناك في ما قيل.
الاقتران الخامس عشر من جزئية سالبة حَمْلية وجزئية موجبة شرطية تاليها كلي موجب، كقولنا: (ليس كل "ب" "ج") و(قد يكون إذا كان "د" "ر" فكل "أ" "ج") ونتيجته جزئية موجبة متصلة، مقدمها مقدم الشرطية، وتاليها نتيجة اقتران التالي بالحَمْلية، وهي قد يكون إذا كان "د" "ر" فليس كل "ب" "أ") وبيانه كبيان الاقتران الذي قبله.
الاقتران السادس عشر من جزئية سالبة حَمْلية وجزئية سالبة شرطية تاليها جزئي سالب، كقولنا: (ليس كل "ب" "ج") و(ليس كل ما كان "د" "ر" فبعض "أ" ليس "ج") ونتيجته جزئية سالبة شرطية، مقدمها مقدم الشرطية، وتاليها نتيجة اقتران التالي بالحَمْلية وهي ليس كل ما كان "د" "ر" فكل "ب" "أ") وبيانه كبيان ما قبله.
هذا إن كانت جهة الحَمْلية والتالي صرفة من جنس واحد، أو أن جهتهما متداخلتان، وأما إن كانت جهتاهما غير متداخلتين فقد تأتلف من الحَمْلية ومن الشرطية ستة عشر اقتراناً، وهي ما التوالي فيها مساوية للحملية في الكيف، فاعتبره.
The fourteenth conjunction: from a particular negative categorical and a universal negative conditional whose consequent is a particular negative, as we say: "Not every B is J" and "It is never the case that if D is R, then not every A is J" — its conclusion is a universal negative conditional, its antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the result of combining the consequent with the categorical, namely "It is never the case that if D is R, then not every B is not A". Its demonstration is by converting the conditional to affirmative, then you do as we taught you in what was said.
The fifteenth conjunction: from a particular negative categorical and a particular affirmative conditional whose consequent is a universal affirmative, as we say: "Not every B is J" and "It may be that if D is R, then every A is J" — its conclusion is a particular affirmative conditional, its antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the result of combining the consequent with the categorical, namely "It may be that if D is R, then not every B is A". Its demonstration is like the demonstration of the previous conjunction.
The sixteenth conjunction: from a particular negative categorical and a particular negative conditional whose consequent is a particular negative, as we say: "Not every B is J" and "Not every time when D is R, some A is not J" — its conclusion is a particular negative conditional, its antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the result of combining the consequent with the categorical, namely "Not every time when D is R, every B is A". Its demonstration is like the demonstration of what preceded it.
This is if the modality of the categorical and the consequent are pure of one genus, or if their modalities are overlapping. But if their modalities are not overlapping, then sixteen conjunctions may be composed from the categorical and the conditional, those in which the consequents are equal to the categorical in quality, so consider that.
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage defines the fourteenth, fifteenth, and sixteenth valid conjunctions (اقترانات) that arise from combining a particular negative categorical premise (جزئية سالبة حملية) with a conditional premise (شرطية) that is either universal negative, particular affirmative, or particular negative. The author follows a consistent pattern: state the premise types, give an example using logical variables (ب, ج, د, ر, أ), state the conclusion (which is always a conditional whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional premise and whose consequent is derived by combining the consequent of the conditional with the categorical premise via a categorical syllogism), and briefly indicate the method of demonstration (بيان). For the fourteenth conjunction, the demonstration involves converting the conditional to its affirmative counterpart. For the fifteenth, it is similar to the fourteenth. For the sixteenth, it is similar to the previous ones. The passage ends with a general remark: if the modality (جهة) of the categorical and the consequent are of the same genus or overlapping, the above pattern holds; otherwise, there are sixteen conjunctions where the consequents match the categorical in quality. The student should note that the variables (ب, ج, etc.) stand for any terms, and the modalities (دائم, قد يكون, ليس البتة) indicate temporal or modal force. This is a technical classification of valid mixed syllogisms in the Islamic logical tradition.

Checkpoint
In the 14th conjunction, the categorical premise is 'Not every B is J' and the conditional premise is 'It is never the case that if D is R, then not every A is J'. What is the conclusion?
A) It is never the case that if D is R, then not every B is not A
B) It is always the case that if D is R, then every B is A
C) Not every B is J and it is never the case that if D is R, then not every A is J
D) If D is R, then not every B is A

Key Takeaway
The 14th conjunction yields a universal negative conditional whose consequent is derived by a categorical syllogism from the original categorical and the conditional's consequent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the categorical premise 'Not every one who prays is sincere' and the conditional premise 'It is never the case that if one is a hypocrite, then not every one who prays is sincere', derive the conclusion of the 14th conjunction.

The 15th Conjunction: Particular Negative Categorical + Particular Affirmative Conditional
Author defines the 15th conjunction with example and states its demonstration is like the previous.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive the conclusion of the 15th conjunction, which combines a particular negative categorical with a particular affirmative conditional that has a universal affirmative consequent.

Core Idea
The 15th conjunction produces a particular affirmative conditional by integrating the categorical with the conditional's consequent.

The 15th conjunction is the second pattern in this set. Here, the conditional is particular affirmative with a universal affirmative consequent. The method mirrors the 14th but yields a different conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises
The categorical is 'Not every B is J' (particular negative). The conditional is 'It may be that if D is R, then every A is J' (particular affirmative conditional with universal affirmative consequent).
Note the modality: 'may be' indicates possibility.
2
Structure the conclusion
The conclusion is a conditional. Its antecedent is 'D is R'. Its consequent is derived by combining the categorical premise with the consequent of the conditional (every A is J).
3
Derive the consequent
The demonstration is like the 14th. The resulting consequent is 'Not every B is A', which is a particular affirmative (since 'not every B is A' is equivalent to 'some B is not A'? Wait: 'Not every B is A' is particular negative. But the passage says the conclusion is جزئية موجبة متصلة (particular affirmative conditional). The consequent is 'ليس كل ب أ' which is particular negative. There's a nuance: the conditional as a whole is 'قد يكون إذا كان د ر فليس كل ب أ' which is a particular affirmative conditional (it affirms possibility). So it's correct.
The consequent 'Not every B is A' is a particular negative categorical, but the conditional's modality is possibility, making the whole conditional particular affirmative.
4
Assemble the full conclusion
The final conclusion is 'It may be that if D is R, then not every B is A' (a particular affirmative conditional).

The 16th Conjunction: Particular Negative Categorical + Particular Negative Conditional
Author defines the 16th conjunction with example and states its demonstration is like the previous.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive the conclusion of the 16th conjunction, which combines a particular negative categorical with a particular negative conditional that has a particular negative consequent.

Core Idea
The 16th conjunction produces a particular negative conditional by integrating the categorical with the conditional's consequent.

The 16th conjunction is the third pattern. Here, the conditional is particular negative with a particular negative consequent. The method is again similar to the previous conjunctions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises
The categorical is 'Not every B is J' (particular negative). The conditional is 'Not every time when D is R, some A is not J' (particular negative conditional with particular negative consequent).
Note: 'Not every time' indicates particular negative modality.
2
Structure the conclusion
The conclusion is a conditional. Its antecedent is 'D is R'. Its consequent is derived by combining the categorical premise with the consequent of the conditional (some A is not J).
3
Derive the consequent
The demonstration is like the previous one. The resulting consequent is 'Every B is A', which is a universal affirmative. Wait, the passage says: 'ليس كل ما كان د ر فكل ب أ' which is 'Not every time when D is R, every B is A'. That is a particular negative conditional. The consequent is 'كل ب أ' (every B is A). So the derived consequent is a universal affirmative.
The categorical 'Not every B is J' and the original consequent 'some A is not J' combine to produce 'Every B is A'? Let's check: Not every B is J (i.e., some B is not J). Some A is not J. From these, can we deduce Every B is A? That seems invalid. But the author asserts it is correct. The demonstration involves a conversion step. So we follow the text.
4
Assemble the full conclusion
The final conclusion is 'Not every time when D is R, every B is A' (a particular negative conditional).

The Syllogism

Mixed Syllogisms: Negative Conditional with Particular Categorical
Introduction and listing of five new conjunctions with demonstrations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the pattern of the first five conjunctions and derive the conclusion for a given pair of premises.

Core Idea
24 moods arise when the conditional is negative and categorical particular; the first five follow a pattern of converting terms.

The author continues the art of conjunctive syllogisms, now specifying a new rule: when the conditional is negative and the categorical is particular, the consequent must be particular, yielding 24 productive moods. The first five are listed with a universal affirmative categorical premise and varying conditional forms. This lesson will guide you through recognizing the structure and generating the conclusion using conversion rules.

Guided Path
1
Identify the categorical premise
In each of the first five conjunctions, the categorical premise is always 'Every B is J' (universal affirmative). This is fixed for these examples.
The rule applies when the categorical is particular, but the examples here use a universal one; still, the pattern holds.
2
Identify the conditional premise and its consequent
The conditional premise varies in quality (affirmative or negative) and quantity (universal or particular) of its consequent. Note the four types: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative.
The first four conjunctions have both premises universal affirmative; the fifth has a universal negative conditional.
3
Note the conclusion pattern
The conclusion is always a conditional: its antecedent is the same as the antecedent of the conditional premise, and its consequent is the result of combining the original consequent with the categorical premise via a categorical syllogism.
The combined result often requires conversion of the categorical or the consequent.
4
Apply the conversion rule
For the first conjunction, from 'Every B is J' and 'Every B is A', we get 'Some J is A' by converting 'Every B is J' to 'Some J is B' and then using a first-figure syllogism. The demonstration is 'by conversion of the categorical'.
For the third, 'conversion of the consequent' is used. For the fifth, the conditional is first converted to affirmative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تتمة الفن الأول: في تعريف صور القياسات الاقترانية
وإن كانت الشرطية سالبة والحَمْلية جزئية، فيجب أن يكون التالي جزئياً، وقرائنه المنتجة أربعة وعشرون اقتراناً:
الاقتران الأول من كليتين موجبتين، والتالي من الشرطية كلي موجب كقولنا: (كل "ب" "ج") و(دائماً كل ما كان "د" "ر" فكل "ب" "أ") ونتيجته كلية موجبة متصلة، مقدمها مقدم الشرطية، وتاليها نتيجة اقتران التالي بالحَمْلية، وهي دائماً كل ما كان "د" "ر" فبعض "ج" "أ") وبيانه بعكس الحملية.
الاقتران الثاني: من كليتين موجبتين، والتالي من الشرطية كلي سالب، كقولنا: (كل "ب" "ج") و(دائماً كل ما كان "د" "ر" فلا شيء من "ب" "أ") ونتيجته كلية موجبة متصلة، مقدمها مقدم الشرطية، وتاليها نتيجة اقتران التالي بالحملية، وهي (دائماً كل ما كان "د" "ر" فبعض "ج" ليس "أ") وبيانه كبيان ما قبله.
الاقتران الثالث من كليتين موجبتين، والتالي من الشرطية جزئي موجب كقولنا: (كل "ب" "ج") و(دائما كل ما كان "د" "ر" فبعض "ب" "أ") ونتيجته كلية موجبة متصلة، مقدمها مقدم الشرطية، وتاليها نتيجة اقتران التالي بالحَمْلية، وهي (دائماً كل ما كان "د" "ر" فبعض "ج" "أ") وبيانه بعكس التالي.
الاقتران الرابع من كليتين موجبتين، والتالي من الشرطية جزئي سالب، كقولنا: (كل "ب" "ج") و(دائماً كل ما كان "د" "ر" فليس كل "ب" "أ") ونتيجته كليّة موجبة متصلة، مقدمها مقدم الشرطية، وتاليها نتيجة اقتران التالي بالحَمْلية، وهي (دائماً كل ما كان "د" "ر" فليس كل "ج" "أ") وبيانه كبيان الاقتران الثاني.
الاقتران الخامس من كليّة موجبة حَمْلية وكليّة سالبة شرطية تاليها جزئي سالب، كقولنا: (كل "ب" "ج") و(ليس البتة إذا كان "د" "ر" فليس كل "ب" "أ") ونتيجته كليّة سالبة متصلة، مقدمها مقدم الشرطية، وتاليها نتيجة اقتران التالي بالحَمْلية، وهي (ليس البتة إذا كان "د" "ر" فلا شيء من "ج" "أ") وبيانه بقلب المتصلة إلى الإيجاب، ثم تفعل كما عرف في الاقتران الثاني.
Continuation of the first art: On defining the forms of conjunctive syllogisms.
And if the conditional is negative and the categorical is particular, then the consequent must be particular, and its productive moods are twenty-four conjunctions:
The first conjunction: from two universal affirmatives, and the consequent of the conditional is universal affirmative, as we say: "Every B is J" and "Always, every thing that is D is R, then every B is A" — its conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional, its antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the result of combining the consequent with the categorical, namely "Always, every thing that is D is R, then some J is A". Its demonstration is by conversion of the categorical.
The second conjunction: from two universal affirmatives, and the consequent of the conditional is universal negative, as we say: "Every B is J" and "Always, every thing that is D is R, then no B is A" — its conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional, its antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the result of combining the consequent with the categorical, namely "Always, every thing that is D is R, then some J is not A". Its demonstration is like the demonstration of the previous one.
The third conjunction: from two universal affirmatives, and the consequent of the conditional is particular affirmative, as we say: "Every B is J" and "Always, every thing that is D is R, then some B is A" — its conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional, its antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the result of combining the consequent with the categorical, namely "Always, every thing that is D is R, then some J is A". Its demonstration is by conversion of the consequent.
The fourth conjunction: from two universal affirmatives, and the consequent of the conditional is particular negative, as we say: "Every B is J" and "Always, every thing that is D is R, then not every B is A" — its conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional, its antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the result of combining the consequent with the categorical, namely "Always, every thing that is D is R, then not every J is A". Its demonstration is like the demonstration of the second conjunction.
The fifth conjunction: from a universal affirmative categorical and a universal negative conditional whose consequent is particular negative, as we say: "Every B is J" and "It is never the case that if D is R, then not every B is A" — its conclusion is a universal negative conditional, its antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the result of combining the consequent with the categorical, namely "It is never the case that if D is R, then no J is A". Its demonstration is by converting the conditional to affirmative, then you do as known in the second conjunction.
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This passage continues the classification of mixed syllogisms from the first art (الفن الأول) of conjunctive syllogisms (القياسات الاقترانية). The author states a new rule: when the conditional premise (الشرطية) is negative and the categorical premise (الحملية) is particular, the consequent of the conditional must be particular, and there are 24 productive conjunctions (اقترانات منتجة). The passage then details the first five of these conjunctions. Each conjunction follows a consistent pattern: (1) the categorical premise is 'Every B is J' (universal affirmative), (2) the conditional premise varies in quality (affirmative or negative) and quantity of its consequent (universal or particular), and (3) the conclusion is always a conditional whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional premise and whose consequent is derived by combining the original consequent with the categorical premise via a categorical syllogism. For the first to fourth conjunctions, both premises are universal affirmative, but the conditional's consequent varies (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative). The demonstration (بيان) uses conversion of the categorical (عكس الحملية) for the first, a similar method for the second and fourth, conversion of the consequent (عكس التالي) for the third. The fifth conjunction differs: the categorical is universal affirmative but the conditional is universal negative with a particular negative consequent; here the demonstration involves converting the conditional to affirmative (قلب المتصلة إلى الإيجاب) and then using the method of the second conjunction. The author's systematic approach shows how valid conclusions are derived by applying known conversion rules.

شرطية
اقتران
عكس الحملية

Demonstration Methods: Conversion and Transformation
Detailed demonstration of the first five conjunctions using conversion rules

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the three demonstration methods: conversion of categorical, conversion of consequent, and converting negative conditional to affirmative.

Core Idea
Three logical transformations underpin the demonstrations: 'aks al-hamliyya, 'aks al-tali, and qalb al-muttasila ila al-ijab.

The author not only lists the conjunctions but also provides demonstrations (بيان). These are not arbitrary; they apply specific conversion rules that are foundational in Arabic logic. This lesson will teach you how each demonstration works, using the examples from the first five conjunctions.

Guided Path
1
Understand conversion of the categorical ('aks al-hamliyya)
In the first conjunction, the demonstration uses this rule. From 'Every B is J', we infer 'Some J is B'. Then with the conditional consequent 'Every B is A', we perform a first-figure syllogism to get 'Some J is A'. The second and fourth conjunctions use a similar method.
The conversion of a universal affirmative yields a particular affirmative.
2
Understand conversion of the consequent ('aks al-tali)
In the third conjunction, the consequent is 'Some B is A'. Converting this gives 'Some A is B'. Then with 'Every B is J', we get 'Some A is J', which converts to 'Some J is A'. The passage simply says 'by conversion of the consequent'.
This is the same as conversion of a particular affirmative.
3
Understand converting the conditional to affirmative (qalb al-muttasila ila al-ijab)
In the fifth conjunction, the conditional is negative: 'It is never the case that if D is R then not every B is A'. This is logically equivalent to 'Always, if D is R then every B is A' after removing double negatives. This step is 'converting the conditional to affirmative'. Then we apply the method of the second conjunction.
This transformation relies on the equivalence of 'not (if p then not q)' to 'if p then q'.
4
Practice with all five conjunctions
Work through each conjunction and verify the conclusion using the stated demonstration. Pay attention to when the demonstration is 'like the previous one'.

The First Four Productive Conjunctions: Affirmative Conditionals
Enumerates first four productive conjunctions with affirmative conditionals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive the conclusion of any conjunction where the conditional is universal affirmative by converting the categorical premise.

Core Idea
When the conditional is affirmative, the categorical is converted by limitation to produce a particular conclusion.

In conjunctive syllogisms, the major premise is a conditional (shartiyya) and the minor is a categorical (hamliyya). The author presents the first four productive conjunctions where both premises are universal affirmative. The key operation is conversion ('aks) of the categorical or the conditional's consequent to obtain a valid conditional conclusion. This lesson walks you through the pattern.

Guided Path
1
Identify the categorical premise
In the first four conjunctions, the categorical is always universal affirmative: 'Every B is C' (kulliyya mujiba). This premise is fixed across all four.
The variables B, C, D, R, A are placeholders for terms in Islamic sciences.
2
Examine the conditional's consequent
The conditional is universal affirmative: 'Always, if D is R then ...'. The consequent varies: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, or particular negative. Note its quantity and quality.
3
Convert the categorical premise
Since the categorical is 'Every B is C', it converts by limitation to 'Some C is B'. This allows the consequent's subject (B) to be replaced by C in the conclusion.
Conversion preserves truth but weakens universality.
4
Form the conclusion
The conclusion is a conditional with the same antecedent as the premise, and its consequent is obtained by substituting C for B in the original consequent, using the converted categorical.
For example, if consequent was 'Every B is A', after conversion it becomes 'Some C is A'.
5
Verify validity
Check that the conclusion follows: the categorical provides a link between B and C, so the relation between D, R, and A is preserved.
The author's explanation (byan) confirms this via conversion rules.

الاقتران السابع من كليّة موجبة حَمْلية وكلية سالبة شرطية تاليها كلي سالب كقولنا : (كل "ب" "ج") و(ليس البتة إذا كان "د" "ر" فلا شيء من شيء من "ب" "أ") ونتيجته كلية سالبة متصلة، مقدمها مقدم الشرطية، وتاليها نتيجة اقتران التالي بالحَمْلية وهي (ليس البتة إذا كان "د" "ر" فلا شيء من "ج" "أ") وبيانه بقلب المتصلة إلى الإيجاب، فإنه يعود إلى الاقتران الثالث، وقد عرف إنتاجه.
الاقتران الثامن من كليّة موجبة حَمْلية وكليّة سالبة شرطية تاليها كلي موجب كقولنا: (كل "ب" "ج") و(ليس البتة إذا كان "د" "ر" فكل "ب" "أ") ونتيجته كلية سالبة متصلة، مقدمها مقدم الشرطية، وتاليها نتيجة اقتران التالي بالحَمْلية، وهي (ليس البتة إذا كان "د" "ر" فكل "ج" "أ") وبيانه بقلب المتصلة إلى الإيجاب.
الاقتران التاسع من كليّة موجبة حَمْلية وجزئية موجبة شرطية تاليها كلي موجب، كقولنا : (كل "ب" "ج") و(قد يكون إذا كان "د" "ر" فكل "ب" "أ") ونتيجته جزئية موجبة شرطية، مقدمها مقدم الشرطية، وتاليها نتيجة اقتران التالي بالحمْلية وهي (قد يكون إذا كان "د" "ر" فبعض "ج" "أ") وبيانه كبيان الاقتران الأول.

Completion of the first art: on defining the forms of conjunctive syllogisms. And if the conditional is negative and the categorical is particular, then the consequent must be particular, and its productive conjunctions are twenty-four conjunctions: The first conjunction: from two universal affirmatives, and the consequent of the conditional is universal affirmative, as we say: 'Every B is C' and 'Always, every D is R implies every B is A' and its conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional, its antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, namely 'Always, every D is R implies some C is A', and its explanation is by conversion of the categorical. The second conjunction: from two universal affirmatives, and the consequent of the conditional is universal negative, as we say: 'Every B is C' and 'Always, every D is R implies no B is A' and its conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional, its antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, namely 'Always, every D is R implies some C is not A', and its explanation is like the explanation of the previous. The third conjunction: from two universal affirmatives, and the consequent of the conditional is particular affirmative, as we say: 'Every B is C' and 'Always, every D is R implies some B is A' and its conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional, its antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, namely 'Always, every D is R implies some C is A', and its explanation is by conversion of the consequent. The fourth conjunction: from two universal affirmatives, and the consequent of the conditional is particular negative, as we say: 'Every B is C' and 'Always, every D is R implies not every B is A' and its conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional, its antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, namely 'Always, every D is R implies not every C is A', and its explanation is like the explanation of the second conjunction. The fifth conjunction: from a universal affirmative categorical and a universal negative conditional whose consequent is particular negative, as we say: 'Every B is C' and 'Never, if D is R then not every B is A' and its conclusion is a universal negative conditional, its antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, namely 'Never, if D is R then no C is A', and its explanation is by converting the conditional into affirmative, then you do as was known in the second conjunction. The sixth conjunction: from a universal affirmative categorical and a universal negative conditional whose consequent is universal affirmative, as we say: 'Every B is C' and 'Never, if D is R then every B is A' and its conclusion is a universal negative conditional, its antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, namely 'Never, if D R then every C is A', and its explanation is by converting the conditional into affirmative, for it returns to the third conjunction, and its product is known. The seventh conjunction: from a universal affirmative categorical and a universal negative conditional whose consequent is universal negative, as we say: 'Every B is C' and 'Never, if D is R then no B is A' and its conclusion is a universal negative conditional, its antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, namely 'Never, if D is R then no C is A', and its explanation is by converting the conditional into affirmative, for it returns to the third conjunction, and its product is known. The eighth conjunction: from a universal affirmative categorical and a universal negative conditional whose consequent is universal affirmative, as we say: 'Every B is C' and 'Never, if D is R then every B is A' and its conclusion is a universal negative conditional, its antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, namely 'Never, if D is R then every C is A', and its explanation is by converting the conditional into affirmative. The ninth conjunction: from a universal affirmative categorical and a particular affirmative conditional whose consequent is universal affirmative, as we say: 'Every B is C' and 'Sometimes, if D is R then every B is A' and its conclusion is a particular affirmative conditional, its antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, namely 'Sometimes, if D is R then some C is A', and its explanation is like the explanation of the first conjunction. The tenth conjunction: from a universal affirmative categorical and a particular affirmative conditional whose consequent is universal negative, as we say: 'Every B is C' and 'Sometimes, if D is R then no B is A' and its conclusion is a particular affirmative conditional, its antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, namely 'Sometimes, if D is R then some C is not A', and its explanation is like the explanation of the second conjunction. The eleventh conjunction: from a universal affirmative categorical and a particular affirmative conditional, its consequent is particular affirmative, as we say: 'Every B is C' and 'Sometimes, if D is R then some B is A'
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This passage is a systematic enumeration of productive conjunctions (iqtiranat) for conditional syllogisms (qiyasat iqtiraniyya), where the major premise is a conditional (shartiyya) and the minor is a categorical (hamliyya). The author begins by stating that if the conditional is negative and the categorical is particular, the consequent must be particular, and there are twenty-four such conjunctions, but only the first eleven are detailed here. Each conjunction is defined by the quantity (universal/particular) and quality (affirmative/negative) of both the categorical and the conditional's consequent, and the resulting conclusion. For example, the first conjunction takes a universal affirmative categorical ('Every B is C') and a universal affirmative conditional with a universal affirmative consequent ('Always, if D is R then every B is A') and yields a universal affirmative conditional conclusion ('Always, if D is R then some C is A'). The author explains that this is done by conversion ('aks) of the categorical premise. Subsequent conjunctions follow similar patterns but may involve conversion of the conditional's consequent or conversion of the conditional itself (e.g., converting a negative conditional into an affirmative one). The fifth through eighth conjunctions involve a universal negative conditional, and the ninth through eleventh involve particular conditional premises. This enumeration is crucial for understanding how to derive valid conclusions in conjunctive syllogisms, a core part of Aristotelian logic as adopted in Islamic logic. In practice, such syllogisms are used in kalam arguments, e.g., 'If the world is originated, it requires a creator; the world is originated; therefore it requires a creator.'

In the first conjunction, if the categorical is 'Every B is C' and the conditional is 'Always, if D is R then every B is A', what is the conclusion?
A) Always, if D is R then every C is A
B) Always, if D is R then some C is A
C) Always, if D is R then some B is A
D) Always, if D is R then no C is A

Key Takeaway
The first four conjunctions show that a universal affirmative categorical combined with a universal affirmative conditional always yields a universal affirmative conditional conclusion with a particular consequent derived by conversion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Syllogism

The Remaining Seven Productive Conjunctions: Negative and Particular Premises
Enumerates conjunctions 5-11 with negative and particular conditionals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive conclusions from conjunctions involving negative conditionals or particular premises using conversion and obversion.

Core Idea
Negative conditionals require converting the whole conditional to affirmative before applying the categorical conversion.

The fifth through eleventh conjunctions introduce variety: the conditional can be negative (universal) or particular, and its consequent can be any combination. The key methods are converting the conditional from negative to affirmative, or using the categorical conversion directly. This lesson shows how to handle these cases systematically.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of conditional
Determine if the conditional is universal affirmative (as in first four) or universal negative (conjunctions 5–8) or particular affirmative (9–11). The categorical remains universal affirmative in all these.
2
If the conditional is negative, convert it to affirmative
The author explains that for negative conditionals (e.g., 'Never, if D is R then ...'), we first convert the whole conditional into an affirmative one by negating the consequent. This reduces it to one of the earlier patterns.
For example, 'Never, if D is R then every B is A' becomes 'Always, if D is R then not every B is A'.
3
Apply the categorical conversion
After converting the conditional (if needed), apply the same conversion on the categorical premise as before: 'Every B is C' becomes 'Some C is B'. Then substitute into the (possibly modified) consequent.
4
Form the conclusion
The conclusion maintains the antecedent of the original conditional. The consequent is the result of substitution. For negative conditionals, the conclusion is also negative (universal).
The author's 'byan' (explanation) for conjunctions 5-8 involves converting the conditional to affirmative and then following the pattern of the third conjunction.
5
Handle particular conditionals (9–11)
When the conditional is particular ('Sometimes, if D is R ...'), the conclusion becomes particular as well. The categorical conversion still applies, but the quantifier changes.
The ninth conjunction, for example, yields a particular affirmative conditional conclusion.

Commentary
This passage is a systematic enumeration of productive conjunctions (iqtiranat) for conditional syllogisms (qiyasat iqtiraniyya), where the major premise is a conditional (shartiyya) and the minor is a categorical (hamliyya). The author begins by stating that if the conditional is negative and the categorical is particular, the consequent must be particular, and there are twenty-four such conjunctions, but only the first eleven are detailed here. Each conjunction is defined by the quantity (universal/particular) and quality (affirmative/negative) of both the categorical and the conditional's consequent, and the resulting conclusion. For example, the first conjunction takes a universal affirmative categorical ('Every B is C') and a universal affirmative conditional with a universal affirmative consequent ('Always, if D is R then every B is A') and yields a universal affirmative conditional conclusion ('Always, if D is R then some C is A'). The author explains that this is done by conversion ('aks) of the categorical premise. Subsequent conjunctions follow similar patterns but may involve conversion of the conditional's consequent or conversion of the conditional itself (e.g., converting a negative conditional into an affirmative one). The fifth through eighth conjunctions involve a universal negative conditional, and the ninth through eleventh involve particular conditional premises. This enumeration is crucial for understanding how to derive valid conclusions in conjunctive syllogisms, a core part of Aristotelian logic as adopted in Islamic logic. In practice, such syllogisms are used in kalam arguments, e.g., 'If the world is originated, it requires a creator; the world is originated; therefore it requires a creator.'

Reducing Universal Negative Conditionals with a Universal Categorical
Author demonstrates the reduction of mixed universal-negative conditionals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reduce a mixed syllogism with a universal negative conditional to a known categorical mood by converting the conditional to affirmative.

Core Idea
A negative conditional converts to an affirmative one to unlock a known syllogism.

In the previous lessons, you learned about pure categorical and pure conditional syllogisms. Now we encounter mixed syllogisms—where one premise is categorical and the other is conditional. The author begins with the seventh and eighth conjunctions, where the categorical is universal affirmative and the conditional is universal negative. To prove these moods, he converts the negative conditional into an affirmative one, which reduces the mixed syllogism to a previously proven categorical mood.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises
The categorical premise is 'Every B is C' (universal affirmative). The conditional premise is a universal negative conditional: either 'It is never the case that if D is R then nothing of B is A' (seventh) or 'It is never the case that if D is R then every B is A' (eighth).
In the seventh, the consequent is universal negative; in the eighth, universal affirmative.
2
Convert the negative conditional to affirmative
Use the rule: 'not (if P then Q)' is equivalent to 'if P then not Q' for conditionals. Convert the conditional to an affirmative one. For the seventh: 'never if D is R then nothing of B is A' becomes 'always if D is R then some B is A'. For the eighth: 'never if D is R then every B is A' becomes 'always if D is R then some B is not A'? Actually, careful: original 'not (if D is R then every B is A)' converts to 'if D is R then not every B is A' i.e., 'some B is not A'. But the author says it reverts to the third conjunction, which uses a particular affirmative? Let's follow the text: The author says the proof for the seventh is by converting the conditional to affirmative, and it reverts to the third conjunction. The third conjunction from earlier (not shown) likely is 'from a universal affirmative categorical and a universal affirmative conditional with a particular consequent'? We'll trust the source. The key step is conversion.
The exact conversion rule for conditionals is: '~(if P then Q)' equals 'if P then ~Q'.
3
Apply the known categorical syllogism
After conversion, the conditional becomes affirmative. Now the premises are an affirmative conditional and the categorical. This is a known mood (the third conjunction), whose conclusion is already established. The conclusion of the known mood will be an affirmative conditional. Then, to get the original conclusion, you must negate back if needed? Actually, the conclusion of the seventh is a negative conditional, but the proof shows that after conversion the affirmative conclusion is derived, and by negating it you get the required negative. The text says: 'then it reverts to the third conjunction, whose productivity is known.' So the final conclusion is derived by negating the affirmative conclusion.
For the eighth, the proof is by converting the conditional to affirmative as well.
4
Formulate the final conclusion
The conclusion of the original mood is a universal negative conditional with the same antecedent and a consequent that is the result of substituting C for B in the original consequent, using the categorical. For the seventh: 'never if D is R then nothing of C is A'. For the eighth: 'never if D is R then every C is A'.
This matches the pattern: the consequent of the conclusion is derived by applying the categorical syllogism to the consequent of the conditional.

The seventh conjunction: from a universal affirmative categorical and a universal negative conditional whose consequent is a universal negative, such as our saying: 'Every B is C' and 'It is never the case that if D is R then nothing of B is A'. Its conclusion is a universal negative conditional, whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional and whose consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, which is 'something of C is A'. Its proof is by converting the conditional to affirmative, for then it reverts to the third conjunction, whose productivity is known. The eighth conjunction: from a universal affirmative categorical and a universal negative conditional whose consequent is a universal affirmative, such as our saying: 'Every B is C' and 'It is never the case that if D is R then every B is A'. Its conclusion is a universal negative conditional, whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional and whose consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, which is 'It is never the case that if D is R then every C is A'. Its proof is by converting the conditional to affirmative. The ninth conjunction: from a universal affirmative categorical and a particular affirmative conditional whose consequent is a universal affirmative, such as our saying: 'Every B is C' and 'Sometimes when D is R, every B is A'. Its conclusion is a particular affirmative conditional, whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional and whose consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, which is 'Sometimes when D is R, some C is A'. Its proof is like the proof of the first conjunction. The tenth conjunction: from a universal affirmative categorical and a particular affirmative conditional whose consequent is a universal negative, such as our saying: 'Every B is C' and 'Sometimes when D is R, nothing of B is A'. Its conclusion is a particular affirmative conditional, whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional and whose consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, which is 'Sometimes when D is R, some C is not A'. Its proof is like the proof of the second conjunction. The eleventh conjunction: from a universal affirmative categorical and a particular affirmative conditional whose consequent is a particular affirmative, such as our saying: 'Every B is C' and 'Sometimes when D is R, some B is A'. Its conclusion is a particular affirmative conditional, whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional and whose consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, which is 'Sometimes when D is R, some C is A'. Its proof is like the proof of the first conjunction. The twelfth conjunction: from a universal affirmative categorical and a particular affirmative conditional whose consequent is a particular negative, such as our saying: 'Every B is C' and 'Sometimes when D is R, some B is not A'. Its conclusion is a particular affirmative conditional, whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional and whose consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, which is 'Sometimes when D is R, some C is not A'. Its proof is like the proof of the second conjunction. The thirteenth conjunction: from a universal affirmative categorical and a particular negative conditional whose consequent is a particular negative, such as our saying: 'Every B is C' and 'Not every time when D is R is it the case that not every B is A'. Its conclusion is a particular negative conditional, whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional and whose consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, which is 'Not every time when D is R is it the case that nothing of C is A'. The fourteenth conjunction: from a universal affirmative categorical and a particular negative conditional whose consequent is a particular affirmative, such as our saying: 'Every B is C' and 'Not every time when D is R is it the case that some B is A'. Its conclusion is a particular negative conditional, whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional and whose consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, which is 'Not every time when D is R is it the case that every C is A'. The fifteenth conjunction: from a universal affirmative categorical and a particular negative conditional whose consequent is a universal negative, such as our saying: 'Every B is C' and 'Not every time when D is R is it the case that nothing of B is A'. Its conclusion is a particular negative conditional, whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional and whose consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, which is 'Not every time when D is R is it the case that nothing of C is A'. The sixteenth conjunction: from a universal affirmative categorical and a particular negative conditional whose consequent is a universal affirmative, such as our saying: 'Every B is C' and 'Not every time when D is R is it the case that every B is A'. Its conclusion is a negative conditional, whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional and whose consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, which is 'Not every time when D is R is it the case that every C is A'. The proof for these conjunctions is by converting the negative conditional to affirmative. The seventeenth conjunction: from a particular affirmative categorical and a universal affirmative conditional whose consequent is a universal affirmative, such as our saying: 'Some B is C' and 'Always, every time when D is R, every B is A'. Its conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional, whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional and whose consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical.
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage classifies the various 'conjunction' (iqtiran) moods that combine a categorical premise with a conditional premise. The author systematically enumerates moods seven through seventeen, each time specifying the quantity and quality of both premises and the resulting conclusion. The pattern is that the categorical premise is universal affirmative ('Every B is C'), while the conditional varies in universality, particularity, affirmation, and negation, and the consequent of the conditional also varies. The conclusion is always a conditional whose antecedent is the antecedent of the original conditional and whose consequent is derived by substituting the categorical's predicate (C) for the subject (B) in the consequent of the conditional, following the rules of categorical syllogism. The author indicates the proof: for moods with negative conditionals, one converts the conditional to affirmative, which reduces the mood to a previously proven one (the third, first, or second conjunction). For example, in the seventh mood, the conditional is 'never if D is R then nothing of B is A'; converting it to affirmative yields 'always if D is R then some B is A', which together with 'Every B is C' gives the third mood, whose conclusion is 'always if D is R then some C is A'. This conclusion, when negated back, gives the required 'never if D is R then nothing of C is A'. Similarly, the seventeenth mood starts with a particular categorical 'Some B is C' and a universal conditional. The result is universal, which is interesting—it shows that a particular categorical combined with a universal conditional can yield a universal conclusion. The author does not elaborate the proof here but implies it follows the earlier pattern. This section is crucial for understanding how mixed syllogisms work in Islamic logic, especially in the context of complex arguments in kalam and fiqh where conditional and categorical statements combine.

Checkpoint
In the seventh conjunction, after converting the negative conditional to affirmative, to which previously proven mood does the syllogism revert?
A) The first conjunction
B) The second conjunction
C) The third conjunction
D) The fourth conjunction

Key Takeaway
A universal negative conditional can be converted to an affirmative one, reducing the mixed syllogism to a known categorical mood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the premises: 'Every believer is truthful' (Every B is C) and 'It is never the case that if someone is a liar then every believer is truthful' (i.e., ~(if D then every B is A)). Use the conversion method to derive the conclusion in the form 'It is never the case that if someone is a liar then ... (conclusion about C)'.

The Syllogism
advanced
10 min
Particular Affirmative Conditionals with a Universal Categorical
Author enumerates four moods with particular affirmative conditionals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the pattern for mixed syllogisms with a particular affirmative conditional and derive the conclusion.

Core Idea
A particular conditional combines with a universal categorical to yield a particular conclusion.

After handling universal negative conditionals, the author turns to particular affirmative conditionals (moods 9-12). In each, the categorical is still 'Every B is C', but the conditional is particular affirmative ('Sometimes when D is R...'). The consequent of the conditional varies: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, or particular negative. The conclusions are all particular conditionals. The proof is said to be similar to the first or second conjunctions, which are earlier established moods.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises
The categorical is 'Every B is C'. The conditional is particular affirmative: 'Sometimes when D is R, (consequent)'. The consequent can be: 'every B is A' (9th), 'no B is A' (10th), 'some B is A' (11th), or 'some B is not A' (12th).
The ninth and eleventh have affirmative consequents; tenth and twelfth have negative consequents.
2
Notice the pattern for the conclusion
The conclusion is a particular conditional with the same antecedent 'Sometimes when D is R', but the consequent is derived by substituting 'C' for 'B' in the original consequent, and adjusting quantity and quality according to categorical syllogism rules. For affirmative consequents (9th and 11th), the conclusion has a particular affirmative consequent: 'some C is A' (9th) or 'some C is A' (11th). For negative consequents (10th and 12th), the conclusion has a particular negative consequent: 'some C is not A' (10th) or 'some C is not A' (12th).
The eighth conjunction had a universal negative conditional and gave a universal conclusion; these give particular conclusions because the conditional is particular.
3
Relate to the proof method
The author states the proof is 'like the proof of the first conjunction' for the 9th and 11th, and 'like the proof of the second conjunction' for the 10th and 12th. The first and second conjunctions are presumably moods with particular affirmative conditionals already proven. So these are essentially the same pattern, just varying the consequent.
The proof likely involves converting the categorical and conditional into a categorical syllogism, but the author does not elaborate.
4
Construct the conclusion for any given particular affirmative conditional
Given 'Every B is C' and 'Sometimes when D is R, (consequent about B)', the conclusion is 'Sometimes when D is R, (consequent about C)'. To get the consequent about C, take the original consequent about B, replace B with C, and apply the rules of categorical syllogism: if the consequent is universal affirmative ('every B is A'), then from 'Every B is C' and 'Every B is A' we cannot directly infer 'Every C is A' (that would be a fallacy). But the conclusion given is 'some C is A'. So the proof must use conversion or other techniques. The pattern is consistent: the consequent in the conclusion is always a particular version of the original consequent with B replaced by C.
For example, if the original consequent is 'no B is A' (universal negative), the conclusion becomes 'some C is not A' (particular negative). This matches the rule that from 'Every B is C' and 'No B is A', we deduce 'Some C is not A' via the categorical syllogism Cesare or Festino.

الاقتران السابع عشر من جزئية موجبة حَمْلية وكليّة موجبة شرطية تاليها كلي موجب، كقولنا: (بعض "ب" "ج") و(دائماً كل ما كان "د" ر" فكل "ب" "أ") ونتيجته كليّة موجبة متصلة، مقدمها مقدم الشرطية، وتاليها نتيجة اقتران التالي بالحَمْلية.
The seventh conjunction: from a universal affirmative categorical and a universal negative conditional whose consequent is a universal negative, such as our saying: 'Every B is C' and 'It is never the case that if D is R then nothing of B is A'. Its conclusion is a universal negative conditional, whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional and whose consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, which is 'something of C is A'. Its proof is by converting the conditional to affirmative, for then it reverts to the third conjunction, whose productivity is known. The eighth conjunction: from a universal affirmative categorical and a universal negative conditional whose consequent is a universal affirmative, such as our saying: 'Every B is C' and 'It is never the case that if D is R then every B is A'. Its conclusion is a universal negative conditional, whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional and whose consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, which is 'It is never the case that if D is R then every C is A'. Its proof is by converting the conditional to affirmative. The ninth conjunction: from a universal affirmative categorical and a particular affirmative conditional whose consequent is a universal affirmative, such as our saying: 'Every B is C' and 'Sometimes when D is R, every B is A'. Its conclusion is a particular affirmative conditional, whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional and whose consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, which is 'Sometimes when D is R, some C is A'. Its proof is like the proof of the first conjunction. The tenth conjunction: from a universal affirmative categorical and a particular affirmative conditional whose consequent is a universal negative, such as our saying: 'Every B is C' and 'Sometimes when D is R, nothing of B is A'. Its conclusion is a particular affirmative conditional, whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional and whose consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, which is 'Sometimes when D is R, some C is not A'. Its proof is like the proof of the second conjunction. The eleventh conjunction: from a universal affirmative categorical and a particular affirmative conditional whose consequent is a particular affirmative, such as our saying: 'Every B is C' and 'Sometimes when D is R, some B is A'. Its conclusion is a particular affirmative conditional, whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional and whose consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, which is 'Sometimes when D is R, some C is A'. Its proof is like the proof of the first conjunction. The twelfth conjunction: from a universal affirmative categorical and a particular affirmative conditional whose consequent is a particular negative, such as our saying: 'Every B is C' and 'Sometimes when D is R, some B is not A'. Its conclusion is a particular affirmative conditional, whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional and whose consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, which is 'Sometimes when D is R, some C is not A'. Its proof is like the proof of the second conjunction. The thirteenth conjunction: from a universal affirmative categorical and a particular negative conditional whose consequent is a particular negative, such as our saying: 'Every B is C' and 'Not every time when D is R is it the case that not every B is A'. Its conclusion is a particular negative conditional, whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional and whose consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, which is 'Not every time when D is R is it the case that nothing of C is A'. The fourteenth conjunction: from a universal affirmative categorical and a particular negative conditional whose consequent is a particular affirmative, such as our saying: 'Every B is C' and 'Not every time when D is R is it the case that some B is A'. Its conclusion is a particular negative conditional, whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional and whose consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, which is 'Not every time when D is R is it the case that every C is A'. The fifteenth conjunction: from a universal affirmative categorical and a particular negative conditional whose consequent is a universal negative, such as our saying: 'Every B is C' and 'Not every time when D is R is it the case that nothing of B is A'. Its conclusion is a particular negative conditional, whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional and whose consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, which is 'Not every time when D is R is it the case that nothing of C is A'. The sixteenth conjunction: from a universal affirmative categorical and a particular negative conditional whose consequent is a universal affirmative, such as our saying: 'Every B is C' and 'Not every time when D is R is it the case that every B is A'. Its conclusion is a negative conditional, whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional and whose consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, which is 'Not every time when D is R is it the case that every C is A'. The proof for these conjunctions is by converting the negative conditional to affirmative. The seventeenth conjunction: from a particular affirmative categorical and a universal affirmative conditional whose consequent is a universal affirmative, such as our saying: 'Some B is C' and 'Always, every time when D is R, every B is A'. Its conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional, whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional and whose consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical.
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage classifies the various 'conjunction' (iqtiran) moods that combine a categorical premise with a conditional premise. The author systematically enumerates moods seven through seventeen, each time specifying the quantity and quality of both premises and the resulting conclusion. The pattern is that the categorical premise is universal affirmative ('Every B is C'), while the conditional varies in universality, particularity, affirmation, and negation, and the consequent of the conditional also varies. The conclusion is always a conditional whose antecedent is the antecedent of the original conditional and whose consequent is derived by substituting the categorical's predicate (C) for the subject (B) in the consequent of the conditional, following the rules of categorical syllogism. The author indicates the proof: for moods with negative conditionals, one converts the conditional to affirmative, which reduces the mood to a previously proven one (the third, first, or second conjunction). For example, in the seventh mood, the conditional is 'never if D is R then nothing of B is A'; converting it to affirmative yields 'always if D is R then some B is A', which together with 'Every B is C' gives the third mood, whose conclusion is 'always if D is R then some C is A'. This conclusion, when negated back, gives the required 'never if D is R then nothing of C is A'. Similarly, the seventeenth mood starts with a particular categorical 'Some B is C' and a universal conditional. The result is universal, which is interesting—it shows that a particular categorical combined with a universal conditional can yield a universal conclusion. The author does not elaborate the proof here but implies it follows the earlier pattern. This section is crucial for understanding how mixed syllogisms work in Islamic logic, especially in the context of complex arguments in kalam and fiqh where conditional and categorical statements combine.

The Syllogism

Particular Negative Conditionals and a Special Case
Author concludes with particular negative conditionals and a surprising universal conclusion from a particular categorical

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive conclusions from mixed syllogisms with particular negative conditionals and handle the case where the categorical is particular.

Core Idea
Even negative conditionals can yield conclusions when combined with categoricals, and a particular categorical can still give a universal conclusion.

The final moods (13th-16th) involve particular negative conditionals, where the conditional says 'Not every time when D is R...'. Then comes a surprising case: the 17th mood uses a particular categorical ('Some B is C') with a universal conditional, resulting in a universal conclusion. This shows that mixing quantities can have unexpected effects.

Guided Path
1
Understand the particular negative conditional
A particular negative conditional has the form 'Not every time when D is R, then Q'. This is equivalent to 'Sometimes when D is R, not Q'. The consequent Q varies: for 13th, Q is 'not every B is A' (i.e., some B is not A); for 14th, Q is 'some B is A'; for 15th, Q is 'no B is A'; for 16th, Q is 'every B is A'. The conclusions are all particular negative conditionals with consequent about C.
The 13th and 15th have consequents that are already negative in sense; the 14th and 16th have affirmative consequents. The conclusion is always particular negative.
2
Derive the conclusion for each
The pattern: replace B with C in the consequent. For the 13th, original consequent 'not every B is A' (i.e., some B is not A) becomes 'not nothing of C is A' i.e., 'some C is A'? Wait, the text says the conclusion's consequent is 'not nothing of C is A' which is equivalent to 'some C is A' but written negatively. Actually, the author writes: 'ليس كل ما كان د ر فلا شيء من ج أ' which means 'Not every time when D is R is it the case that nothing of C is A' i.e., 'Sometimes when D is R, some C is A'. So the consequent is particular affirmative in meaning. For the 14th, original consequent 'some B is A' becomes 'every C is A' (universal affirmative) in the conclusion? The text says: 'ليس كل ما كان د ر فكل ج أ' i.e., 'Not every time when D is R is it the case that every C is A' (so particular negative in form, meaning 'Sometimes when D is R, not every C is A' i.e., some C is not A). This is confusing. Let's re-read carefully. Actually, the author's Arabic for 14th: 'وليها نتيجة اقتران التالي بالحملية وهي ليس كل ما كان د ر فكل ج أ' – so the conclusion is 'not every time when D is R, then every C is A', which is a particular negative conditional (since 'not every' is particular negative). And the original consequent was 'some B is A' (particular affirmative). This is tricky. The pattern is not straightforward substitution; it depends on the logical equivalence rules. It's best to memorize the given results.
The proof is by converting the negative conditional to affirmative, similar to earlier moods.
3
Analyze the 17th mood
Here the categorical is particular affirmative: 'Some B is C'. The conditional is universal affirmative: 'Always, every time when D is R, every B is A'. The conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional: 'Always, every time when D is R, every C is A'. This is surprising because a particular categorical seems to support a universal conclusion. But note that the conditional's antecedent is universal ('every time'), and the categorical says only some B are C. However, the conclusion 'every C is A' follows? Let's reason: From 'Some B is C' and 'Every B is A', we cannot conclude 'Every C is A' (that would be a fallacy). But here the conditional says 'always, if D then every B is A', and we have 'Some B is C'. This doesn't give 'every C is A' directly. The author must be using a different proof. This mood shows that the rules are more complex.
The author does not provide proof for the 17th, implying it follows from earlier principles.

Mixed Syllogisms: Combining Categorical and Conditional Premises
Enumerates five moods of mixed syllogisms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive the conclusion of any mixed syllogism that pairs a universal affirmative categorical premise with a particular conditional premise, following the pattern of the twelfth through sixteenth conjunctions.

Core Idea
The categorical premise modifies the conditional's consequent while preserving modality and quantity.

This passage systematically presents five moods where a fixed universal affirmative categorical 'Every B is J' is combined with various particular conditionals. Mastering these patterns enables you to construct valid mixed syllogisms, which are common in juristic reasoning where a categorical rule interacts with conditional scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the categorical premise
In each conjunction, the categorical premise is always universal affirmative: 'Every B is J'. Recognize that B is a subset of J.
2
Analyze the conditional premise
Note the quantity (particular) and quality (affirmative or negative) of the conditional, and the quantity/quality of its consequent. For example, in the 12th, the conditional is particular affirmative with a particular negative consequent.
3
Combine the consequent with the categorical
Replace the subject of the conditional's consequent (B) with J, using the subordination relation 'Every B is J'. Preserve the quantity and quality of the original consequent exactly.
For example, 'some B is not A' becomes 'some J is not A'; 'every B is A' becomes 'every J is A', etc.
4
Formulate the conclusion
The conclusion is a conditional with the same antecedent as the conditional premise, and the new consequent from step 3. Ensure the quantity matches that of the conditional premise (particular in these cases).
5
Verify validity using conversion of negative conditionals
The author notes that these conjunctions are explained by converting negative conditionals to affirmative ones. Check that the resulting conditional follows logically from the premises.

The twelfth conjunction: from a universal affirmative categorical and a particular affirmative conditional whose consequent is particular negative, as in our saying: 'Every B is J' and 'It may be that when D is R, then some B is not A.' The thirteenth conjunction: from a universal affirmative categorical and a particular negative conditional whose consequent is particular negative, as in our saying: 'Every B is J' and 'Not every C [sic] that is D is R, so not every B is A' – and its conclusion is a particular negative conditional, whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and whose consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, namely 'Not every C that is D is R, so no J is A.' The fourteenth conjunction: from a universal affirmative categorical and a particular negative conditional whose consequent is particular affirmative, as in our saying: 'Every B is J' and 'Not every [case] where D is R, some B is A' – and its conclusion is a particular negative conditional, whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and whose consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, namely 'Not every [case] where D is R, so every J is A.' The fifteenth conjunction: from a universal affirmative categorical and a particular negative conditional whose consequent is universal negative, as in our saying: 'Every B is J' and 'Not every [case] where D is R, so no B is A' – and its conclusion is a particular negative conditional, whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and whose consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, namely 'Not every [case] where D is R, so no J is A.' The explanation for these conjunctions is by converting the negative conditional into an affirmative one.

Commentary
This passage describes five specific moods (conjunctions) of mixed syllogisms that combine a universal affirmative categorical premise with a conditional premise of varying quantity and quality. The author systematically lists each conjunction with an example in Arabic logical variables (B, J, D, R, A) and states the resulting conclusion. The categorical premise is always 'Every B is J' (universal affirmative). The conditional premise varies: for the 12th, it is a particular affirmative conditional with a particular negative consequent; for the 13th–16th, it is a particular negative conditional with consequents that are particular negative, particular affirmative, universal negative, and universal affirmative respectively. The conclusions are always conditional, sharing the same antecedent as the conditional premise, and the consequent is derived by combining the categorical premise with the original conditional's consequent. The author ends by noting that these conjunctions are explained by converting the negative conditional into an affirmative one, a standard technique in logic. For example, in the 12th conjunction, from 'Every B is J' and 'It may be that when D is R, some B is not A', we infer 'It may be that when D is R, some J is not A' (since B is a subset of J). In fiqh terms, one might say: Every intoxicant (B) is forbidden (J); it may be that if a substance (D) is fermented (R), then some intoxicants are not wine (A). Therefore, it may be that if a substance is fermented, then some forbidden things are not wine.

The Syllogism
