The Six Mixed Conjunctions with a Particular Affirmative Categorical
Author enumerates six moods combining a particular affirmative categorical with a universal or particular conditional.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the conclusion of any mixed syllogism with a particular affirmative categorical premise and a conditional premise, identifying the necessary operation (conversion of categorical or conditional).

Core Idea
A particular categorical can combine with a universal conditional to yield a universal conditional conclusion, thanks to conversion.

In mixed syllogisms, the quantity and quality of the premises determine the operations needed to derive the conclusion. This lesson walks you through six specific moods where one premise is a particular affirmative categorical (Some B is J) and the other is a conditional. By mastering the pattern, you will be able to handle any such combination in logical reasoning within Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the categorical premise
Note that the categorical is always particular affirmative: 'Some B is J'. Recognize the variables (ب = B, ج = J).
This premise will be converted if needed.
2
Identify the conditional premise
Determine its quantifier (universal or particular) and its quality (affirmative or negative). Also note the consequent's quantity and quality (universal/particular, affirmative/negative).
The conditional has antecedent 'D is R' and consequent involving B and A.
3
Apply the appropriate operation
When the consequent is universal, convert the categorical (aks al-ḥamliyya: 'Some B is J' becomes 'Some J is B'). When the conditional is universal negative, convert the conditional into an affirmative by a logical transformation (qalb al-muttaṣila ilā al-ījāb).
The operation changes the form while preserving truth.
4
Construct the conclusion
Combine the converted premise with the original conditional to produce the final conditional conclusion. The antecedent stays the same; the consequent is derived by linking the converted categorical with the original consequent.
The conclusion is always a conditional (universal or particular) with the same antecedent as the original conditional.

The seventeenth conjunction: from a particular affirmative categorical and a universal affirmative conditional whose consequent is universal affirmative, as in our saying: 'Some B is J' and 'Always, whenever D is R, every B is A' – and its conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional, whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and whose consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, namely 'Always, whenever D is R, some J is A.' Its explanation is by conversion of the categorical. The eighteenth conjunction: from a particular affirmative categorical and a universal affirmative conditional whose consequent is universal negative, as in our saying: 'Some B is J' and 'Always, whenever D is R, no B is A' – and its conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional, whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and whose consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, namely 'Always, whenever D is R, not every J is A.' Its explanation is like the explanation of the second conjunction. The nineteenth conjunction: from a particular affirmative categorical and a universal negative conditional whose consequent is particular negative, as in our saying: 'Some B is J' and 'Never, whenever D is R, is it that not every B is A' – and its conclusion is a universal negative conditional, whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and whose consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, namely 'Never, whenever D is R, is it that no J is A.' Its explanation is by converting the conditional into an affirmative and taking the necessary consequence of the conclusion. The twentieth conjunction: from a particular affirmative categorical and a universal negative conditional whose consequent is particular affirmative, as in our saying: 'Some B is J' and 'Never, if D is R, some B is A' – and its conclusion is a universal negative conditional, whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and whose consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, namely 'Never, if D is R, every J is A.' Its explanation is also by converting the conditional into an affirmative and taking the necessary consequence of the conclusion. Its explanation is by conversion of the categorical. The twenty-second conjunction: from a particular affirmative categorical and a particular affirmative conditional whose consequent is universal negative, as in our saying: 'Some B is J' and 'It may be that when D is R, no B is A' – and its conclusion is a particular affirmative conditional, whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and whose consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, namely 'It may be that when D is R, some J is not A.' Its explanation is by conversion of the consequent.

Commentary
This passage continues the enumeration of mixed syllogisms, now with a particular affirmative categorical premise (Some B is J) combined with various conditional premises: universal affirmative, universal negative, and particular affirmative. The conjunctions from 17th to 22nd are listed. Notably, the conclusions are often universal in quantity despite the particular categorical, because the author uses conversion (e.g., converting the categorical 'Some B is J' to 'Some J is B' – a simple conversion) to derive the result. For example, in the 17th conjunction: from 'Some B is J' and 'Always, when D is R, every B is A', we get 'Always, when D is R, some J is A' – because the categorical converts to 'Some J is B', and then linking with 'every B is A' yields 'some J is A'. Similarly, the 18th gives 'Always, when D is R, not every J is A' (i.e., some J is not A) from a universal negative consequent. The 19th and 20th involve universal negative conditionals and require a different technique: converting the conditional into an affirmative and taking the necessary consequence. The 21st and 22nd use particular affirmative conditionals and again involve conversion of the categorical or the consequent. In fiqh terms, one could think of an example: Some intoxicants (B) are forbidden (J); always, if a substance (D) is fermented (R), then every intoxicant is impure (A). Therefore, always, if a substance is fermented, then some forbidden things are impure.

The Eighteenth Conjunction: Particular Categorical + Universal Conditional
Author details the eighteenth conjunction of a mixed syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structure of the eighteenth conjunction and derive its conclusion using the combination of categorical and conditional premises.

Core Idea
A particular affirmative categorical combines with a universal conditional to yield a new conditional.

This passage introduces one of many valid conjunctions in mixed syllogisms. By understanding it, you will see how categorical and conditional premises interact in Islamic logic, a skill essential for evaluating arguments in kalam and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises
The first premise is a particular affirmative categorical: 'Some B is C'. The second premise is a universal affirmative conditional with a universal negative consequent: 'Whenever D is R, then not every B is A'.
2
Note the terms
The variables are B, C, D, R, A. The categorical premise relates B and C. The conditional relates D and R to a relation between B and A.
3
Derive the antecedent of the conclusion
The conclusion's antecedent is the same as the conditional premise's antecedent: 'Whenever D is R'.
4
Derive the consequent of the conclusion
Combine the categorical premise with the conditional premise's consequent using a categorical syllogism. From 'not every B is A' and 'some B is C', infer 'not every C is A'.
This step assumes a basic understanding of categorical syllogisms.
5
State the conclusion
The final conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional: 'Whenever D is R, then not every C is A'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاقتران الثامن عشر من جزئية موجبة حَمْلية وكليّة موجبة شرطية، تاليها كلي سالب كقولنا: (بعض "ب" "ج") و(دائماً كل ما كان "د" "ر" فليس كل "ب" "أ") ونتيجته كليّة موجبة متصلة، مقدمها مقدم الشرطية، وتاليها نتيجة اقتران التالي بالحَمْلية، وهي دائماً كل ما كان "د" "ر" فليس كل "ج" "أ") وبيانه كبيان الاقتران بالحَمْلية، وهي الثاني.
The eighteenth conjunction: from a particular affirmative categorical and a universal affirmative conditional whose consequent is universal negative, as in our saying: 'Some B is C' and 'Whenever D is R, then not every B is A.' Its conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional, its antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the result of combining the consequent with the categorical, namely: 'Whenever D is R, then not every C is A.' Its explanation is like the explanation of the conjunction with the categorical, and it is the second.
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This passage details a specific type of mixed syllogism combining a categorical (ḥamlī) premise and a conditional (sharṭī) premise. The first premise is a particular affirmative categorical: 'Some B is C'. The second premise is a universal affirmative conditional with a universal negative consequent: 'Whenever D is R, then not every B is A'. The conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional with the same antecedent as the conditional premise ('Whenever D is R') and a consequent derived by combining the categorical premise ('some B is C') with the consequent of the conditional (''not every B is A') through categorical syllogism. The resultant consequent is 'not every C is A', so the conclusion reads: 'Whenever D is R, then not every C is A'. The author notes that the explanation (bayān) is the same as for the conjunction with the categorical, referring to a previous example (the second conjunction).
